Of Australian Art and Literature, for the Belgian Relief Fund.
Australian Authors and Artists, at the invitation of Madame Melba, have combined to make a worthy
Gift Book, to assist the Belgian Relief Fund. There are eight plates in full color, twenty full-page
etchings, two-color, and black-and-white drawings, as well as numerous drawings and decorative designs,
in the text of 176 pages. Everyone should possess a copy as a memento of this Great War.
Price, 4/-; posted, 4/3.

The Red Page
Fiction
The cunning publishers have chosen the
title, The German Lieutenant, for the latest
collection of Strindberg’s short stories
(Laurie) with an appreciation of its
topical war-time value. It is only fair to
presumptive readers to warn them that the
story is one of the war of 1870, and that
the JHun depicted is so kind-hearted that
he cannot bear to watch an execution of
alleged French spies! He broods so in-
tensely upon this act that he suffers from
the obsession that he has committed a mur-
der, and becomes insane. The gentle Hun!
On his recovery he settles in Switzerland,
renounces his country, becomes \a Swiss,
thus imagining that he has for ever disso-
ciated himself from war. If by any chance
this German lieutenant had chosen Belgium
for his neutralised haven! As for the other
stories, if you are a Strindbergian you will
like them ; if not, you will find them some-
what childish and dull.
Jt
Bealby: A Holiday, the latest output
from the H. G. Weils factory, is pure joy.
This story of Bealby, the boy who from
hatred of becoming a servant ran away
from the butler and adventured upon the
highway, in the company of silly women
playing at caravaning, and the tramp—-
this story of the hunting of Bealby and his
capture, a chastened Bealby, reads like the
early Wells of his Love and Mr. Lewisham
days; in fact, a suspicion arises whether
Bealby (Methuen, per Dymock’s Library)
isn’t a discarded novel that Wells has fur-
bished up or disinterred from some forgot-
ten manuscript bf those ante-bellum days.
It is full of delightful characterisations,
ranging from a Lord Chancellor to a pinky-
faced and irrepressible army captain—Wells
could not really help making him deeply
interested in aviation- —and from the popular
actress to the tramp. The book needs no
further criticism than the statement that it
is a genuine Wells of the Wells’ pre-socialis-
tic and pre-sex days.
J*
A. Neil Lyons has never done more humor-
ous work than in his Kitchener Chaps
(Lane, Is.). These amusing studies of the
modern recruit are real —as real as Clara;
and if A. Neil Lyons cannot help seeing the
humorous side of even so massive an institu-
tion as the British Army, he can also show
the pathos beneath the light-hearted comedy.
This little book is the most humorous of all
the war publications, not even excluding
Hun Hedin’s heroic effort to picture the
Kaiser as a benevolent Deity. Take, for
instance, the views of Private Dodd, men-
tioned in despatches.” This honor, he ex-
plains, with a desperate modesty, is nothing.
“A kind of a custom, sir, that’s all. If
there’s any Distinguished Conduct Medals
to spare, one o’ them might come a fellow’s
way; but that ain’t likely, not with so many
sergeants about. They mostly gets the
medals. One of our sergeants got a D.C.M.
in the Bower War for sitting (which kindly
read as such) between the lines. No, sir,
there’s no particular cop about these men-
tions’ : only something for your pals to read.
The chaps in the regiment will know about
it, of course, and it’s nice to think they know
about it. A bit o’ swank. See? And if a
Distinguished Conduct Medal does come
your way, why that’s a bit of all right, be-
cause they give you a five-pound gratooty
along with it.” And this wounded soldier
was eager to get hack to the Front. Heroism ?
Hardly. “Soon me rib’sll grow together
once again, and then they’ll have me out o’
this and send me back to what they calls
‘light duty.’ Back to Aldershot or Salis-
bury Plain or Colchester. I don’t want none
o’ that. I’d rather go back to the Front
again—to dror me second ration. They ain t
so rigimental at the Front. They don't
mess a man about so much.. . .There’s more
peace for a man out there at the Front.”
Lord Dunsany, whose Five Plays display
so remarkable an imagination and so delight-
ful a sense of humor, evidences the same
rare characteristics in his Fifty-One Tales
(Elkin Mathews, per Sydney Book Club).
In these tiny tales, seldom more than a page
in length, Dunsany manages to compress
all time and space, together with a tremen-
dous allotment of chaos and a long lease-
hold of eternity. His art consists equally
of an exquisite arrangement and choice of
words, of a rigid selection and compression
of material, and of an eerie and grotesque
humor. “The Guest,” in which a young
man dines with Death, is a short story that
is flawless. “A Moral Little Tale” puts
into brief compass all that need be said about
the Wowser. This Wowser held that it
was wrong to dance. To him came the
Devil, hailing him as “Friend!” For the
Wowser had done the Devil’s work.
“ ‘Have you not put apart the couples that
would dance? Have you not checked their
laughter and their accursed mirth? O friend,
friend, you do not know what a detestable
thing it is to sit in Hell and hear people
being happy, and singing in theatres and
singing in the fields, and whispering after
dances under the moon?’
“ "Then,’ said the man, ‘I have mistaken
right for wrong; but as soon as I wake I
will fight you yet.’
“ ‘Oh. no you don’t,’ said the Devil. ‘You
don’t wake up out of this sleep.’
“And somewhere far away Hell’s black
steel doors were opened, and arm in arm
those two were drawn within, and the doors
shut behind them, and still they went arm
in arm, trudging further and further into
the deeps of Hell, and it was that Puritan’s
punishment to know that those he cared for
on Earth would do evil as he had done.”
Dunsany’s art is the most individual thing
that has yet come out of Irish literature.
Dunsany Tales
AFTER THE FIBE.
When that happened which had been so
long in happening and the world hit a blade,
uncharted star, certain tremendous crea-
tures out of some other world came peering
among the cinders to see if .there were any-
thing there that it were worth while to
remember. They spolce of the great things
that the world was known to have had;
they mentioned the mammoth. And pre-
sently they sate man’s temples, silent and
windowless, staring like empty skulls.
“Some great thing has been here,” one
said, “in these huge places.”
“It teas the mammoth,” said one.
“Something greater than he,” said
another.
And then they found that the greatest
thing in the world had been the dreams of
man.
The Demagogue and the Demi-monde.
A demagogue and a demi-mondaine
chanced to arrive together at the gate of
Paradise. And the Saint looked sorrowfully
at them both.
“Why were you a demagoguef” he said to
the first.
“Because,” said the demagogue, “I stood
for those principles that have made us what
we are and have endeared our Party to
the great heart of the people. In a word, I
stood unflinchingly on the plank of popular
representation.”
“And you?” said the Saint to her of the
demi-monde.
“I wanted money,” said the demi-mon-
daine.
And after some moments’ thought the
Saint saidr “Well, come in; though you
don’t deserve to.”
But to the demagogue he said: “We genu-
inely regret that the limited space at our
disposal and our unfortunate lack of inter-
est in those Questions that you have gone so
far to inculcate and have so ably upheld in
the past, prevent us from giving you the
support for which you seek.”
And he shut the golden door.
The Food of Death.
Death was sick. But they brought him
bread that the modern bakers make,
whitened with alum, and the tinned meats
of Chicago, with a pinch of our modern sub-
stitute for salt. They carried him into the
dining-room of a great hotel (in that close
atmosphere Death breathed more freely),
and there they gave him their cheap Indian
tea. They brought him a bottle of unne
that they called champagne. Death drank
it up. They bought a newspaper and
looked up the patent medicines; they gave
him the foods that it recommended for
invalids, and a little medicine as prescribed
in the paper. They gave him some milk and
borax, such as children drink in England.
Death arose ravening, strong, and strode
again through the cities.
The Raft-builders.
All we who ivrite put me in mind of sailors
hastily making rafts upon doomed ships.
When we break up under the heavy years
and go down into Eternity with all that is
ours, our thoughts, like small rafts, float on
aiohile upon Oblivion’s sea. They will not
carry much over those tides, our names and
a phrase or two, and little else ;
See now Oblivion shimmering all around
us, its very tranquillity deadlier than tem-
pest. Hoto little all our keels have troubled
it. Time in its deeps swims like a mon-
strous whale; and, like a whale, feeds on the
littlest things—small tunes and little un-
skilled songs of the olden, golden evenings—
and anon turneth whale-like to overthrow
whole ships.
See now the wreckage of Babylon floating
idly, and something there that once was
Nineveh; already their kings and queens are
in the deeps among the iveedy masses of old
centuries that hide the sodden hulk of sunken
Tyre, and make a darkness round Persepolis.
For the rest, I dimly see the forms of foun-
dered ships on a sea-floor strewn with
crowns.
Our ships were all unseaworthy from the
There goes the raft that Homer made for
Helen.
From “Fifty-One Tales,” by Lord Dun-

The suggestion that possibly a corres-
pondent might be able to identify the old
Frenchman, without hands, who figured in
Conrad’s gruesome story, published in Within
the Tides, brought forth J.H.R., who (R.P.
17/6/T5) claimed to have found the bloke in
an inventor of a water bicycle that was once
seen about Sydney Harbor. L.L.W. now
states that the old Frenchman’s name was
Pierre Marc de Sers, that he kept for some
years the Bayswater Hotel at Rushcutter’s
Bay, and that he had numerous daughters
and one son, by various wives. L.L.W. was
the. person employed to paddle the ill-fated
water-bicycle. Pierre’s hair was not black,
but a very ordinary brown. His English was
very fauity, but though L.L.W. lived with
him some months he never heard any of the
variety of languages with which J.H.R.
credits him. Still even a Frenchman might
be silent in 17 languages for a year. Pierre,
according to his latest discoverer, lost his left
arm while in the French navy. He smoked a
pipe more often than he smoked cigarettes.
L.L.W. does not think that Pierre is Con-
rad’s Frenchman at all. We shall try to get
Conrad’s opinion on this quite unimportant
matter.
Galsworthy
John Galsworthy has descended to doing
dainty little things delicately. The Little
Man 'and Other Satires (iMnemiann) is a
collection of little plays, little stories and
little essays. The title play is a delightful
satire upon—well, what? Anyhow, it is a
delightful little satire. “The Voice of • •”
shows. Galsworthy deliberately forcing his
accent in the effort to achieve sensational-
ism. A voice from the “gods” in a music-
hall sternly orders a dancer off the stage:
“Woman! Blasphemous creature! You have
profaned Beauty! Go!” And immediately
things begin to happen. The owner of the
voice cannot be discovered. “Limes,” ordered
to cut off his light, replies that he has done
so —and still a brilliant ray showers down
upon the stage! The audience thinks it is a
fire, and disappears. The manager orders
every light to be cut off, and every light goes
out except the “golden pathway still flowing
down the darkness.” Gradually the sus-
picion grows that the voice did not come
from the gallery, but from higher up. Then
the golden ray disappears.
“The boss spun round. ‘Electrics! You
blazing ass. Ten to one but. you’ve cut my
connection, turning up the lights like that.
The voice of Great snakes! What a
turn! I’d have given it a thou’ a week!
Hullo ! up there ! Hullo!’
“But there came no answer from under
the gilt-starred dome.”
Infinitely finer work is a series of
“Studies of Extravagance,” where Gals-
worthy’s subtle irony flickers round such
types as the writer, the critic,-the plain man,
the artist, the housewife, and other dealers
in superlatives. Each is a clever little cameo
of snobbery. The housewife shows us the
difficulties in the way of a conventional
mother trying to bring up her daughters in
the way Mrs. Grundy would have them go.
“It was her principle to rule them with
good, motherly sense* as became a woman in
whose hands the family life of her country
centred; and it was satisfactory, on the.
whole, to find that they obeyed her whenever
they wished to. On this occasion, however,
she spoke to them severely: ‘The place of
woman,’ she said, ‘is in the home.’
“ ‘The whole home —and nothing but the
home.’
“‘Ella! The place of woman is by the
side of man; counselling, supporting, ruling,
but never competing with him. The place
of woman is in the shop, the kitchen,
and’
“ ‘The —bed !’
“ ‘Ella!’
“ ‘ln the soup !’
“ ‘ Beatrice ! I wish —I do wish you girls
would be more respectful. The place of
woman is in the home. Yes, I’ve said that
before, but I shall say it again, and don’t
you forget it! The place of woman is—the
most important thing in national life. If
you want to realise that, just think of your
own mother ; and’
“ ‘Our own father.'
“ ‘Ella! The place of woman is in
the’ She left the room, feeling that, for
the moment, she had said enough.”
The housewife, too, has trouble with her
servants. “The ‘day out’ was, perhaps, the
most fruitful source of disillusionment —girls
of that class, no matter how much they pro-
tested their innocence, seemed utterly unable
to keep away from man’s society. It was
only once a fortnight that she required
them to exercise their control and self-
respect in that regard, for on the other 13
days she took care that they had no chance,
suffering nd male footstep in her basement.
And yet—would you believe it?—on those
fourteenth days she was never able to be
easy in her mind... .It was as if these girls
were always rudely saying to themselves:
‘What do you know of us? We wish you’d
leave us alone!’ The idea! As if she could,
or would !... .No, no! If the well-to-do did
not look after and control the poor, no one
would, which was just what they would like.
“A religious woman, she rarely missed the
morning, and seldom went to evening, ser-
vice, feeling that in daylight she could best
set an example to her neighbors.... She
made a point of never drawing Frederick’s
attention to female beauty; not that she felt
she had any real reason to be alarmed, for
she was a fine woman: but because men
were so funny... .It was often said that she
was a vanishing type, but she knew better.
Pedantic fools murmured that Ibsen had
destroyed her, but she had not yet heard of
him.”
But it is perhaps unfair to quote the per-
fect housewife, because Galsworthy is just as
delightfully ironical about the perfect men
that he pillories in this pleasant little series.
Still, one would give a dozen of such books
for another Country House or Man of Pro-
perty, another Justice or Strife.
. E. V. Lucas and George Morrow have
done an unnecessary thing. They have
parodied Hun Hedin’s With the German
Armies in the West in a shilling book called
In Gentlest Germany, by Hun Svedend

Vers-librettists and blank-versifiers are re-
minded that the competition for the guinea
prize for the best poem in blank verse or
vers libre upon the subject, “Gallipoli,” will
close on August 7. Poems must be addressed
to this page.. As several competitors have
already submitted verses rimed and regular,
it is necessary once more to state that only
contributions in the two forms mentioned
above are admissible.
Katherine Prichard, who won the Hodder
and Stoughton’s Australian novel competi-
tion, the judge being Charles Garbage, who
has never seen Australia, spent her youth in
Victoria and Tassy. She attended J. B.
O’Hara’s school, and, later, became a
teacher. She contributed to this paper and
the Melb. Herald. Some years ago she
became society editor of the latter paper. Her
first volume, a tiny book of verses, Clovelly
Verses, was published last year. In 1910 a
play of hers was produced in Melbourne.
Anyhow, The Pioneers couldn’t be worse
than any novel written by her judge.
Samuel Butler
There is much more Gilbert Caiman than
Samuel Butler in the former’s critical study
of the latter, published in Seeker’s valuable
series. Yet he contrives to indicate, though
in Cannanese, the mentality of this delight-
ful personality. Butler narrowly escaped
becoming a parson ; and in order to explain
why this escape became a turning-point in
Butler’s life, it is necessary to remember in
what environment and century Butler lived.
“Nowadays a young man’s refusal at the last
moment to take orders would not be taken
tragically. It is not uncommon for young-
men to leave the lean prospects of the' Church
for the more exciting hopes of the stage.
But in the mid-nineteenth century, when
Paley was a prophet and sectarian quarrels
could shake the country, secession from the
Church was an insult to tradition and breed-
ing and the best hopes of England, which
then believed that it had made a corner in
true religion and was destined to prove the
salvation of the world. As this belief coin-
cided with commercial expansion, and was
very useful to it, it was extremely popular.”
Samuel Butler fled to Maoriland. It was'
there, a Canterbury sheep-farmer, that he
received, in common with the world, a greater
shock. He read Darwin’s The Origin of
Species. The immediate < result was
Erewhon. “It is an amateur’s book.
Its author regarded himself as a painter
by profession, and wrote it with vari-
ous intentions. First, to collect his
contributions to the M.L. press; second,
because a friend suggested it; third, because
England was too interestingly absurd to
escape ironic description.... Few good books
have so many faults, and yet it remains the
one enduring satire of the 19th century in
England. It cuts deep.”
Cannan is probably right in stressing the
importance of Butler’s sojourn in M.L. There
he made enough money by sheep-farming to
assure his support for life. And had he
remained in England he would probably have
become a journalist, becoming a professional
writer instead of the delightful amateur he
never ceased to be.
Butler’s second book, The Fair Haven, pub-
lished under a pseudonym, had so mixed a
reception and its irony so deceived its re-
viewers that when it became known as But-
ler’s work he was regarded finally as too
dangerous for the literary world. As Butler
admits, he made enemies of the parsons once
for all with Erewhon and The Fair Haven,
while his criticism of Darwinian evolution
made the scientific world even more angry
than The Fair Haven made the clergy. As
proof, Butler’s novel, The Way of All Flesh,
is vet so little known that Bernard Shaw re-
centlv complained that his “Butlensms had
been ascribed to Ibsen and Nietzsche. Lan-
nan compares this novel, “in a smaller way,
with Tom Jones, Pickwick. Jude the Obscure
War and Peace, and The Idiot, The subject
of this novel is now particularly the subject
of Shaw —the relation of parents and kiddies.
“In thought, as in painting, writing and
music, Butler was an amateur; and rather
in the French sense of the word than tne
Engiish. a collector, with knowledge and
taste, hut without profound purpose and the
power of concentration that goes with it. ...
But he loved his thoughts, and lavished hi?
humor on them, and decorated even . his
index with his humor. And every now and
then when he saw how ideas wei e being
mangled in public, he would take out some
of his choicest specimens just to show what
could be done with them. However, as the
public never paid any attention, be would
laugh and put them back again. Whether
they liked it or not he would leave his. col-
lection to the nation. A picture of his is in
fhe Tate Gallery, his portrait is in the
National Portrait Gallery, his manuscripts
are in the British Museum. .. -The tragodv
of the 19th centnry in England cauvht
Butler bv the scruff of his neck and tried
to shake the humor out of him. as it had
done with the divines and the politicians and
the scientists. .. .Butler found himself after
his shaking with liis brain rather rattled,
hut with no serious damage done. His con-
temporaries ... -hated being shaken, whereas
he. after the first astonishment, had rather
liked it.” . ~ _
So Cannan, interminably. Fdr those in-
terested in Butler and his narrow tract of
satire — and they ought to be niany—the
following works are worth notice: The Fair
Haven, The Notebooks of Samuel Butler.
Erewhon. Erewhon Revisited. T.ife . ana
Habit, The Way of All Flesh, all published
by Fifield.

ANTHONY HORDERNS’ SALE

VARIOUS VERSE
A Year Ago.
Last year by beach and headland
Night was glorious to behold,
And the heavens were full of splendor
And the stars were made of gold.
Then ’twas little that we reckoned
Of the evil days to come ;
For no voices gave us warning,
And the prophets all were dumb.
’Twas of Art and Song we gossiped
As we walked the coast by night—
Of the pictures you intended,
Or the verses I would write.
Or perchance our thoughts, expanding.
In a wider channel ran,
And we talked of Peace and Progress
And the Brotherhood of Man.
That was then ; but now ’tis altered,
And alone I walk the coast;
And the cliffs are grey and spectral,
And each wave is like a ghost.
And it seems, as I go walking,
That a shadow walks with me.
And I think of you, my artist,
And of far Gallipoli.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
In a Tram.
It was in a tram I saw her —
Small and pale, with eyes pathetic,
Big and brown, that held the mem’ry
Of a half-forgotten sorrow.
But still slumbered in their brown depths
Power to wake, to love and suffer;
And her woman’s dower immortal
Of forgiveness, self forgetting.
And I looked and wondered greatly
What had drawn those lines of patience,
What that look of world-old sorrow,
With such knowledge pack’d behind it,
On a face so young and girlish;
Stoop’d those shoulders, starved that
bosom,
That seemed yet so full of promise !
And while life brims o’er with beauty,
Thrilling sweetness, joy of living
On this Earth so lit with sunshine,
Where the rivers, reed-embowered,
Laugh their way through gum : clad
mountains ;
Where the gorgeous colored bird life,
Sailing under skies of sapphire,
Hymns the long days, warm with sun-
shine,
Nests at night ’mid velvet darkness,
Scented, star-gemmed and prophetic
Of the glory of to-morrow —
In a world of so much beauty,
What should brand a lasting sorrow
On a face where youth and brightness
Should sit smilingly together?
Oh, a plague on man-made sadness !
Life is spilling joys a-plenty.
Little Brown Eyes—wake to gladness !
.VICTORIA.
M. B. PATON.
The Last Struggle.
Within the ante-room of Heaven,
A place of spectral gloom,
Sat shadows twain, who did not heed
The magazines and things to read
That decked the dismal room.
Their wait was likely to be long;
With Europe running red
Saint Peter’s staff was overtasked.
At last one shadow turned and asked:
“Why is it you are dead?”
The other spectre’s eyeballs flashed:
“A tyrant foe arose
To war against my peaceful land
With outrage, shell and fiery brand.
To guard her from her foes
“My country called her patriot sons,
And I—l heard the call
To battle for her righteous cause :
I joined her splendid arms, and was
Amongst the first to fall.”
Two cold hands met in friendly grip.
“I. too, for freedom fought,
And fell! But there are more to slay
The tyrant foemen, and repay
The villainies they wrought!
“Their crimes were terrible! God help
The world should they prevail!
Wild beasts they seem in all their acts.
Wild beasts? Why, words, by all the facts,
To name them right must fail!
“But, after all, they’re coward curs.
Our braves will never yi Id.
Look at the battles we ha e won!
Think of the way the foe has run
When our men take the iield !”
To this the other shade agreed,
And said he: “After all,
God’s with our arms, for greed and sin
And craft and cunning cannot win.
Base Germany must fall!”
But, here, the other screamed aloud,
Hot anger in his face.
“Why, Gott in Hijnmel, this is wrong!
You. you yourself—you must belong
To England’s lying race!”
“Why, then, you are a cursed Hun,”
Rose in a kindred yell.
Regardless of their future life.
They closed at once in vengeful strife,
And into space they fell.
Saint Peter shortly came to tell
The pair their time was due :
But. looking out through Heaven’s bars,
A wild commotion in the stars
Was all that met his view,
iN'.S.W.

The Model Bicycle is Built for Reliability.
What are the particular points about a bicycle that will cause you to make your final
selection? Will you take the bicycle that has the best gloss to its enamel, or glitter to its
nickel? No, hardly that. You of course prefer a bicycle to have these things, but then you
must first consider more important features. It must be strong; it must be light; it must be
moderately priced.
The “Model” has these attributes. Every turn of the wheel, every flash of the spokes in the
sun, and the steady whirr of the chain as it carries you along swiftly, quietly and safely, tells
of strength, reliability and power.
It is made to our own order
by one of the best bicycle
builders in Australia. Each part
is the latest, the best known,
and the most widely approved.
Below are the specifications
of this machine, which we
consider to be the best value
on the market.

THURSDAY, JULY 29, 1915.
Pbice 6d.
THE CALL TO THE OTHERS.


The Bulletin
The National Australian Newspaper*
“Australia for the White Man.”
Printed and published by William Macleod, of
Musgrave and McLeod Streets, Mosman, for The
Bulletin Newspaper Co., Ltd., at the Office of the
Co., 214 George Street, Sydney, N.S.W., Australia.
PUBLISHED ONCE A WEEK.
TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. 6d. per Annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, post paid.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
If anager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say.
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letter's or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such

THURSDAY, JULY 29, 1915.
TO TEST THE NECESSITY FOR THE
REFERENDA BILLS.
The contending parties to the Referenda dis-
cussion are radically at issue except on one
point. All agree that “for the purposes of
the war” the National Parliament must have
practically unbounded powers. For the rest,
upholders of the Bilis, like W. M. Hughes,
say that Parliament under the existing Con-
stitution is “powerless” (which may turn
out to be wrong the first time, and every
other time, one of Parliament’s war meas-
ures is tested in the High Court) ; and op-
ponents of them, such as Joseph Cook,
Anstey, M.P., and the Age, assert without
qualification that Parliament’s powers are
“infinite and illimitable” —which is, of course,
absurd. E. F. Mitchell, K.C., of Victoria,
a Tory who has been fighting the Referenda
for years, put the position fairly in the
Argus. “If,” he wrote, “such matters of
legislation are really required for the defence
of Australia, then there is full power to legis-
late as to them under section 51, paragraph
vi.” The crux of the whole dispute is to
be found in the italicised words. What
measures are necessary for the defence of
Australia? There is not one Commonwealth
citizen who can decide with anything like
certainty; and there is only one body—
namely, the High Court —which has a right
to decide. The section says:—
The Parliament shall, subject to this Constitution,
have power to make laws for .... the .... Defence
of the Commonwealth and of the several States.
The words “subject to this Constitution”
must mean that Parliament could not, under
cover of exercising the powers conferred by
the section, exercise a power which was for-
bidden to it elsewhere, merely by putting
forward the excuse that a state of war
existed. On the other hand, there can be no
doubt that the existence of a state of war
brings into being a large category of mat-
ters which would be beyond the Common-
wealth’s powers if there were no war.
Moreover, there is equally no doubt that this
category would extend as the danger in-
creased and diminish as it contracted. For
example, it is certain that the Federal Par-
liament can now so far spurn the Trade and
Commerce clause as to censor intra-State
newspapers, and it is practically certain
that it could seize all the wheat and meat
in N. S. Wales or Queensland. But it is
also certain that it could not, at the present
stage, claim to fix all rents in a given area
as Britain did the other day. Yet if things
got very desperate it might even be intra
vires the National Parliament to close down
the Parliaments and Courts of the differ-
ent States, or do any other revolutionary
thing imaginable. And again it might not.
All would depend on what interpretation the
High Court put on the measure in relation to
the defence of Australia. And it must be
remembered that the High Court —which is
composed of citizens who, like the rest of us,
get their war-views from severely-censored
newspapers—is in no better position to esti-
mate the way the war is going than anyone
else. It must clearly be much more in the
dark in regard to the situation at the Front,
and Imperial affairs generally, than the
Ministers who are responsible for the legis-
lation on which it adjudicates. We thus
have the anomaly of a judicial body which is
insufficiently supplied with the facts em-
powered to allow or invalidate, on the
grounds of its apiilicahility or otherwise to
the defence of Australia, legislation eman-
ating from men who are in a position to
know everything possible about the Common-
wealth’s war requirements.
* * * *
One constantly finds Labor writers and
speakers demanding that the country shall
be “freed from the domination of the High
Court.” People of this school of thought
desire that all power should centre in the
National Parliament. The dream can never
come true so long as Australia remains a
Federation. If the Government’s Refer-
enda questions were carried to-morrow, two
things would still stand higher than Par-
liament— (1) the Constitution and (2) the
Court charged with the interpretation of the
Constitution. At present there is hardly a
war-measure passed by the National Parlia-
ment which is not fairly challengeable, on the
score of being ultra vires the Constitution
Act, by the first unpatriotic pointer who
happens along. If the Referenda are car-
ried next December the position will, of
course, be greatly eased. Parliament will
be given a host of powers which no reason-
able man would waste money contesting.
But even in that case an appeal to the
High Court is conceivable on any given war
Bill, since the High Court alone can decide
what legislation is or is not valid. The
undesirability of such an arrangement at
the present juncture is plain. It is cal-
culated to produce uncertainty and wild
allegations and party rancor. The per-
petually changing exigencies of the war will
have to be met, whether the Referenda go
through or not, by Parliamentary Bills,
each one of which must involve discussion,
and the wranglings of conflicting interests,
the loss of precious time, and, in the. end,
maybe, invalidation. The Bulletin is as
whole-hearted a supporter of the Referenda
principle as ever. But for the purposes, of
the present crisis it is in favor of something
much more sudden. The Referenda cannot
be carried for months. The Bulletin’s
scheme could be made operative in days.
The Referenda will cost £lOO,OOO or more.
The Bulletin’s scheme will not cost as
many farthings. The Referenda will arouse
—has already aroused —party bitterness.
The Bulletin’s scheme cannot shock . the
susceptibilities of either party. The idea
is to put into one measure all the powers
which the Commonwealth needs in relation to
Defence, and by a simple device to place those
powers in the hands of the Executive. The
Bill (which would be about the same size
as the Defence of the Realm Act) would
run,, without preamble, as follows: —
(1) This Act shall come into operation on a date
to be proclaimed, and shall expire on the th day
of or upon the Declaration of Peace, whichever
shall first happen. .
(2) The Governor-General, acting with the advice
of the Executive Council, shall have power by procla-
mation or proclamations to acquire, take over and
control for the purpose of the proper organisation
of the Defence of the Commonwealth and of the
several States as and so far as necessity arises in
respect thereof, all the industries, activities, matters
and things set out in the schedule hereto.
(3) At the first sitting of the High Court of Aus-
tralia after the passing of this Act the question shall
be submitted to the said Court whether or not this
Act is within the legislative power of the Parliament.
* * * *
Section 1 is intended to still all outcry
about Federal despotism by removing the
possibility of it beyond the period during
which all are agreed that it is necessary for
the safety of the nation. It is on similar
lines that England avoids militarism.
Englishmen at the beginning of the eighteenth cen-
tury found themselves placed in this dilemma. With
a standing army the country could not, they feared,
escape from despotism ; without a standing army the
country could not, they were sure, avert invasion.
The maintenance of national liberty appeared to in-
volve the sacrifice of national independence.— -Dicey.
Wherefore Britain invented the Mutiny Act
-—nowadays called the Army Act —as a
result of which the British Army dies each
year and could not revive unless Parlia-
ment so willed it. The objects of Section 2
are certainty and speed. The schedule would
specify all those “matters . and things”
which properly relate to Section 51 of the
Constitution Act; and it would. enable all
or any of them to be dealt with by the
simple and speedy method of Proclamation
instead of the slow and cumbrous method
of Bills and debates. The specified matters
and things would comprise the national
food, including its production, supply and
distribution, coal and all other minerals,
explosives, munitions, clothing and all the
factories suitable to the production of the
same, and all matters pertaining to the
supply, production and distribution thereof.
It would be the business of the Attorney-
General to advise the Navy and Defence
Departments with regard to the compila-
tion of this list. Section 3 would enable
the Act to be “put upon its trial.” A High
Court judgment which affirmed the validity
of the schedule to Section 2 in its entirety
would clear up all doubt, reduce chaos to
order, and enable the war to be prosecuted
with as near an approach to efficiency as
our Constitution allows. If any item of the
schedule was ultra vires the Parliament it
would be “severable” in accordance with a
well-known legal principle, without invali-
dating any other part of the Act. Incor-
rigible Tories on the one hand and bigoted
Referenda enthusiasts on the other may
claim hopefully that the High Court would
refuse to pronounce upon such an Act: on
the ground that its determination would
not be an exercise of the Judicial power.
But the High Court contains some of Aus-
tralia’s greatest citizens. Bearing }n mind
the beneficial effect which the Court’s affir--
mation of the Act would have upon the
national welfare, and the countless in=
stances in legal history of the adaptability
of the Judiciary to the necessities of the
time", it is hard to believe that the Court
would shirk the exercise of a power which
it would acquire by the simple process of
saying that it had it.
* * * *
Here is a scheme which should satisfy
every Australian who is for his country
first and his party afterwards. It will be
no good to the Liberal who is using the
war as an argument against every sort of
political change; and it will be revolting to
the Laborite who wants to use the war as
an argument in favor of the Referenda. It
will effect the reforms at which the Refer-
enda aims while leaving the Referenda to
be carried or otherwise in less strenuous
times. But no Laborite who believes in
the Referenda should fear to see the prin-
ciple given a chance to justify itself by a
temporary trial ; and no consistent Liberal
can doubt that, provided the electors are
allowed to behold “Unification” at work,
they will turn the Referenda questions down
at the end of the war. If the High Court
on some abstract technical nicety refuses
to touch the Act as an Act, then, in view
of the notorious inadequacy of the War
Precautions Act, the carriage of the Refer-
enda will be a certainty in December. And,
furthermore, in such an unthinkable event,
the High Court, which so many Laborites
distrust, would damn itself as a body that
had proved incapable of rising to the neces-
sities of a great occasion. Unification
would then come with a rush.
THE WAY OF THE GREAT BEAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We have crossed the land from Dan to Beer-
sheba, and it has cost us £l4. Look at the map
and put your finger on Vladivostok and
then run it through to Petrograd. Six thou-
sand miles, isn’t it? I don’t know. It
may have been sixty thousand. We were 14
nights in the train, and 14 days when we
went no further from our carriage than the
length of a railway station. The Russian
steppe is as the sea —a sea that blows up
a fine dust like sea-fog into your eyes and
nostrils, and fills your mouth with grit and
your soul with a longing for clean air and a
bath. In the midst of a vast, interminable
ocean of heaving green hills that you have
travelled through for days, you get down
and survey your engine. It is like a ship.
The single track stretches into the distance
like the course of a sea chart. There is a
single station building beside you, a tall,
gaunt water-tower, a narrow platform with
an armed and uniformed guard upon it, a
ragged porter and a solitary, wild-eyed Tar-
tar tribesman beside him. The blue sky is
above, and the immense green sea around.
The passengers get down and hurry into the
buffet for hot water and tea. Two bells
sound and they scramble back into the train.
Three bells sound, a faint whistle blows, the
engine snorts twice, a belated traveller runs
out of the buffet and scrambles aboard. We
jolt twice uncertainly and then suddenly
resume the leisurely pace at which we have
eaten up 3000 miles, and at which we have
to eat up 3000 miles more.
Sometimes in the midst of a desolation,
unrelieved even by a railway building, we
draw up on a siding and wait for an east-
ward-bound train of immigrants. One or
two people get down and stretch their legs.
The wait drags on for an hour or more.
Women and children begin to climb down.
They grow bolder and scatter over the hills
in search of wild flowers. Our engine aban-
dons us and goes off to do some haulage else-
where. The railway people chat with the
military patrol which mounts guard over the
train whenever we pull up. The train we
have waited for creeps in. Returning peas-
ants fraternise with immigrants sent out by
a paternal and far-seeing Government to
people the vast, empty garden of Siberia.
Our train still lingers, having lost its engine.
Our engine returns, but there is no haste.
Two bells ring, and flower-laden children
come running from the hills. Three bells
ring and we bump slowly back on to the
main track again.
Sometimes you wake up at night amidst
the soft breathing of 30 Russian nostrils
that share the bottled atmosphere of your
compartment with you, and find the train
stock-still in an intense silence, broken only
by the distant panting of the engine. There
are the lights of a city in the distance, shin-
ing on the edge of the dark plain like the
lights of a seaport on the sea horizon. People
pass up and down, and lantern-light gleams
on bayonets. You fall asleep and wake up
again in the morning to the steady motion of
the train, progressing westward.
* * * *
There are two classes of trains on the
Siberian railways. There are those of the
Compagnie Internationale des Wagon-lits
and those of the Russian Government. The
trains of the foreign company are fast
trains. They minister to the foreigner’s
incomprehensible love of rushing from one
place to another place at the highest speed.
They are likewise expensive trains. They carry
doctors, trained nurses, interpreters and
enamelled baths. They run up to scheduled
time, much to the discomfort and annoyance
of the Russian officials, and great is the in-
dignation of their passengers if they are an
hour late in a journey of 6000 miles. The
Russian travels on the Russian State train.
He is never too late to catch a Russian
train. He is never too poor to travel, with
his wife and family and all his worldly
possessions, for any distance on a Russian
train. The slow train is to the fast train
as the wild interior is to the seaboard. A
foreigner is rara avis on the slow train. He
is looked at with wild amaze which changes
to kindly interest. Until he solves the mys-
teries of the 50-odd letters of the Russian
alphabet, the time-tables and the station
names are a sealed book to him.
Our fellow-passengers were mostly soldiers.
The stations were full of soldiers. An
armed guard sat on our tailboard, and an-
other surrounded our engine. Every few
miles a soldier stepped smartly out of a log
cabin, a grinning brood of children staring
between bis legs, and stood to the salute till
the train had passed > and where the line ran
through the Chinese province of Manchuria a
little Russian army tumbled out of a block-
house every mile py so and stood to atten-
tion, the occasional Chinese soldier, repre-
senting the Republic’s sovereign authority,
looking perfectly impotent beside them.
We had no reading matter with us on that
interminable journey. We had no need of
reading matter. The panorama of a new
continent was spread for us outside the
windows. The country which two weeks
ago had been one sheet of snow was now a
mass of magnificent wild flowers. Vast herds,
locked up during the bitter winter, were now
roaming over incomparable pasture, camels,
donkeys, Siberian ponies, fat-tailed sheep and
oxen all mixed in one mob. The humps of
the camels and the tails of the sheep, after
the long winter fast, were mere folds of flap-
ping integument. Some vicious insect, worse
than any Australian summer pest, infested
the air, and all the Siberians wore thick nets
over hands and faces. At the stations fresh
butter, brick tea, curdled milk, huge sour
cheeses and roasted fowls were to be bought
from peasant women at open-air booths.
Manchurian women with queer head-dresses,
Mongolian priests, Kirghiz tribesmen mounted
on extraordinary wooden saddles, descendants
of the ancient Tartar invaders of Europe,
came into the stations to greet the violator
of their ancient fastnesses. At intervals of
days we passed big towns, new, aggressive,
in the green desolation of the unpeopled
plains. Almost hourly immigrant trains
passed us going east, to fill a land
crying for population. These are the
modern representatives of the old con-
vict gangs which have tramped the
same w r on foot through the pages of Eng-
lish fiction for a hundred years past, and
which are still doing it. The enlightened
public opinion of Siberia long ago compelled
the Government to check the transportation
of criminals and nolitical offenders into what
will one day be among the fairest countries
in the world. Twice we passed long rows
of American harvesting machinery going
east to take the place of the wooden plough
with which Asia has scratched the soil from
time immemorial.
As we drew nearer to the boundaries of
Europe towns became more frequent. Vast
virgin forests of pine divided vaster stretches
of cultivated land. Big grain elevators ap-
peared at the side of the line, and such reck-
less quantities of spare rolling stock awaiting
the necessities of the harvest as would make
an Australian wheatgrower long for the
benevolent despotism of the Russian Tsar.
The same benevolent despotism showed it-
self in the railway line which runs as straight
as the crow flies between Moscow and Petro-
grad, ignoring all the cities on its way. Like
all railways in all countries, a mass of cor-
x'uption and intrigue centred in the selec-
tion of its route, one wealthy landlord obtain-
ing a deviation in one direction and another
in another. The Tsar on being appealed to
for a final decision, took a ruler, drew a
straight line between the two principal points
and commanded the surveyors to take that
route. Many a democratic country would be
the better for an autocratic hand occasionally
to rule a straight line through its
“diplomacy.”
Moscow has a golden roof of church spires
and domes. The great fire of 1812, when
Moscow was burnt about Napoleon’s ears by
its own patriotic inhabitants, was probably
as splendid a sanitary measure as the Fire
of London. Moscow has wide streets and
squares as vast as deserts, on whose hither
side the buildings look like distant ranges of
blue hills. Magnificent shops, full of Pari-
sian millinery, alternate with ancient ornate
shrines to the Virgin and Child. Our
bloated drosky driver in passing drops his
reins and crosses himself fervently. The
skyline is dominated by the grotesque domes
of the churches, like monstrous vegetable
shapes erected to some pagan god of agricul-
ture —golden oranges gleaming in the sun ;
enormous, variegated, spiky things, like pine-
apples ; smaller bulbous things like onions
on long stalks. Inside these curious monu-
ments to the God of Greek Orthodoxy is col-
lected vast wealth —golden ikons set with
precious stones, a barbarous profusion of
silver, gilt and jewelled ornaments spread on
the altars like offerings to an infernal God of
Wealth. Mothers held children up to kiss
each jewel in turn, and performed the act of
worship before each sacred emblem.
Inside the Kremlin is gathered a magnifi-
cent collection of cathedrals, royal palaces
and monuments. Parties of ragged people
were shepherded up and down marble stair-
ways and in and out of gorgeous rooms
amongst the pomp and splendor of imperial
residences. Outside the Kremlin a cathedral,
as mad a freak of architecture as the childish
mind of barbarism ever perpetrated. Driving
back from a crowded cafd we passed a shrine
whose steps were covered with sleeping
figures crouching in the cold twilight at the
feet of an effigy of Christ, as if they hoped
to get warmth out of its sparkling jewels.
It was fitting that, after a journey of 14
days, our train should arrive at Petrograd
four days late. Russia never hurries. No
other country sets the pace for Russia. On
its trains, in its factories, in its social devel-
opment, it has no schedule times. Russia is
not a country, it is a world; and those travel-
ling aldermen and town councillors who draw
invidious comparisons between its backward-
ness and the civic perfections of their own
blest municipalities forget the fact. Such criti-
cisms are as relevant as comparisons of the
rival merits of the Earth and Mars. Russia
has faced tremendous problems courageously.
Fifty-five years ago the vast mass of the
population were slaves, without land, without-
rights, without hope. In 1860, Alexander
11., with one sweep of his autocratic hand,;
gave 50 million slaves their freedom. Fifty
million landless serfs, unused to and unaware
of the dangers and the privileges of freedom,
had to be absorbed into the State as free
individuals, against the opposition of their
rich and powerful masters. And this was
done successfully. The United States of.
America did not do so well. The same Tsar
that liberated the slaves was blown to pieces
by a revolutionary, the draft of a Constitu-
tion he had drawn up in his Royal pocket.
But 40 years afterwards the Russians got
their Duma, which is slowly gaining recogni-
tion from the State and people. In 1906 c.
despite the intolerance of the vast bulk of
the established Church, general religious
freedom was granted, and now the spires of
churches of every creed may be seen ming-
ling with the golden domes.
Since then there has been continuous re-
form in the civil, military and police admin-
istrations ; corrupt officials have followed in
the footsteps of political exiles to Siberia;


greater and greater sums have been spent
on education, and attempts have been made
to control the sale of vodka, m
August, 1914, the Tsar instituted complete
and permanent prohibition throughout the
country. " Even England has not been able
to follow that lead.
Mervyn G. Skipper.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
An Archbishop Misses the ’Bus.
Archbishop Clarke, of Melbourne, has told
a meeting of churchmen that he does not
consider it advisable for their profession to
join the ranks of the army. And therein
the Archbishop makes a grievous error of
ecclesiastical policy. For generations his
Church and other Churches have been get-
ting more and more out of touch with the
people, and especially with the male half of
the people. It has become a truism with
clergymen everywhere that the young men —
the section from which the Expeditionaries
are drawn —have almost ceased going to
church. Here was a chance that may not
come again in centuries for the Church to
revivify itself by showing the world unan-
swerably that it teaches a man’s religion;
and the first step which occurs to its chiefs
is to anchor the able-bodied priests of the
Faith in the puerile arena of the bun-fight.
The Bulletin has never been hostile to
any religion. But it has always objected,
and does still, to clerical humbugs and self-
advertisers ; and it has found matter for
derision in the weaklings and asses who are
fostered by the attitude of prelates such as
Archbishop Clarke. If this constitutes hos-
tility to religion, then the surgeon is hostile
to life who tries to cut out a cancer. The
truth is the Church has labored for years
beneath the futility of its parsons. There
have been brilliant exceptions—plenty of
them—but mostly the parson is a futile, in-
efficient little man, in absurd clothes, who
for a starvation wage hurries about minister-
ing to the already “converted,” pouring spirit-
ual comfort into the ears of those who are
regenerate and know it. It is a rare thing
to find in him the golden mean between
the bleater and the bellower. The young
man whom the Church desires to allure
watches the efforts of both varieties with dis-
favor, and decides that they are not the per-
sons to save him. Which is only natural.
No one is going to feel confident of being
snatched from Beelzebub by a human ab-
surdity. The tragedy of normal, peaceable
times is that owing to the obtrusiveness of
the actor-parson and his foil the fool-parson,
the sensible, understanding and manly par-
son never gets a show. Billjim avoids him
so firmly, on the reputation of his class, that
he finds the utmost difficulty in coming
within a mile of that elusive citizen’s blanky.
Armageddon comes and offers the Church
an opportunity to get right up alongside
Billjim ; to be with him in the great and
burning moments of his career; to win his
confidence ; and to prove to him that a par-
son can be as good a man as any. And the
unique chance is being spurned. What a mis-
take that is French experience is showing at
this moment. A year ago the priest was
one of the stock jokes of the French press.
There was no position too degrading to de-
pict him in, no story too obscene to write
round him. To-day, since thousands of him
have been and still are serving with the
colors, the priest jest has died out of the
papers. But with us, though we too are
fighting for Justice and Freedom against
premeditated robbery with violence, and
every description of moral infamy, foolish
Archbishops gather their parsons of a fight-
ing age around them and say: “You ought
not to join in this struggle. Your herma-
phroditic job is here, in comfortable security,
among the women and children !” How can
such an institution hope to grip those whom
it says it is the most anxious to grip?
• <>
A Nation with Its Head in the Sand.
When will the daily newspapers start tell-
ing the whole truth about the war? They
have stopped talking of German “routs”; of
famine in Berlin and Vienna; and of the
likelihood of the whole trouble being at an
end in a few weeks. But their reservations
are almost as damaging now as their mis-
statements were six months ago. What
object has anyone but a German, for ex-
ample, in concealing the fact that the posi-
tion on the whole of the Russian front is
most alarming? The German objective is
Warsaw. The capture of that city will
mean vast stores of clothing and provisions
for the enemy; worse still, it will mean the
descent upon the British and French, on the
Western front, of a horde of picked soldiers.
There is no sense in the papers bleating
about the Russians’ genius for fighting a
rearguard action, and the likelihood that
Russia will, in the end, “weather the
storm.” If Russia is beaten within the
next few months there will be no comfort
to the British Empire in the knowledge that
the Bear must still “weather the storm” in
the end, since there is grave peril of our
armies being met and exterminated in the
interim. If anyone wants to understand how
well the Austro-German forces have done
in the East during the past three months,
let him follow their progress on a map, and
then imagine the degree of confidence which
would justifiably exist amongst the Allies if
they had such achievements to their credit.
In the beginning of May the Germans, hav-
ing got together enormous stores of muni-
tions from their well-organised war-shops,
and having put in the field another 2,000,000
men (the compulsory trainees of the preced-
ing nine months), were in a position to start
pushing the invader out of Galicia. It
was no half-hearted push. The Russians
were bustled briskly over the Western Car-
pathians through Gorlice to Jaroslav, which
was taken. Przemysl fell next, and shortly
afterwards L/emberg. Supposing our armies
had, in a month, hunted the Germans
through Flanders, killing 750,000 of them and
capturing a quarter of a million prisoners
and a thousand guns; and supposing that we
Fad retaken Brussels and Antwerp, and found
in these cities war-materials as. valuable as
the products of the Galiciam oil-wells—sup-
posing these things, we would be" justified ifr
feeling as the Germans doubtless feel to-day.
In the past week the Russians have been
driven out of Prasnysz—or, as the papers
put it, “the attack on Prasnysz forced us to
concentrate on a position nearer the Narew.”
The strongholds around Warsaw have been
systematically invested. And it was men-
tioned encouragingly by one of the daily-
paper war-experts on Friday, that ‘ even
if Warsaw fell the Russians would fight
steadily, falling back on the Volga if neces-
sary.” Things are as bad for us, in effect,
as they would be for Germany if the Allied
troops had crossed the Rhine after investing
Cologne and Dusseldorf, and were pouring
towards the Weser. The curse which falls
upon us for the persistent suppression of the
truth is that old men of means do not help
financially, and young men of good physique
do not enlist —both justifying themselves on
the plea that the Germans are being beaten.
The time is due, and long since overdue, to
tell the truth and shame the devil, the devil
in this hour of the nation’s great need being
the shirker who withholds his service either
of muscle or of money.

Muddle Goes Down in a Heap.
Maoriland rocked on its foundations lately.
The storm centre was the big military camp
at Trentham, near Wellington. There had
been much sickness in it; recruits contracted
a habit of dying in a hurry; strong men
were swiftly flattened out by something call-
ing for a nurse ; friends and relatives at a
distance found themselves unable to get in-
formation about the condition of Bill or
Jim until a telegram came to say he would
be buried to-morrow. Red tape did its best
to hold the community down; but the rumb-
ling grew so ominous, and the accusative
paragraph in the newspapers so frequent,
that Minister for Defence Allen felt the
time was about ripe to say something. He
therefore ascended to a high place and said
the population was being needlessly alarmed
by agitators ; that the camp was a most de-
lightful sanatorium; that the boys were
doing well, and were too happy to write
home and explain the situation. Then a
great deluge of letters poured over the land
from the happy lads to say they had measles,
influenza, wet fet and damp beds; that if
mother didn’t hear from them again she
would know they were dead; that things
were misery and woe. After that Parlia-
ment met. The first squall carried the
Speaker’s wig away; at the second the Ser-
geant-at-Arms heroically lashed the Mace to
a granite pillar. The fury of the storm
staggered the oldest inhabitant. It was
shown that the camp was a pest hole, a bog,
a breeding place for microbes; that the sick
were numbered by hundreds ; that the hospi-
tal arrangements were a tragic jest; that
there was muddle and incompetence in
everything connected with the place. The
upshot of the riot was that the Government
hastily evacuated Trentham and set up
camps at other places. Then Allen was re-
lieved of a couple of portfolios, and a Com-
mission was appointed to let the Governor
know who should be scalped. At latest
everybody who had a finger in the pie was
accusing everybody else of being the Jonah.
Also Massey’s feet were feeling very cold,
and the brown majority of one was sus-
pected of having chilblains.

An Idol with Cast-iron Feet.
Germany must be well satisfied with Mr.
Asquith as Prime Minister of Great
Britain. Asquith, who enjoys a strong-
man superstition, is opposed to sweeping
reforms. He mourned the temporary sus-
pension of Party Government, the errors of
which arc dear to him through long
acquaintance. His consideration for vested
interests over-ruled his colleague, Lloyd
George, when that “iconoclast” proposed
to make Britain less unsober by Act of
Parliament. Whilst every other leading
member of the previous Asquith Govern-
ment (bar the spiritually-German Hal-
dane) was denouncing the evils of drink,
the Prime Minister was opposed to any
legislation of which Socialists could
approve. To restrict the consumption of
alcohol and interfere with its manufacture
was to move in the direction of nationalisa-
tion of the liquor trade; therefore Asquith
was against “drastic” reform of the trade.
Again, his shocking lack of pluck to deal
reasonably with the Welsh miners’ rebel-
lion was evidently due rather to anti-
socialism than to natural affection for the
private mine-owners. The workers wanted
nationalisation of the industry for their
own protection; therefore, at a moment
when their labor was of paramount im-
portance to the nation, they tried to force
Asquith to transfer the private property
to the State. But to Asquith—calm,
strong, upright, gentlemanly Asquith—
the bare idea of establishing Government
control was positively alarming. It would
be a triumph for Socialism, and a blow at
the prejudices of all the Asquiths of
Britain. The coal strike became a national
peril for the same reason that the short-
comings of the private munition factories
were a national peril—because Asquith
is a Premier who shies off drastic reforms.
The drink evil, the evil of strikes in coal
mines and munition works, the German spy
and German intriguing perils, the War
Office scandals —all these things have been
paltered with by the Governments in which
Asquith has had chief say. When one
thinks of all the things that Asquith has
been with difficulty compelled by public
opinion to sanction within the past year—
the ousting of Haldane from the War
Office and his subsequent ousting from the
Cabinet; the appointment of clever com-
mercial men to take the management of
war-supply business, the internment and
deportation of thousands of Germans for
whose arrest the wisest newspapers had
long clamored in vain, the obliteration of
party lines, and finally the introduction of a
sort of national service—when one thinks
of these things and of how soundly England
slept under Asquith’s -leadership,- the ques-
tion is: .Can England ever be quite awake
whilst he remains in charge? These are
times for swift reform, and Asquith is no
reformer. He is too inherently Conserva-
tive. All tjie influential people whom he
respected' before the war he still respects.
He hates to make himself unpleasant.
Everybody speaks nicely of him. He is
the hope of Germunj'. Lim despair of evei*y-
one who wanted the Allies to win the war
this year.

Big Wrongs and Little Remedies.
The recent case of J. G. Jardine —he is an
actor who was hit by a revolver wad whilst
playing in “The Kelly Gang” at Perth—
illustrates the prehistoric inequity of the
law of Master and Servant. Jardine lost
his eye and recovered £200 from the manage-
ment. Julius Knigiit could have got no
more in Australia, and in England he
couldn’t have got anything. This is because
an actor is excluded by the nature of his
calling from the benefits of the Employers’
Liability Act, and can only sue under the
Workman’s Compensation Act. A plaintiff
suing under the Employers’ Liability Act is
faced by two “doctrines” which are obstacles
for all persons, and insuperable ones for
actors. These are (a) the doctrine of
Common Employment, and (b) the doctrine
of volentia. The one means that for
damage caused to an employee by the act
of a fellow-servant the employer is not
liable; the other says in effect that all em-
ployees embark on their jobs with notice
of the perils involved, and have no right to
complain if the risk falls in. Church v.
Appleby is an example of volentia. Church
worked on a staging over the Thames. _ It
was protected on one side only. One night
he fell over the unprotected side and was
drowned. Since he had voluntarily under-
taken the risk his widow got nothing. As
for Common Employment, one Hutchinson,
a servant of a railway company, was travel-
ling from work in the co.’s train, when a
collision took place owing to the negligence
of other servants, and he was killed. It
was held that because he was one of the
servants—though he had nothing to do with
the accident —his widow could not recover.
Either of these doctrines would have sufficed
to defeat Jardine had he proceeded under
the Employers’ Liability Act. Mepiiisto
and Faust mangled by an accident to the
“trap”; the son of William Tell taking
the arrow in the neck; Henry Y. falling off
his horse at Agincourt; the melodrama
heroine crushed to death by the property
train—all would fail because of volentia;
whilst it is impossible to imagine anything
that an actor does which would not arise
from an act of “common service.” For the
purposes of the Workman’s Compensation
Act these two doctrines are, happily, abo-
lished. As long as a workman has been
injured “in and about” the course of his
employment the employer is liable—but only
to a very small amount, as little in some
States as £200. This legislation was copied
by Lloyd George from Germany, and by
Australia from Lloyd George. In the
English edition only persons earning less
than £150 a year may claim, which is why
Julius Knight could have recovered
nothing in England had he instead of
Jardine got the pistol-wad in the eye. He
would not have been “a workman.” Australia
has improved on this by putting the limit at
the other end. Anyone can claim, but nobody
can recover more than £200, or a little more
in some parts. Whatever may be said for
volentia, the “Common Employment” doc-
trine forms no part of the symmetrical body
of the law, and it hasn’t even reason at the
back of, it. Until 1837 no one had ever
heard of it, and it was then evolved from the
air by Chief Baron Abinger to meet a par-
ticular circumstance, which was that for the
first time in history a servant had dared to
sue his master. Plaintiff was a butcher-boy
who fractured his thigh because the cart
which he drove had been badly loaded by a
fellow-servant. The Court was paralysed
at his claim for damages. Lord Abinger
said: —•
To allow this sort of action to prevail would be
an encouragement to the servant to omit that dili-
gence and caution which he is in duty bound to exer-
cise on the behalf of his master.... and which dili-
gence and caution, while they protect the master, are
a much better security against any injury the ser-
vant may sustain by the negligence of others engaged
under the same master, than any recourse against his
master for damages could possibly afford.
A truly splendid bit of Tory cant! As to
volentia, it is contrary to the whole spirit
of modern sociology. It is based on the
fiction that the working man is in a position
to pick and choose his jobs. In any case, it
is monstrous that a man may have no more
for the loss of an eye or a widow for the
loss of a husband —than £200. If a piece of
inanimate machinery is broken, no matter
how, the employer must replace it. The
State or private charity does not give him
half the money to restore his plant, or com-
pensate him for going without it. But
when a part of his human machinery is
destroyed or damaged, he is not called upon
to pay more than a very limited amount,
which may and generally does bear no rela-
tion to the value of the human “machine”
affected. Mostly public benevolence or pri-
vate charity has then to come to the rescue.
It is uneconomic; it is unjust.
“Moresby” on a matter of administration
Re Papua (B. 3/6/’15). It is stated that when
the costly mountain zig-zag up the Hombron Bluff
and the Vaigana swamp stud farm were carried out,
the Lieut.-Governor was absent and unaware of the
idiocies. The site of the stud farm was selected in
December, 1907. The road up Hombron Bluff was
laid out in 1908 (see Annual Report 1908). Judge
Murray did not leave the Territory from the date
of his appointment in 1907 until June, 1910. The
road is the only one used to the Sogeri Tablelands-—
the most prosperous district in the Territory. If it
was a mistake, why have travellers absolutely dis-
carded the old road ?
Whether the stud farm is a success or not can be
judged by the fact that it was started with 41 head
of stock, and by natural increase it has now 74 head.
If the Kikori exploration was “a comic-opera ven-
ture,” I wonder why Staniforth Smith is the only
British New Guinea explorer since Sir William
Macgregor who has been invited to lecture before
the Royal Geographical Society of London, which is
the final Court of Appeal regarding the merits of
exploration? His lecture was given pride of place in
the Geographical Journal of April, 1912. The Vice-
President, Major Leonard Darwin, said: “There are
three qualifications every explorer must have. He
must have personal courage, he must have fore-
thought, and he must have the aptitude of making
friends with the natives. I think our lecturer has
been conspicuous in all these three qualities.”

Defence Minister Allen, of Maoriland,
has announced that in the case of certain
soldiers who have been returned from
abroad on account “of disability caused by
their own misconduct,” he has decided that
they must be “punished” by being refused
for further enlistment. Which shows that
Allen has some queer notions about con-
tagious disease. If these men have been
restored and are now physically fit to join
the colors, the talk about punishing them
by turning them down is blank idiocy. Men
are wanted badly, and it is wildly grotesque
to refuse the services of a lad because he has
been an unlucky young ignoramus who fell
into the soup prepared for him by the asses
that prevent the spread of essential knowledge.
If, on the other hand, a man will not stand
the physical test required for service, who
in the name of Mangaweka does Allen
imagine he is punishing while the unsani-
tary culprit wanders casually round the
landscape? If Allen has turned any .of
these disease-disseminators loose on the com-
munity, he deserves to be shot out of his
job ; if he is still preventing the enlistment
of eligible men by assuming the functions
of a Minister of Retribution, he deserves a
straight waistcoat decorated with the Iron
Cross qf Ihc Kaiser.
Drift.
Randolph Bedfoed meets the British
Empiah:—
News that the Germans’ aerial torpedo steered by
wireless is the invention of Australians—the Rob-
erts brothers —lifted by the Germans from the
specifications for patent, brings the memory of the
fact that the Robertses offered their invention to
the British War Office, and, after being delayed until,
their patience was exhausted, were finally turned’
down. It is the experience repeated of that brilliant
Australian, the inventor of the range-finder, who,
after being driven almost crazy by the drifters of :
English officialdom, abandoned in despair his attempt;
to help the Empire, and returned to Australia with a
record of lost time. The Caldwell gun was offered!
them a year ago, and they are still paltering. They
were offered it direct, but the Australian syndicate
had finally to pass it on to a “placing” and ex-
ploiting syndicate for £65,000 and a royalty—which
means that the British nation is to pay just so much
unnecessary money as represents the syndicate’s pro-
fit.
Twelve years or so ago I joined H. H. Drysdale
in London in the attempt to float the 15,000,000
acres of Arafura into a pastoral company. I got out
of the active job after six months—which is quite
enough to waste on any one scheme—but Drysdale
stood by for a further 18 months; and formed the
Eastern and African Cold Storage Supply Go., Ltd.-,'
in 500,000 shares of £1. It was the time the
remount scandals were raging. Certain British
officers and gentlemen had been discovered in league
with German-Jew horse-copers buying worn-out Hun-
garian cab-horses at £4 or £5 a head and selling
them as British remounts for S’Africa at £40 a
head. The treason in selling in war-time a menace
to the life of any British soldier who mounted it
called for somebody’s death; but when in London
I asked, “Who is to be hanged?” they looked shocked,
after the manner of the English when the word
“scandal” is mentioned, and said “Ssh! Bad form I
Ssh! ”
Drysdale and I talked over getting another string
to the Arafura bow by trying to arrange a remount
scheme with the War Office. There is no better horse-
breeding ground in the world than the big limestone
plains south of the Roper, and there, in addition to
Arafura itself, we had many thousand square miles
of country. Chamberlain was then Secretary for
the Colonies, and my introduction to the War Office
had to come through him. The “colonies” is slow
enough, but it does not sutler the red-tape worm so
continuously as the other Departments. I was
handed over to a man of energy—Lord Monkhurst
—and he gave me an introduction to Major-General
Truman, and lie sent me to a very high and very
weary official of the Remount Department in the
little, old, faded-red brick building of the War
Office in St. James. The gentleman was frankly
bored, not only by my business, but by all business;
his mind seemed to have a chronic habit of yawning
at everything—Australia, me and the Firmament.
He seemed to have a detestation of horses, and
especially of remounts —and remounts were his job.
He waxed almost enthusiastic as I explained the pro-
position—the War Office to pay a retaining fee per
head per annum, and for that insurance we to guaran-
tee delivery of a stated number at a stated price
when called upon. He asked me to call again.
Another call, and he threw me down to a wearier
gentleman than himself; a man whose brains de-
manded a 41 hat, and whose feet asked for number
14 boots. He also took a 13 collar. After one
interview with him I sent out the S.O.S. signal, and
was returned to the man who had been enthusiastic
over remounts for five minutes.
I got him enthusiastic again, and he gave me
reason to believe that I would have finality next day.
The Government, he said, was stuck for horses, and
whether the Boer War lasted 10 years or 10 days
longer, the Government was determined to put its
remount business into organisation for this war or
any other war of the future. And would I call
to-morrow? I called to-morrow, and was asked to
call to-morrow. And I called to-morrow, and was
asked to call to-morrow for a fortnight. At the end
of the fortnight I was asked to call in a few days
for three weeks, and at the end of three weeks I was
asked to call next week for two weeks.
When I last called the man who had been enthu-
siastic looked at me in a puzzled way, repeated my
name, searched a basket of papers, and said help-
lessly: “Let me see; what was your mattah?” I
explained that my matter was horses; real horse
made of meat, with a leg at each of his four cor-
ners, some tail, and a place in his face to put a bit
to which were attached two lines, one to pull him
to the right, the other to pull him to the left.
“Oh! Quaite,” said he. “Come again next week.”
That day we went out of the remount business for
keeps. The only thing they seemed to know about
horses in that department was stringhalt, which is
the disease of English administration.
Drift! Drift! Stringhalt! Bad form! Hush it
up! Ssh!
And half a mile or so eastward—in the grimy
Exeter Hall, stuffed with vanity and lunacy, the
other ruler of England, the Nonconformist Con-
science, which is mostly brainless and conscienceless,
bleated of the things which do not matter.
These are the rulers who should be pilloried and
then abolished after the war —the petticoat influence
at the Foreign Office, and all the other offices, for
that matter; the cult of “birth,” which is often to
say scrofula; Government by bishops; the Noncon-
formist Conscience, the myth of a ruling class, and
the immorality of a starving one.
Drift! Stringhalt! Bad form! Hush it up! Sssssh!
