Amongst the liberal helping of confused
war cablegrams published in Australia dur-
ing the week, there are at least two that
have not been contradicted. One is that
Russia is praying for victory; the other
that the Hun has a vast number of flags
ready to unfurl when the triumph of Yon
Hindenbtjkg in Poland shall be complete.
That Ivan is being still hard pressed is
certain. But equally clear is it that the
German arms have not yet been carried to
a point where decisive victory can be claimed.
Both are as clear as the evidence that our
part of the world is profoundly ignorant
about the progress of the struggle in detail.
As to the . broad, general course of events
there remains, however, not much room for
doubt, and that course is not of a character
to make the German particularly sad. If
the allied forces in France and Belgium
were making as much progress as the asso-
ciated Huns have achieved during the last
couple of months in Galicia and thereabouts,
the end of the war would not look so re-
mote as it does just now. Nor perhaps
would the attitude of the several Balkan
States be quite the same. What is taking
place there remains more or less shrouded
in mystery, although it appears that Bul-
garia has secured a slice of territory from
Turkey and sent over, to Berlin for a loan,
that Roumania is being addressed in per-
emptory. language by Potsdam, and that
Greece is trembling on the verge of some-
thing critical. In Gallipoli bayonet and
bomb are still hard at work at the same old
spot. Incidentally Uncle Sam has spoken
about murder to Berlin in terms of such
studied emphasis that on his birthday
William is not likely to be cheered by the
usual cable message from Washington.
Also the borrowing of money goes on at
frantic speed all over the world, the maimed
and wounded are creeping back from the
line of battle, and the call for volunteers to
take their place is as urgent as ever it was.
A good scheme to help the fund for
the wounded has been evolved by the
Commonwealth Patriotic Playing Card
Association. D. H. Souter, Sydney Ure
Smith and Harry Julius have de-
signed a series of playing cards, which
will be on sale throughout the world
immediately. The picture cards will repre-
sent the four Kings and four Queens of the
Allies, while for the four knaves there is
considerable competition among the Huns.
Australians who buy these cards, which are
sold at various prices, will know that they
are doing a patriotic service. The manage-
ment of the association, which is non-profit-
bearing, is in the hands of very capable
business men. General-manager Conley, of
the S. M. Herald, is chairman; F. H. G.
Chambers (Hardie and Gorman) is trea-
surer ; and Arch Annan (Parsons Trading
Co.) hon. sec.
From the Defence Department:—
In connection with the work of the Munitions
Committee, a sub-committee has been appointed to
make initial inquiries regarding the possibilities
of producing toluene in Australia. Toluene is
obtained either by extraction from coal gas or by
distillation of coal tar, and is the necessary base
for the manufacture of high explosives. The mem-
bers of the sub-committee are Messrs. Marcus Bell and
P. C. Holmes Hunt, and Dr. P. H. Campbell; and the
Minister will be glad if cos., councils and others
that may be communicated with by the sub-com-
mittee will reply promptly to any inquiries put ta
them in order that data may be collected as soon as-
possible.
If Hughes is speaking without reserva-
tion when he says that the Commonwealth
Govt, does not contemplate conscription or
compulsory service, what earthly use is this
war census going to be, except to provide
work of great futility for a multitude of
officials? In any case, what is the point of
all this vast mass of hurried intelligence
about the value of Smith’s furniture, the
amount Jones pays for rent, the number of
camels in Robinson’s herd? All the broad
facts necessary for the Government’s pur-
poses when considering methods of taxation
can easily be obtained at 24 hours’ notice
from sources which are already at command'
—and, what is more, they will be obtained
in that way, since the piles of matter now
about to be dredged up and sorted out at
great expense will simply hide the few things
the Government really wants to know.
Last week in N.S.W. Assembly three
Labor members voted against a measure
introduced by the Minister for Education
relating to the acquisition of a certain home
for children. The point at issue was one
on which there was ample room for reason-
able difference of opinion, and the vote of
the Assembly went against the Minister.
The immediate consequence was a wild
escape of steam from party engines and
large headings in the daily papers and pon-
derous head-shaking about “splits” and
“discipline” and “a crisis.” Consideration!
of whether the Assembly had come to a wise
decision was, of course, a matter no one
took the slightest interest in. All of which
again shows what a fine democratic animal
Party Government is and how ludicrous it
is when mere representatives of the people
dare to get tired of being marionettes.

“Sunday,” revived for Muriel Starr at
Sydney Criterion last Saturday, belongs to
the shivery type of drama. At the middle
of each act the violins become tremulous,
and they keep on being tremulous till the
curtain falls. The story appeals equally to
the violin strings of the orchestra and the
heart-strings of the audience. Life in this
Wild West is idyllically Bret Hartian. The
Wild Westers may occasionally swear, but
otherwise they are good as gold. There are
no villains there, except one visiting Eng-
lishman, and even he is conveniently shot
before the end of the first act. For the rest
of the play it has to get along without a
villain, and in consequence it suffers from a
fatty-degeneration-of-the-heart interest. But
this deliberate appeal to the handkerchiefs of
the audience is welcome after a long course
of the crudities and artificialities of the
modern U.S. melodrama. Both types of play
are false: the tremolo type is the more
pleasingly false.
* * * *
Once again Muriel Starr surprises us.
The part of the youthful Sunday seems at
first sight quite beyond her range, yet she
contrives to portray the innocent youth and
careless vivacity of this young girl with a
charming naivetg. Muriel Starr has many
faults, but when one considers that her
characters comprise “Madame X” and a girl
young enough to be that person’s grand-
daughter, it is evident she possesses a varied
artistic equipment. Incidentally, her frock-
ing in the second and third acts is daringly
and delightfully up to the moment in fashion.
* %’j * *
Charles Millward’s mannerisms are lost in
the conventional character of an English
aristocrat. The stiffness and carefully-re-
hearsed gestures habitual to him are quite
in the picture he has to present. Sydney
Stirling, the one bad man of the play, makes
love _ with difficulty, but otherwise is a
sufficiently agreeable villain. As Jacky, Clar-
ence Blakiston gets every pathetic ounce out
of this patently theatrical part. It is a re-
lief to find Lincoln Plumer having a holiday
from the U.S. detective service; but he
makes a better policeman than he does a
Father Christmas. Maurice Dudley is more
at home. in his pathos as Davy; but George
Bryant is much too gloomy and melodramatic
a member of Sunday’s Sabbath-school of
good little boys. Dorothy Davies, Gertrude
Boswell and Boyd Irwin do their best with
colorless parts. Handkerchiefs at 10.30.
“MY MATE,” AT SYDNEY ADELPHI.
’Three humorists. Ethel Buckley as Bunny,
Frank Crossley who refused a colored kiss,
and the wallaby that nearly leapt into the
orchestra.
Edmund Duggan has had his eye to the
theatrical keyhole long enough to know
what the public wants ; and when he wrote
“My Mate” he delivered the goods. But no
one, not even Duggan himself, is likely to
say that “My Mate” will go down as an im-
perishable monument of what Australia was
like in the bad, bold days of 1915. On Satur-
day night at Sydney Adelphi the house
laughed—at times roared —and when a
house behaves like that it is satisfied. But
“My Mate” won’t satisfy everybody. By
this time the Adelphi will have received a
cargo of expostulatory Notes from China,
along with a four-column protest from A.
G. Huie against its damnable heresy of
Protection. Also, every abo. in Australia is
liable to threaten instant death to Miss
Ethel Buckley for her portrait of black
sister. As Bunny Miss Buckley is allegedly an
Australian half-caste; the other half seems
to be Yankee comic-paper nigger. Neverthe-
less, the house enjoyed Bunny with the rest.
Gunyah, another Australian native, is the
long-distance black tracker. On Saturday
night he crawled 376 miles, and no doubt is
still crawling. Walter Hunt and . Frank
Nielson provide excellent representations of
repentant curates who have gone on the
land, although the programme for some un-
known reason makes no mention of their
being curates. John Cosgrove as Jinks, a
heavyweight drunk, is another star per-
former —one, two, or three star: it is all
the same to Jinks. Add to these a wallaby,
a star, too, doing what he pleases and taking
no notice of what is laid down for him to
do. “My Mate” will continue at the Adelphi
until Gunyah has crawled himself to a
standstill, or Jerry Jerome has smitten
Ethel Buckley on the neck. It might even
suffer the horrible fate of seeing its two
Australian settlers rejoin the Church; or
the wallaby may become tired of Bunny and
leap over the footlights and murder the big
drum. There are heaps of other possibili-
ties, one of them being that “My Mate” may
be hurriedly withdrawn some time in De-
cember to make room for the usual Christ-
mas panto.
* * * *
“Charley’s Aunt” is not a play but an
institution. It will probably outlast the
war. And Sydney’s Little Theatre revival by
George Willoughby on Saturday evening
proves that it still possesses its draw-
ing power. Those who have seen it before
“MY MATE ,” AT SYDNEY ADELPHI.
Rear, side and front elevations of John
Cosgrove as Booser Juicy Jinks.
have a reminiscent smile for its absurdities ;
those who were too young to have seen it
before shriek with laughter. The produc-
tion drags when neither Tom Cannam, who
plays the part of the nice old lady, nor
George Willoughby, who is a pleasantly
absurd Uncle Spettigue, is upon the stage ;
but fortunately these occasions are rare.
Tom Cannam’s aunt is a direct descendant
of Penley’s relative; a few points are missed,
but most of the others are incisively made.
Nellie Calvin shows what a charming lady
the real Brazilian aunt is. Eirene de Grant,
a newcomer, has much to unlearn. As the
ingenue she shows charm, but her acting
would be much better if, for a few moments
at least, she would forget to act.
In the Assembly last night the Minister
for Public Entertainment was asked if it
were a fact (1) that the Royal Strollers
were offering a poor show at Sydney Palace
at exorbitant prices; (2) that, in conse-
quence, it was the intention of the Govern-
ment to borrow £6,000,000 at 51 per cent, to
improve the show. The Minister replied that
the hon. member had been grossly misin-
formed. The Strollers were providing a
charming entertainment at very reasonable
prices for admission. He was prepared to
put the matter of charges to the public
before the Unnecessary Commodities’ Com-
mission if the hon. member gave notice of
motion. It was true that the Government
intended to go on the market to raise
£6,000,000, but that was merely the usual
monthly application to meet interest and
pay for the construction of permanent and
reproductive monkey-houses and elephant-
temples at the new Zoo. It was a matter
of the most urgent public importance that
the construction of the monkey-houses and
elephant-temples should not be suspended
while the Empire was in danger.

“Who’s the Lady?” is the question before
the house at Sydney Royal this week, with
Beaumont Smith as chief instigator of the
conundrum. Ethel Dane, as Gobette, the
actress, still steers the play along the top of
the fence, and doesn’t once allow it to go
over. The identity of the minx will occupy
the attention of the clergy till the end of
next week. “Stop Your Nonsense” will
be then substituted.

* * * *
So far as the present Sydney season is
concerned, the last nights of Louise Mack’s
unconventional lectures upon the war in
Belgium are announced. The lady has
quite found her feet now, and the longer
the season runs the more obviously glad
this bright observer is that she started it.
$ * * *
This week’s comment on “Potash and
Perlmutter” at Melbourne Royal will go no
further than three interrogatives as to the
exact measure of the worth of Henry
Steuerman, the Pride of New York. Steuer-
man is the incidental philanthropist, imper-
sonated by Edwin Kelly, who meets P. and
P. by appointment after they have been
worshipping an unsuspected book-agent by
mistake. Steuerman, it seems, is the golden
calf who goes around in a shiny belltopper
seeking whom he may philanthropise, and
The Bulletin would like to know how he
came to be rolling in ducats, and what his
present financial business is, and how, in the
name of Holy Moses, he can be at once a
multi-millionaire and a paragon of all the
virtues. Evidently a lot of Jewish faith is
necessary to a satisfactory solving of the-
Steuerman problem, which simply staggers
a Gentile newspaper, and leaves it scratch-
ing its troubled head.
* * * *
At Melbourne Majesty’s a six-nights’
revival of “The Chocolate Soldier,” begin-
ning on Saturday, will end the musical-
comedy season pro tem. Muriel Starr comes
a week later and brings “Sunday.” To-
morrow (Friday) there is to be a matineg
performance in aid of sick and wounded
Australian soldiers, and the programme is
a guarantee that all who book seats will get
full value for their half-guineas.
* * * *
“The Girl Without a Home,” revived at
Melbourne Princess’s last Saturday night, is
an old. low-grade nightmare full of Costers
and Beer. Several of its unruly per-
sons belong to the underworld of pearl-
buttoned clobber and feathered ’ats,
while nearly every member of the
cast, barring the heroine and her sis-
ter, becomes an unsober victim of the mis-
spent “tray.” One female vagrant, who is
child-beater by appointment to the head vil-
lain, is so attached to the bottle that she
performs her office in a sort of gin-sodden
tango, with her countenance twisted into a
permanent gibber. In this elevating atmo-
sphere the poor heroine, Nell Maitland, is
doomed to express her pessimism in ragged
black, and wring her hands fitfully over the
tragedy of her lot. Seeing that Nell’s woes
include a blind sister, a ticket-of-leave father
and a convict lover, she has plenty of suffer-
ings to signal. During her semaphoring
exercise she comes into a fortune of

Department of the Navy,
Melbourne, 15th July, 1915.
Appointment of a Nursing Sister at the
Royal Australian Naval College, Jervis
Bay, New South Wales.
APPLICATIONS are invited from per-
sons qualified for appointment as
Nursing Sister at the Royal Australian
Naval College, Jervis Bay, N.S.W.
Salary will be at the rate of £lOO per
annum, with free quarters, rations, and
laundry.
Duties.
(a) In charge of all patients in the hos-
pital ;
(6) Performance of all dressings;
(cj In charge of all bedding, equipment,
etc., issued to her;
( d ) In addition to the wards, to be in
charge of the operating theatre and
casualty room, but not of any other
stores, or of the dispensary;
(e) To be responsible for the good order
and conduct of the wards ;
(/) To assist, at the discretion of the
Medical Officer, in the treatment of
the wives and children of officers,
petty officers and men attached to the
College, should occasion arise.
( g ) When not required to act in her
capacity as Nurse she will be required
to assist the Matron of the College,
and at needlework, as necessary.
Applications, stating age, and full parti-
culars of training and experience, accom-
panied by. copies of credentials, should be
forwarded to the Naval Secretary, Navy
Office, Melbourne, so as to react him not
later than 16th August, 1915.
GEO. L. MACANDIE,
Naval Secretary.

the villain’s happiness; but these trilling
circumstances fail to distract her from the
anguish in hand. As the much-tortured
female, Vera Remee has to mourn audibly lor
two hours and a half and dip her visage
freely in the flour bag to replenish a pallor
damaged by many tears. Undoubtedly the
pasty-faced moaner is a great success; but
the present writer, being a vegetarian in
the matter of heroines, prefers something
combining the lusty health of rhubarb with
the sweetness of the rose. A dubious lady
(Miss Hotson), who dresses like the missus
of a nigger potentate, relieves the heroine
occasionally with bits of tucker, and in one
particular burst of kindness acts as Nell s
buffer against the biffs of cruel and out-
rageous fortune. George Cross is the young
hero who goes into gaol like a tourist and
comes out like a tramp; and Ward Lyons is
Nell’s innocent lag of a father. Most of the
other characters are slum identities, spec-
tators of dog fights, and slouching graduates
from the Spielers’ College, whose clothes
merely illuminate the background of coster
life, as it were. All are excention-
ally thirsty, and the sight of them “indulg-
ing” after 9.30 p.m. on Saturday was most
unsettling to several members of Marlow’s
audience. Next Saturday “The Silence of
Dean Maitland” will be staged, with George
Cross in the part of the speechless sinner.

At Melbourne Bijou a restless character
named Zoli, with rings in his ears and no
bells in his voice, is endeavoring to exploit
the humor of the Dago nation. The Musical
Ibsons and Eleanor and Bertie, the tight-
wire specialists, are acceptable rearrivals;
and the two Franklins are dovetailing their
patter in an act that refuses to conceal its
Yankee identity. As for the Bert Le Blanc
people, they have become solemn roysterers
in a familiar arrangement of legs and colored
lights entitled “At the San Francisco Expo-
sition.” The cast includes Carlton Chase,
Carrie Moore, the New York Comedy Four,
and others; but Israel, represented by Le
Blanc and another Hebrew, mostly holds the
floor. In fact, Israel gets so eloquent at
times that it almost succeeds in holding the
audience.

* * * *
“On Our Selection” will flourish as before
at Melbourne King’s until further notice.
The Steele Rudd play has passed through
the controversial stage, and Dad, Dave, Dan,
Sarah and the rest are characters that bring
their reputations along and run. the secret
of success on co-operative principles. The
Bert Bailey Co. has 15 weeks of work cut
out for it at the King’s, and “Our Selec-
tion” should account for a fat slice of the
season.
#* * *
The new faces at the Melbourne Tivoli
last Saturday were those of A 1 Ripon, ven-
triloquist, and his dummy, both of whom
talk with a powerful Scawtch accent and
are 1 most saddening when they sing. Ripon
is an acceptable duologue merchant of the
old school, but his two voices are uncom-
monly alike, and his occasional smacking of
the dummy’s hpad has no points of novelty
about it. The Randell Jackson concertists
were to finish on Tuesday this week. Clemons
and Dean Galvin, Stone, Marie Terry and
the wearisome monkeys are still there ; also
the jubilant Fields, who states, with un-
conscious humor, that he becomes more
amusing as he proceeds. If he went on for
a whole year he might grow absolutely
funny.

Annette Kellerman continues to figure
weekly on the film at the Auditorium.
Talking of films, 50 of Melbourne’s
picture theatres are to do their bit next
Sunday on behalf of the Australia Day
Fund, and, for this occasion only, the Sab-
batarians are expected to swallow their
scruples and pass up a shilling for admission.

The orchestral concert at Melbourne
Town Hall next Saturday afternoon (Aug-
ust 1) will be in aid of the wounded. Laver
conducts an orchestra of 100. who will open
the concert with Beethoven’s Funeral March
in memory of their departed friend Mar-
shall Hall.
** * *
With the blessing of The Bulletin’s
Melbourne critic: —
Mr. and Mrs. J. A. Davidson, of Oakleigh (Melb.),
have added a theatre-room to their home, and hope
to “stimulate the growth of a National Drama,
which shall deal with all those themes and aims—
romantic, religious, social, moral, political—that
affect the lives of Australian people.” The times
may seem difficult for the project, but the experi-
menters argue that, “since no nation ever did great
things that had not faced great tragedies, so no
person ever created great work whose soul had not
been wed to tragedy. And who shall say what songs,
what music or what art will not awaken in a nation
at present facing its first dark Gethsemanean hour?”
The little playhouse at wayout suburban Oakleigh,
where new Australian dramatists are to go through the
furnace of criticism, is just big enough to seat 100
persons, and its stage is “slightly larger than that
upon which Moliere presented his comedies.” Free
admission is offered as a set-off to the cost of time
and railway fare. But the cash necessary for nour-
ishing the proposed enterprise? It is to come from
the enthusiasts, the patrons, in the form of “gratui-
ties ranging from half a guinea per annum,” which
will be “supplemented surely by a small grant from
the nation.” Given sufficient funds, the promoters,
guided by “a Board of Readers,” will pay prizes to
the chosen dramatists and “fees for all plays pro-
duced.” The prospects of successfully rocking
National Drama in a cradle at Oakleigh are not bril-
liant ; still, the outlook for many another presump-
tuous artistic venture has been not more promising.
The Davidsons, anyhow, should get the sympathy of
the most possible Australian play-makers, as well as
the embarrassing attention of the wildly impossible.
They hope to have a house-warming production early
in August—a four-act play hy George Byfield, deal-
ing with “the segregation of the unfit.” The address
of the little theatre is Niagaroom, Warrigal-road,
Oakleigh, where any good Australian is welcome to
inquire further into the whole matter.
* * * %
Adelaide shows are almost unanimously
warlike. At the Tivoli Brandon Cremer’s
“For King and Country” goes with a bang,
and gets so far ahead of French and Joffre
that it includes the entrance of the vic-
torious Allies into Berlin. It is now mak-
ing way for a Turk drama, “Secrets of the
Harem,” which the management booms on
the strength of “risque revelations.” The
Royal has finished with the jolly, impro-
bable Stay-at-Home Man. It has also held
a crammed house at fancy prices for patri-
otic purposes, with a stirring speech by
Lady Galway and a reckless auction sale
to supplement a big mixed programme. At
West’s the “Hero of the Dardanelles,” well
faked on the N.S.W. coast, shows neverthe-
less too many villas. That shore of Turkey
is almost unrelieved barrenness. The same
film, given at short intervals all day at the
Pav., is rushed. The Central introduces a
baby-competition on the screen. At the
King’s, now that the hard-working and mod-
erately humorous Bletsoes have gone, the
most striking thing is the return of Jennie
Opie’s fine contralto to the stage, after a
long lapse of years—of course in a recruit-
ing song. The Steele-Paynes have left the
Town Hall, much regretted.
Clever Willie Holman commandeered the
wheat of N. S. Wales and let Queensland
have a little at £6 per ton more than it cost
him. Now the Ryan Government seizes
Queensland. butter, and lets N. S. Wales
have what it can’t dispose of in Queensland
at 245. per cwt. more than it cost in the
State where it was made. What Big Aus-
tralians they all are!
AT POVERTY POINT.
“POTASH AND PERLMUTTER,” AT
MELBOURNE ROYAL.
Paul Burns as Abe Potash.
Now it is William Laver’s turn. Though
he hasn’t held the Ormond Chair of Music
at Melbourne University, he has acted as
director of the Conservatorium when, on
various occasions, a professor was not avail-
able. He is a fine, large person of com-
manding aspect, who has turned out many
highly-qualified artists; he can teach till the
pupil wilts from fatigue, and then go on
with the next one; he is a conductor and
lecturer of repute, and has proved a business
success when he has “run” the Conserva-
torium pro tem. At length the time seems
ripe to give the Australian a chance to fill
the shoes now empty through Marshall
Hall’s death.
“0.K.”: A Poverty Pointer speaks of
Fred Cowen as as- English conductor who
was “brought to shake a stick among better
men in Australia.” Older men, but hardly
better. The young Hebraic Cowen (he was
understood to have had Cohen for an ances-
tor) had a reputation then, and has a larger
one now. And let’s remember this to his
credit: he didn’t kick the Australian ladder
from his patent-leather shoes when he climbed
up among the minor composers. Also he
spoke of the splendid education he got during
his six months with a fine orchestra on great
musical works at Melbourne Exhibition;
and he never complained that the perform-
ances were not generally patronised.
Manager Courtneidge, at London Shaftes-
bury, started a new departure by engaging
a call-girl to replace the call-boy who had
enlisted. The novelty has come to stay, no
doubt. Why should an able-bodied man
devote his life to the business of rounding-
up mummers to take their cues?
“J.F.H.” (from London) : Actor-manager
Arthur Bourchier, of London Garrick, an-
nounces that one of the plays he has in view
is “a new Australian piece by Mr. Adams,
a well-known Melbourne joux-nalist—his first
stage effort and full of Antipodean atmo-
sphere.” It is entitled “Mrs. Pretty and
the Premier,” and, according to Bourchier, is
of more consequence than any Australian
production “since Haddon Chambers came
along nearly 30 years ago with ‘Captain
Swift.’ ”
Poet Stephen Phillips’s ambitious war play,
“Armageddon,” at the New Theatre, London,
was such a hopeless failure that actor-man-
ager Martin Harvey had to take it off after a
run of a week or so. He replaced it with the
good old “Corsican Brothers.”
“E.T.”: “Sunday,” revived at Sydney Cri-
terion, was originally produced in England
12 years ago. It made a lot of money there
as here, but the world has heard nothing of
the author, Thomas Raceward, since. And
he is still alive —-all three of him. Which
being interpreted means that “Thomas Race-
ward” was an amalgam compounded of the
Christian names of three mummers who
started play-writing about the time they
were in Australia with the first Wilson Bar-
rett combination. “Thomas” stands for T.
W. Percyval; “Race” for Ho-race Hodges;
“Ward” for Ed-ward Irwin. The last-named
fell out with, and so out of, the firm. There-
upon the two remaining members set to work
and scored again with another piece bearing
a similar six-lettered, single-word title. This
play, “Grumpy,” which luckily for all con-
cerned fell into the hands of Cyril Maude,
will probably reach these shores before very
long, as Maude’s idea is to tour the world
with his money-spinner, just as Joe Jeffer-
son did with “Rip,” and Jennie Lee with
“Jo.”
From London: The Times critic says of
Marie Lohr, in Knoblauch’s new war-play,
“Marie Odile,” that she was “wonderfully
sweet” as the impossibly-innocent novice;
the Daily Telegraph declares she “made
an adorable figure in her novice’s habit, and
was so natural, so sincere, that you could
not resist her appeal”; the Morning Post
alleges that the Australian girl “has never
looked more entrancing than in the white
veil and brown habit of the novice”; the
Standard says she was “quite splendid,
made a wonderful figure of the sweet but
ignorant girl, and held us enraptured all
the time”; the Daily News thinks Marie
“has never done anything half so g00d....
a veritable triumph”; while the Westmin-
ster Gazette eulogises the “great personal
charm and quite exquisite simplicity that
carried her successfully through a very dif-
ficult role.” Queenslander William Archer,
dramatic critic of the Star, is patriotically
complimentary to a charming sister-Austra-
lian and says nice things about the “sin-
cerity and beauty of Miss Marie Lohr’s
acting.” But he candidly confesses that he
did not like the play: “I sat on pins and
needles, and would have given any reasonable
sum to be free to leave the theatre.” At
the same time he acknowledged that “the
workmanship of the play is good, and its
moral —the sin of confounding innocence
with ignorance—is excellent.” The discord
is supplied by the Daily Chronicle, which
simply declines to regard the piece seriously.
“S.S.”: Somebody complains in a Mel-
bourne paper that actors are notoriously
backward in coming forward at recruiting.
It’s just as well; about 90 per cent, of
them have defective feet. It rarely hap-
pens that an actor who has been long in the
game has a sole to call his own.
“S” : Reading the Argus theatrical criti-
cisms I am forcibly impressed with the idea
that that powerful organ should rearrange
the letters of its name. Sugar would be
much more appropriate.

Monsignor Byrne, dead in Adelaide at 81,
was one of a remarkable batch of three
young priests who came from Ireland to
Westralia in 1855. Bourke stayed there,
and became Vicar-General. Reynolds and
Byrne moved on to South Aus., where the
former in time became Archbishop. Byrne
became Vicar-General. Perhaps he would
have been Archbishop at Reynolds’s death
but that the diocese seemed to need the
financial genius which it found in O’Reily.
Anyway, he “administered” the diocese at
various times, and was its most distin-
guished priest, barring the archbishop.
Before another chance came he was too old
to take it. He had a “golden jubilee” in
1910, and latterly has lived in retirement.
He survived Archbishop O’Reily only a
month.

The stars are getting scarce; the giant of the
forest is laid low; the midpost of the tribal house
has fallen; the ridge pole has snapped asunder; the
house leaks and the shivering orphans of Tu are left
disconsolate. The canoe of fate (Karamu-Rauriki),
fashioned out of the tree of sorrow and tears in
mythologic Hawaiki, has visited the home of my
friend, as it must visit the home of every man, and
has borne him to those mysterious realms of night,
whose gates open but the one way, from which the
canoe ever returns empty:
THE IDENTIFICATION OF EUSTACE PUGH*
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At Candy’s boarding-house in town
Lives Pugh, a seedy gentleman,
With figure sadly broken down.
He borrows sprats from all the clan,
And earns his weekly quid, I hear,
As stoker, cook and overseer
On Buffet’s baked-potato can.
We wondered what the man might be.
His manners were extremely fine,
His “touch” was delicate, and he
Had uppish tastes—his drink was wine.
We knew that better days he’d seen—
Biggs swore he was an earl, and Green
Had met him as a swell divine.
We’d eggs to-day. We opened two,
And when the pungent odor spread,
We cursed the waiting dame. But Pugh
Burst into tears, and bowed his head.
“Excuse me,” said he, “that bouquet
Reminds me of my better day!”
“Betrayed—betrayed ! ” was all we said.

that has so frequently and invariably been

posee, i dram Menthol Crystals and 3oz.
Bay Rum has caused such widespread in-
terest and comment that many chemists
now keep it put up complete and ready for
use under the name of Lavona Hair Tonic.
Its uniform success in destroying dandruff,
stopping falling hair, promoting hair growth
and making thin, dry, brittle hair thick,
soft, glossy and luxuriant, lias been so
astonishing that the manufacturers of
Lavona Hair Tonic, in order to show their
unlimited confidence in its merits, have
arranged with leading chemists everywhere
to sell it under an absolute guarantee of
satisfaction or money back, signed by the
chemist from whom you purchase it. No
claim is made that Lavona Hair Tonic is
any better than that which any chemist
can prepare from the above formula, but if
you wish to convince yourself of its truly
remarkable merits without risking the ''oss
of a penny, go to any good chemist to- lay
and ask for a bottle of Lavona Hair Tonic,
“the kind they guarantee.”*

“Jaques”: Let’s be logical. When a man
wants to get married he hasn’t to go to court
and satisfy the Judge that he is entitled to
do it. Then why should he have to go to a
court to get unmarried? There ought to be
a ceremony, similar to marriage, which would
legally separate a couple without costs, etc.

“Society” pages would come out with reports

UNMARRIAGES.
St. Smitheram’s Church was the scene of a pretty
but quiet umvedding on Wednesday afternon, solem-
nised by the Rev. O. U. Wowser, when Henry James
Jones, eldest son of Captain J. J. Jones, late of the
R.M.S. Mary Ann, was unmarried from Gwendolyn
Sophia Smith, second daughter of Mr. and Mrs. J. P.
Smith, of Potts Gully. The bride, who was taken
back by her father, wore a gown of black satin
designed with a long tunic of net shirred at the
waist, a soft corsage of net and silk lace with pearls,
long ruched sleeves, the whole being finished with a
broad soft satin belt. Mr. O. U. Later (co-re.) acted
as worst man. A reception was held at Belinda Hall,
and later the bride left with the worst man for the
South.
SAFETY.
SHE: “There’s father! For goodness sake, don’t let him see me!”
HE: “ You’re all right. Just stay behind me.”
“D.” : No wonder there are 429 applicants
for the 30 vacancies at the Australian Naval
College at Jervis Bay. Apart from the fas-
cination of the future that lies ahead of the
cadet, the college itself is a far more luxuri-
ous place than the most fashionable of Eng-
lish public schools. Dormitories and studies
are the last word in modernity and com-
fort ; there is a gymnasium with every device
to enthrall the heart of youth; and then
there is the bay. There can be nothing
lovelier than Jervis Bay in fine weather,
viewed from the “quarter-deck” of the col-
lege. The only sign of civilisation is the
distant lighthouse on the North Head. All
else is amethyst blue water, shining white
beach, and sage-green bush. On the waters
of the bay the boys learn to swim, surf, sail
boats and manoeuvre steam launches. Ashore,
picked naval officers teach them the rudi-
ments of every branch of their profession,
and the discipline, ethics and conventions
which the British navy has taken about 900
years to amass. The cadets are as carefully
selected as their instructors. Each of them
has to be a sort of physical, moral and men-
tal super-boy. As recently as two vears ago
Australian parents were sending their young-
sters to the other side of the earth to enter
the British navy. The idea was that the
Australian navy was a fleeting folly that
would die with the downfall of the Labor
Government, and that, in any case, no Naval
or Military College of worth could be insti-
tuted in Australia. Since then the Aus-
tralian navy has won fame, and the local
Service Colleges have become almost em-
barrassingly popular. Never in history has
a great idea been justified so quickly.
Some people never know their luck ! In
1913 six Australian couples whose wedding-
day was back five years in the last century
had to face for the first time the music of a
squalling infant by night; three couples,
who had got into the settled ways induced
by 21 years of childless bliss, had to recast
their dull conceptions of matrimony; four
other couples, married for 22 years, hur-
riedly bought cradles and layouts ; and one
couple, after 2.4 years ! But the record was
attained by the lady who presented her
astounded Father Abraham with a first baby
on or about the “silver” anniversary of
their wedding 17

From Constable X 651: —
Dennis’s verse-stories of “The Sentimental Bloke”
and “The Sentimental Bloke’s” lovely description of
“Ginger” and “Ginger’s Cobber” at Gallipoli remind
me that of the Emu “push,” an offshoot of the once
notorious “Boveroos,” of Carlton, no less than 70
members are on the way to Constantinople; and
South Melbourne's “Flying Angels” have done not
too badly either. Who wrote of the frequent capers
of the push that they were “chivalry upside-down” ?
He wasn’t far wrong, anyway.

The Confession of “10-2-4” :
There is no one who has a greater capacity to
make me feel a mean and paltry cur, not to say a
pusillanimous worm, than my old friend, Eustace Boost.
He is one of our soldiers of alcohol; a drinker sans
peur et sans reproche; a very Paladin of Booze. I
am one of those miserable creatures to whom drink-
ing is not a rite. The ceremony has no significance
for me. I imbibe because I am thirsty, and when
the thirst has gone I stop. Sometimes I feci like
a weak lager shandy. It is a dreadful experi-
ence to enter a bar, and order a shandy (or, even
worse, a straight soda), and find that Boost is there
and has heard. He radiates on such occasions the
atmosphere of Kitchener or General French in the
presence of an officer whose nerve has failed him. i
long to summon the barmaid with an expansive ges-
ture, arid cry “Port and brandy mixed, old dear, and
top it up with a small stout.” It would make Boost
revere, if not love me, I know; but it would also
make me sick, so I refrain. There is something
extraordinarily fearless about Eustace Boost’s man-
ner in, a bar. I never see him without thinking of
Sari Bair and Hill 60 and Neuve Chapelle. Each
“go” of whisky, each flagon of beer, is a fresh posi-
tion gained in face of dreadful odds. The weaklings
and the cowards lag behind drinking nothing, or,
still more degrading, “filling up on soft stuff.”
During the opening shots of the engagement it takes
a nor.-alcoholist with a pronounced “temperament” to
sense that he is regarded on the field of Soak as a
poltroon and an outcast. But as the action warms
up he is left in no doubt. “Fill ’em up again,
boy s ,” cries Boost, after the manner of Wellington
ordering the line to advance. Then, in a very daring
manner to the barmaid, “The old usual for me, my
dear. Yes, all whiskies, except our friend here.
Another lemonade for him.” (Shouts of laughter, in
which the lady sycophant at the pump joins.)
I have often sought for the origin of the pride
which the drinker takes in his work, and the con-
tempt which he obviously feels for the abstainer and
the partial abstainer. It is so universal that there
must be some justification for it. Possibly the bar-
haunter argues that it is as brave to risk your
digestion as any other part of your anatomy. Our
compulsorily teetotal expeditionaries dash through
a rain of lead at the Turkish trenches. Boost and
his unsober compeers dash a rain of whisky at their
stomachs. Both take aw;ul risks, the soldiers of
death and mutilation ; Bcost and his friends of death
and a ruined waist-line. When the millennium arrives
and wars cease for ever the State will perhaps give a
D.S.O. to every proved case of delirium tremens, and
V.C.’s to those who qualify for committal to an
inebriate hom'e.
STEAL A GLANCE AND BE GLAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A little hat cocked like a yacht in the wind ;
Eyes like wet violets ; more violets pinned
To the beautiful furs some one bought;
A short veil to the tip of an aquiline nose,
And a skin like new milk ; in each cheek a pink rose ;
And a coil of black hair deftly caught.
Her hat sits with joy on her dear little head ;
The flowers all weep that they’ll soon be too dead
To be carried about on her coat.
But the happiest thing is the narrow black band,
Which is rivalled alone by the rings on her hand,
For it clings to her beautiful throat.
Steal a look and pretend that you didn't. Don’t shy !
She is watching you out of the tail of her eye!
Take another glance quickly ! Don’t stare !
Yes, she dressed herself up to be looked at, old beau,
Ar.d, when next the train stops, she may get up
and go.
Steal a glance—and rejoice she is there!
N.S.W.

An astonished eaotain at Gallipoli got a
lesson in caste the other day. He had put
50 picked men on to a digging job. A lieu-
tenant told him that another gang would be
required for trucking. The captain pro-
tested that these were not necessary—the
diggers could do the whole job. “But I tell
you they are Bendigo miners,” the lieu-
tenant explained. It took a lot more argu-
ment than that to convince the captain that
the Bendigonians would rather die than
“truck their own muck” ; but he gave way in
the end.
The sordid old Government House row
still proceeds in the N. S. Wales Cabinet.
Holman, Ashford, Estell and Grahame are
the only members who haven’t been nobbled
in the Strickland interest. There is not a
reason—other than that of satisfying the
vanity of an imported figurehead, to whose
office Labor is supposed to be hostile- —for
diverting the Macquarie-street mansion from
the use to which Holman engaged originally
to put it. The arguments to the contrary
are numerous. One may assume, presum-
ably, that Labor means sometime to carry
out the plank of its platform which in-
sists on the abolition of the office of State
Governor. The translation of the potentate
to Macquarie-street will make it more diffi-
cult. if not impossible. Cranbrook is in the
middle of a popular residential suburb. If
the Governor left it to-morrow, it
could be sold at a profit the next day. It
will always be in that position. But no such
use will ever be made of the grounds sur-
rounding the other edifice. Cranbrook is the
sort of modest mansion which is readily sale-
able to a rich man. It might be turned into
a hash-house, a private hospital, or an hotel.
Old Federal Government House can never be
anything but a public institution. One may
take it, therefore, that if the State Governor
BETTER THAN NOTHING.
BEAUTY: “Do you think I should put a
little more red on?”
BEAST: “If you aren’t putting on anything
else I would.”
goes to the place which Holman said
was to be used as a museum or hospital, he
will go there for good. Labor will have the
credit of having pushed one Governor out on
a false pretence, and pushed another in,
over the ruins of its platform, for the price
of a vice-regal smile and a few free meals.

“D. Dotty” : Never mind about the war.
There is too much talk about the war and
too little about the things that matter. Take
my two schemes, for instance. At the pre-
sent moment I am appealing for subscrip-
tions to the Association for Dusting Tram-
guards. Every time I go out my fare is
collected by a clambering
scarecrow with dust all
over him and unscrubbed
hands. Sometimes he
even has chaff in his hair.
It is very painful to me
to have dealings with
these apparitions, and I
will not rest till every
tram terminus is equipped
with a cleaning depot and
a staff which will dust
every guard before he
goes out again. Then
there is my Rotten Hats
Suppression Society. The
place is full of people
who are carrying absurd
hats on their heads. All
that is wanted is a staff
of inspectors armed with
the necessary authority,
long wire tongs and port-
able furnaces mounted
on the side-cars of motor-
cycles. They will scout
along the streets, and,
immediately on catching
sight of a rotten hat, will
intercept the wearer, and,
pointing the tongs
straight at the offending
headgear, say in a loud
voice, “Wrong !” The hat
will then be delivered or,
if necessary, taken by
force from the offender
and cremated on the spot.
There will be no appeal
from an inspector’s deci-
sion ; but, in order to
avoid a possible repetition of the offence, the
inspector will accompany the patient to the
nearest hatter’s and see that he is hatted
fittingly at his own expense. The system
might later on be applied to other wearing
apparel; but we can try it first on the hats.
The Law in N. S. Wales has recently-
been busy with the mean frauds who make
a dishonest pound or so out of the despera-
tion of people badly enough in want of em-
ployment to pay for it. After letting the
heartless business go on until it reached the
dimensions of a scandal, the Law launched
a series of prosecutions, and secured a
batch of heavy sentences for deserving
swindlers, the sentences being usually garn-
ished with remarks by the Judge. But the
Law will have to keep an alarm clock in
the bedroom if it wants to hold this noxious
trade down properly. An advertisement re-
lating to an alluring job which requires no
special qualifications except ability to part
up a small premium costs a shilling, and in
times when work is scanty it seldom fails to
draw its simple-minded victims. Appar-
ently it is drawing the usual number of vic-
tims at present. Anyhow, it is quite as
common as it was before the Law spoke.
The bones of the strike that shook Maori-
land up in 1913 are still kicking about. The
other day a petition fell into the M.L. House
of Reps, praying for cash for three people
who had suffered loss by helping the Minis-
terial rouglmders to bash the strikers. One
man wanted £2O for a horse he had lent, and
concerning which everybody had since been
deaf and dumb. Two others asked for £6 10s.
and £4 10s. for saddles and bridles they had
handed out and never seen again. The
trouble is that everyone promised to do all
kinds of things while the bashing was in pro-
gress, but no one could afterwards be found
to accept any responsibility for money spent
or things lent. When the last riot had been
quelled everybody rushed back to business
and left ttie mess to posterity, while lone
cockies wandered about the landscape looking-
for compensation.
* * * *
The women’s meeting at the Melbourne
Town Hall was reported by Age and Argus
as to the utterances of the bright lights of
the A.W.N.L. and the eloquence of Mrs.
Deakin, Mayoress Ilennessy also found half
a column. But the best situation of the
meeting got two lines in the Age and nothing
at all in the Argus. The silvertail ladies invited
two Trades Hall women —Mrs. Felsted, who
talks without notes and like a seasoned poli-
tician, and Mrs. Laurie Cohen. The
A.W.N.L.’s slogan was “For God and Coun-
try.” and Mrs. Felsted was asked to for-
mally second a motion, and told what to
say. But instead of saying it Mrs. Fel-
sted made a human speech to the hall full
of knitting women ; and when she remarked
that she belonged to the Labor party which
had saved Australia by preparing for war,
a couple of thousand knitting needles stopped
while the A.W.N.L. gasped. By some over-
sight there was no guillotine.
* * * *
An interesting souvenir from Gallipoli was
forwarded to this paper by Lieut. ,T. New-
man, of the heroic Third Brigade, A.I.F. It
is a muddied copy of “The Bulletin Story
Book,” with a shraonel bullet embedded in
its pages. It is probably unique among war
souvenirs, in that the owner makes no claim
that it saved his life. The battered volume
from the trenches was raffled in the office for
the Australia Day fund, and £3 was raised.
The man who won the prize intends handing
it over to the fund, to be re-sold by auction.
From a man in England who is getting
angry
While the War Office and the British press are
shrieking for munitions, ambulances, transports and
the like the Australian Divisional Ammunition Park
and Motor Transport section of the Expeditionary
Force are rusting in Hampshire. They are being
used, not as soldiers, but as navvies. They were
employed carting gravel for some time, and have
even been ordered to make roads, an invitation which
they cordially refused. Those men—and they are not
a few—who gave their own cars to the corps have
had the dubious satisfaction of seeing them practi-
cally ruined over corporation jobs. As a result, the
great Australian slanguage is being spoken with some
vigor in Hants these days. The men naturally feel
the Commonwealth Govt. Isn’t paying them a big
daily wage to help tidy up Britain, and clamor that
they should be given a chance to justify their existence
as a corps, to say nothing of avenging their pals who
have fallen in the Dardanelles. We don’t want to
say unkind things about the tens of thousands in
England who wouldn’t go to the Front unless they
were kicked; but at least those who have come
12,000 miles expressly to do so might be allowed to
get there.

“W.A.Jt.”: My suggestion (B. 8/7/’l5)
that it was time Australia woke up to the
fact that this war can be won only by the
machine gun, and not by that archaic hand
weapon, the rifle, has been answered—by
Canada. The Canadian army has not only
discovered the necessity of a plentiful supply
of machine guns, but it is feverishly manu-
facturing them in hundreds, and every
Canadian unit that goes to the Front will
carry many more of these weapons than are
mentioned as necessary in the British “In-
fantry Manual.” Incidentally, it may be
stated that there are in use in the British
army five tynes of machine guns. The addi
tion of the Caldwell invention to the Aus-
tralian forces will, therefore, not violate
British army traditions or break any red-
tape.
* * * * *
The wowsers of Christchurch have been
regularly having hysterics ever since the
Maoriland Exhibition closed in 1907 and left
behind in Hagley Park to mark the site of
the Maori pa a nude figure carved on top of
a pole by a long-dead Maori artist. Like most
antique art of its kind the figure is power-
fully realistic; nevertheless, when it was de-
cided to leave it as an historic mark, even
the local curates thought the idea good. But
the lone sentinel, standing with awful pro-
minence on a rise in a much-frequented part
of the park, got on the nerves of the wow-
sers on moonlit nights, and every now and
again an agitation burst out to have him put
in an overcoat. At last the howls of McSnuffle
became so persistent that the Domain Board,
fearing a riot, dug the post up the other day
and carried it into the adjoining museum.

“Billy T.”: We’d been to a suburban
party, and were returning with a mixed
company at about two o’ the morn, when we
came upon a house behind a high hedge, and
bedded in a large garden, from which unusual
quantities of smoke were issuing. While we
were debating whether it was or was not a
case of house afire, a tali gentleman in even-
ing-dress, trailing an overcoat after him,
came swinging through the gate, closed it
behind him, leaned against it, and eyed us all
with great amiability. “ ’Sh all ri’,” lie said.
“ ’Sh qui’ all ri’. I was passliin’ goin’ ’ome
from thearer, when I notished smoke. ‘Housh
afire,’ I says, ‘mus’ let ’em know.’ So, it’sli
qui’ all ri’. I’ve put a note tinner the door.”
CUI BONO?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
‘‘Hill 60 has practically disappeared. The guns
have blown it away.”
We blasted, we strived and we fought,
While dead men poisoned the air;
With shell and with mine we wrought
In the searchlight’s ghostly glare;
And the tortured Hill leapt up and fell
To the rending tune of our powder-hell.
We mined, we bombed and we sapped;
We grappled and stabbed and yelled,
While the chatt’ring Maxims snapped,
And the great guns’ chorus swelled.
Week after week, in our wild despair,
We died for the Hill. Now no Hill is there!
N.S.W.

TO CURE COUGHS USE PINE TAR
AND SUGAR.
A SPECIALIST’S SUGGESTION.
Obstinate, tickling coughs, the kind that
racks you to pieces and keeps you awake at
night, can be instantly relieved and quickly
cured by 10 or 15 drops of bitrate of tar on
a lump of sugar, and letting it slowly dis-
solve in the mouth. The specialist who
recommends this plan says it is superior to
anything he has ever tried, and may be used
with perfect safety and success on children
three or four years of age. To make an
excellent inexpensive cough syrup which
children like, he says there is nothing better
at any price than half a pound of granu-
lated sugar dissolved in half a pint of hot
water, and then stir in 2oz. of bitrate of
tar. When cool pour into a pint bottle, and
it is ready for use. From half a teaspoonful
to a teaspoonful every hour or two will
quickly relieve coughs and colds, and if
regularly used for a few days will give
remarkable benefit in cases of catarrh,
asthma and bronchial affections.*

THE ULTIMATUM.
WOODROW: “Impudent and offensive dog, I
am too proud to throw stones at you
myself, but I shall find the stones for
those who are not too proud.”
J. M. Arnott, Lieutenant-Colonel iu the
Light Horse, who has been wounded in the
Dardanelles, is one of the “Biscuit” Aruotts.
He is a man of fine physical proportions and
a splendid horseman, and was a prominent
member of the old Sydney Lancers. As a
sideline he can play a fine game of chess.
One of the Sultan’s worries at present is
Oft. Bin. long. Its name is Private William
Buckley, and it comes from Gulgong
(N.S.W.). Little Willie, standing on tip-
toe, can just see Constantinople. If he ever
gets to France the Germans are certain to
mistake him for a cathedral spire, which will
be very bad for Willie.
Attorney-General Hughes, who is tilting
at the big metal-mining cos. and Mount
Morgan in particular,- feels very sore. The
fact is R. G. Casey, the chairman of Mount
Morgan, put Billy Hughes into a hole, and
Billy had to cave in. That was pretty early
in the war. The Government had stopped
the co. from using its old channels to mar-
ket. Then Casey saw Hughes, and told him
that he had an offer for a big parcel of cop-
per from an allegedly-British firm. Would he
give permission to ship? Hughes said he
didn’t like the crowd. “I don’t care whether
you like the crowd or not,” said Casey. “If
we can’t sell the copper the whole mine
must close down. Tell us we can place this
copper and the mine remains open. Tell us
we can’t, and the men must stand off.”
Hughes gave the consent; but he hasn’t for-
given the people who squeezed it out of him.
HEATED CORRESPONDENCE.
JEALOUS HUSBAND: “I’m using bread for
bait, so if I land a bit of toast I’ll know
that Brungle’s bin writing to my wife
again.”
Are there any triplets in this war? There
are heroic twins, certainly. The Grenfells,
both of them captains in the 9th Lancers,
arrived at one birth. One brother was killed
in action in Flanders last September. The
other, Captain Francis Grenfell, who won
the Victoria Cross in August, went to join
his brother seven months later.
Sandford Fleming, just deceased at Hali-
fax, Nova Scotia, at the age of 88, was an
empire builder. He constructed a large slice
of the Canadian Pacific railway, and for
some years was engineer-in-chief of the
whole transcontinental outfit. He w T as one
of the pioneers of the Pacific Cable, or “All
Red Route,” and as such has a claim on the
kindly remembrance of Australian people.
W. C. Yahland has just passed away at
87, in Bendigo, where he had lived since
1853. Vahland, though a German, was an
old-fashioned one, kindly, simple, good
natured, and at his profession one of the
best. He designed the Bendigo Town Hall,
Hospital, Benevolent Asylum, School of
Mines, and Masonic Hall. Amongst the
pioneer diggers Vahland put down his first
pot-hole as a contemporary of J. B. Wat-
son, Major R. Moorhead, Barnet Lazarus,
Captain Standish (afterwards Chief Com-
missioner of Police), J. A. Panton and
Lachlan McLachlan (two notable police
magistrates), Angus Mackay (of the Ben-
digo Advertiser) and W. D. C. Denovan —-
a rare galaxy of celebrities.
Charles Bright, one of the Victorian ori-
ginals of the Gibbs Bright firm, died in Eng-
land last week. He came to Melbourne 92
years ago, was the first chairman of. the
local Harbor Trust —then a live and re-
spected body—and continued in the very
front rank of the city’s business affairs until
-his return to London in the early ’Bo’s.
Having married a daughter of the reigning
Governor when he was a 40-year-old “nut,”
Charley Bright was for many years the un-
crowned king of Sassiety. He was 87 when
he died —a, reminder that his brother Reggie,
still of the Gibbs Bright firm, is hardly as
young a bachelor as he used to be.
Ben Hoare, for so long the Age’s chief
leader-writer, passed into his 74th year last
week. Coming from Bucks in 1856, Ben
was apprenticed in 1858 to the Portland
Chronicle, and wrote articles and rhyme
for that paper and Hamilton Spectator till
1866. Later he edited the Gawler Bunyip
(S.A.) till IS7O. There he published a
volume of “Figures of Fancy.” Returning
to Victoria in 1871 he established the Gee-
long Times, and edited it until ISB6, when
he joined the editorial staff of the pious
Daily Telegraph, and was a leader-writer
till 1890. Then David Syme collared him
for the Age, and he continued to forge
most of the paper’s loudest political thunder
till May of last year, when he retired.
Five daughters and two sons, 5o grand-
children, 122 descendants of the third gene-
ration and 25 of the fourth came from the
marriage of Anne Potter, who died at \\ el-
lington (M.L.) lately. She landed m Port
Nicholson away back in ’l9 when \\ ellmg-
ton was mostly wind and scrub and brown
brother, and the rigors of colonisation played
such havoc with her constitution that she
lived to be only 97.
The cable brings news of the death in Eng-
land of Henry Edward Gurner, l ie. County
Court Judge, who was son of Henry luekl
Gurner, for many years Crown Solicitor of
Victoria. The latter was a Sydney native,
his father coming to the colony in early
days with Henry Barron Field, _ a Judge,
after whom the \ ic. Crown Solicitor was
named. When a Supreme Court was estab-
lished at Port Phillip, in 1842. Mr. Gurner
went there as Clerk under Mr. Willis, the
erratic first Resident Judge.
The little band of survivors of the charge
of the Light Brigade at Balaclava was re-
duced by one the other day when James
Wilson passed out at Christchurch (M.L.).
James was a Scot, and rode with the 10th
Hussars to the Russian guns on the -lay
after arrival at the Crimean front. About
three years ago he married his neighbor, a
slip of a girl of 75. He was 84 when the
call came; lie had lived in Canterbury since
’59.
Fact keeps fiction moving in war time.
Two cousins, named Harold and William
Anderson, whose parents reside near each
other in Melbourne, enlisted, one with the
Victorians, the other with the Maorilanders.
They had not seen each other for years.
Both went away to fight in April and went
into reserve behind the trenches of Galli-
poli in May without being aware of their
proximity. Then one day an order came to
reinforce the fast-thinning firing line. In a
rush up hill Harold paused to take a
breather. The man in front stopped too.
The cousins had met. A word, a handshake,
and they swept on in the rush. Five min-
utes afterwards Harold was killed and
William taken to hospital with a bullet in
his neck.
One of the oldest solicitors on the N. S.
Wales roll, George de Vial Pilcher, has been
sent on for judgment. He and a brother,
Charles E., K.C., were sons of Henry Iceldon
Pilcher, who was admitted as a solicitor in
N. S. Wales on September 1, 1830. There
was another brother, a parson, who obtained
the Broughton and Forrest Exhibition at
King’s School, Parramatta, which took him
to Oriel College, Oxford. He died in Eng-
land some two years since.

11. 11. Massie, who steps into the shoes of
his father-in-law, Mr. T. A. Dibbs, as gen-
eral manager of the Commercial Bank of
Sydney, joined it in 1878. four years before
he went to England with Murdoch’s famous
Australian XI of 1882. The new g.m. will
require to be a remarkable banker before
he wins that way the fame he attained as the
brilliant hitter of the ’SO’s. To be able to
“score as fast as Massie” is a test that few
modern cricketers hope to satisfy. R. J. A.
Massie, his soldier son, wounded in Galli-
poli, is as fast a bowler as 11.11. was a scorer.

QUEENSLAND. M. FORREST.
BILLY VIGIL, footballer, Victoria.
Bigjolm Forrest will be celebrating a
jubilee while Referenda votes are being
counted. On December IS he will complete
the 50th year of his public service in Wes-
tralia. It is proposed to mark the event
with fireworks and a free distribution of
butter.
Jack Horan, the youngest son of “Tommy”
Horan, the hero of many Australian Elevens,
has gone to be a soldier. The once hefty
“Tommy” is not so agile as he used to be,
but Jack, wide, thick and six feet deep, looks
good enough to keep up the old man’s reputa-
tion for ability to smite hard at the right
time and in the right place.
In G. H. Knibbs’s latest “Year Book”
there is one entry that stands out in pure
purple. Under the head of “Occupations
and Ages of Bridegrooms,” there is the
record of one full-blown married man of 15.
who gives his occupation as “professional.”
The ‘‘profession” followed by the patriarch
of 15 isn’t mentioned. Knibbs, dealing with
the same lot of statistics, records the mar-
riage during the year of one giddy spinster
of 95! Oil. the joyous romances that
Knibbs could, but never does, tell!
Macquarie Fields, which is likely to super-
sede Liverpool as the site of the military
camp near Sydney, was one of the estates
given in the Macquarie era to an historical
old character. It was originally a grant of
2020 acres to James Meehan, the assistant
surveyor of the colony. Meehan had come
to the colony early in 1800 with a number
of others concerned in the Irish demonstra-
tion of ’9B. First he was an assistant to
Surveyor-General Grimes when the latter
was surveying Newcastle. Then lie was sent
with Grimes to explore and survey King
Island and Port Phillip. It was Meehan
who surveyed and measured John McAr-
thur's grants at Camden Park in 1805. In
the disturbance which led to the arrest
of Governor Bligh, Meehan kept quiet; but
when Major Johnston sent Grimes to
England with dispatches he appointed
Meehan Acting Surveyor-General, and when
Macquarie took charge two years later he
confirmed the appointment and recommended
that it should be permanent; but John Oxley
got the job and Meehan was gazetted
assistant. From 1817 to 1819 Meehan and
Hamilton Ilume led important exploring
expeditions. They first went south as far
as the Goulburn Plains and discovered
Lakes George and Bathurst, and in 1819
they explored Illawarra as far ■ down as
Jervis Bay, and from there overland to
Bong Bong. It was for these services that
Macquarie gave Meehan the grant which he
called Macquarie Field; and he died there.
It remained in the family for two genera-
tions. Subsequently it was occupied by the
Rev. George Fairfowl McArthur, who went
from there to take charge of King’s School,
Parramatta.
THE CONQUEROR’S MERCY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Woe to the vanquished ! The conqueror knows no
mercy.— Speech by the Kaiser.
You that decreed no mercy, wherefore should mercy
he yours
When the guns of your trust are broken and the
strength of our arm endures?
Death was the word of your , speaking, iron and tor-
ture and shame;
If you reap of your own black sowing, to you alone
is the blame.
High set on a throne of nations, unheeding the curse
or cry,
You watched where your spike-capped legions in long
array marched by ;
Your cannon, black-mouthed and horrent, were ready
to speak your thought,
And a sword, that your eyes might dazzle, the light-
ning of doom had caught.
Proud in your strength, and boasting the power that
you held in trust,
Trembling lest clear and bloodless the arms of your
strength should rust,
You, in your wanton vaunting, threw gage and chal-
lenge to Fate,
Spoke, and your war-whipped millions surged over
bar and gate.
I.eaped to their arms the nations where dry and
rusted they lay,
Thrown from the warriors’ shoulders in the peace of
an elder day;
Leaped on the forges the hammers, leaped through
the night the flame,
To hold you back in your madness that the world
might be saved from shame.
They held you—drove you and held you—through the
months of stress and pain,
Save there where in short-lived triumph you
stretched at the tightening chain ;
And in pride of your eastward marching, where the
Russian lines fell back,
You uttered the mortal treason of the mercy that
conquerors lack !
You with no mercy to show us? Show us your
conquest first!
Is it Liege and Brussels, where your name is held
accurs’d ?
Forty years were you making ready, and when all but
a year is run —-
A year of your bitter striving—-what is it your arms
have won?
Belgium, bruised and broken under your iron heel,
Knows for an hour in her darkness the anguish the
vanquished feel.
Almost you conquered Belgium—mark well how you
made her bleed !
Boast in your hour of triumph—the triumph was
great indeed !
You with no mercy to show us! What shall our
answer be
When your last stand breaks before us, and the
world of your threats is free?
We shall have mercy—yes, mercy—and bring to the
nations hope.
And mercy to them, proud boaster, to you were a
hangman’s rope!
N.s.w.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Commonwealth Government does the
sensible thing and makes new War Loan
stock good tender at par for Commonwealth
probate and succession duties. Interest
will also be free of Commonwealth and
State income tax. For a third concession
a full six months’ interest (44 p.c. per
annum) will be payable on December 15
next, though the final instalment of the
principal will not be called for till Novem-
ber 30. Any bank or any member of any
Stock Exchange or any post-office where
money-order business is done will accept an
application, along with 10 p.c. An appli-
cant may ask for as little as £lOO, and the
instalments on that amount will be payable
as follows:—£10 on application (prior to
August 31), £l5 on September 15, £2O on
October 15, £25 on November 15, £3O on
November 30. Interest will be paid free
of exchange at any branch of the Common-
wealth Bank anywhere in Australia. As an in-
vestment, yielding well over 44 p.c.. nothing
so good has ever been offered in Australia,
since thus far the Commonwealth has bor-
rowed almost nothing, so that both capital
and interest are a charge upon the prac-
tically virgin security of the Consolidated
Revenue of Australia. And, besides being
an investment of the very highest char-
acter, the Loan ought to present itself to
the moneyed people of the continent as a
patriotic duty, no less urgent than that
which is calling the young men to the
trenches.

This bank’s customers are mostly in those
parts which have had a particularly bad
year. There are about 90 branches in Vic-
toria ; you can’t move in the north and nor’-
west without falling up against one, and
the north and nor’-west have had a sicken-
ing time. Of its other 90 or so branches
more tliah a half are in S. Aus., Westralia
and the Riverina; and they have all had a
sickening time, too. As if that wasn’t bad
enough, the bank has probably been earn-
ing very little in London. All through the
half-year you could hardly give away call
money there. The fact was there was no-
thing to do with it. Usually the C. B. of A.
has about a million and a quarter or more
on the other side, and 4 p.c. on that lot
means at least £0250 a year. Further, owing
partly to the war and more largely to the
drought, there have been few meat and wool
and produce bills on London to buy; and
this branch of the exchange business, in
which the Commercial has rather special-
ised. has been far less profitable than usual.
Meantime the 180 branches or thereabouts
have had to be kept going. In all the cir-
cumstances it isn’t surprising that the pro-
fits fell from about £05.000 to £44.000. The
astonishing thing is that the bank earned
that much.
The Commercial of Australia had a dis-
tressing experience after the great Victorian
boom of the late ’eighties and early ’nineties.
Ai that time, as the bulk of the assets were
really too atrocious to be seen with, the
bank cut them off and turned the worst of
them over to an assets trust, whose job was
to get rid of them with the least possible
loss. As losses were ascertained most of the
share capital was written off. Depositors,
seeing that it was hopeless to keep going
without further capital, converted a large
mass of their paper into pref. shares; and
in the course of the realisation of the old
assets a large part of this capital has also
been absorbed. Combining the operations of
the bank and the trust, which are really one,
suice the bank is responsible for the trust’s
liabilities, this is how things stand :

So in six years something over £307.000 of
the lost capital has been restored, really out
of the current profits of the bank; and at
the same rate of progress the capital will
all be restored somewhere about 1930. But
as the next six years are not likely to see
the same prosperity as the last six, the
period is quite likely to be extended to 1940,
or some other date in the far-distant future.
A more sensible plan would be to write
down. the. capital and pay a decent rate of
dividend forthwith. But there is a conflict
of interests between ordinary and pref.
shares; and the management plainly hasn’t
the courage to take the two bulls by the
horns. So the bank faces its second time of
stress handicapped by having to publish a
balance-sheet which, simply because it hasn’t
been cleaned up, is by far the worst-looking
in Australia. It is all so unnecessary.
There have been no very notable changes
in the figures during the 12 months, not-
withstanding the serious things that have
been happening :—-

The shrinkage of bills, etc., is due to the
contraction of sea-going trade, and appears
on the other side as a reduction of liquid
assets. And the drawing-in of advances to
the extent of about £lOO,OOO is balanced by
a. contraction of free deposits and old debts.
The C. B. of A. is still exceptionally strong
in liquid assets, and the advances are ex-
ceptionally well protected by shareholders’
funds. The two features taken together re-
duce the risks of depositors and other
creditors to practically nil.
As for the future, Mill long ago noted the
extraordinary rapidity with which an agri-
cultural community recovers its prosperity
after a war ; and the same country connec-
tion which was responsible for the serious
shrinkage of the C. B. of A.’s profit last
half-year will most likely fill this bank’s
bunkers with prosperity even while other
interests are still feeling pretty sore and
miserable after the war.

For 48 years the accounts have been signed
“T. A. Dibbs, general manager.” Now Mr.
Dibbs has gone, and assistant-G.M. H. 11.
Massie takes the wheel. Nobody doubts
that he has left the ship snug for the dirty
weather that is ahead. The next seven years
are not going to be the past seven fat smooth
years over again. We may have as good
seasons, on the whole, but we shall be pay-
ing instead of spending. The wicked policy
of the N. S. Wales Labor Government has
produced a boom which can only be main-
tained as long as the borrowings continue:
and they are very near their end now. It
is true that the Norton Griffiths syndicates
have undertaken to provide £10,000,000 in
the next four years—£4,ooo,ooo the first
year, and £2,000,000 a year thereafter. But
it is by no means certain that the British
Government will sanction these loans. And,
anyhow, if they are available, any indepen-
dent issues will on present appearances be
utterly out of the question. This traitor
Government has withdrawn 20,000 men
from ordinary occupations to scramble for
loan money at boom rates of pay. Within
the next year or so most of them will be
thrown out. Meantime artificial rates of pay
have been established, a ruinous system of
slowing-down has come into vogue, and,
generally, almost every conceivable discour-
agement has been offered to the private
employment of labor. The working-man of
N. S. Wales, in particular, has been living in
a fool’s paradise, constructed for him by a
Labor Government which has been a traitor
to almost every Labor principle, and the
awakening is not far off. Everybody knows
where the Devil landed when he fell out of
Paradise. The working-man, when the
bottom falls out of his paradise, will not be
much better; and then he will know what
the “Labor” Government has done for him.
While the artificial conditions have lasted,
nobody has profited so much as the middle-
men and gamblers in land and supplies. Con-
cerns which were worth only a few hundreds
12 years ago are worth tens of thousands to-
day. Prices have been forced up and up, and
holders of stocks of merchandise have made
fortunes out of advancing markets. Inci-
dentally, the banks, which to a great extent
are middlemen, too, have been in clover. But
at least it can be said to their credit that,
with a very few exceptions, they have never
sought to disguise the fact that they were
boom conditions under which we have been
living. Hence, as the business of this bank
has grown with extraordinary rapidity, gen-
eral-manager Dibbs and his directors have
dropped more anchors overboard against the
breaking of the storm. In eight years they
have doubled the share capital and added
substantially to the reserves by way of pre-
mium capital. All the time approximately
50 p.c. of the public’s money in the business
has been kept liquid. The following figures
show the movements in the past 25 years and
in the past 10: — „ „
1890. 1905. 1915.
Capital .. .. £600,000 £1,000,000 £2,000,000

serves combined 1,411,960 1,106,593 3,852,(34

Compare the position as at June, 1890, with
the present, and see how vastly better
equipped the bank is now to meet the diffi-
culties ahead than it was to face those which
followed the last boom.
A bank has to meet two dangers. It must
be able to stand a quick, sharp blow, the re-
sult of more or less sudden pressure from
creditors; and it must be able to stand a
long, steady drain upon its resources through
having overvalued securities and lent too
much on them. To meet the firSt danger it
must be strong in liquid assets; to overcome
the other, its capital and reserves must be
large in proportion to its advances and other
risks. To take the second —the more impor-
tant —first. In 1890 the Commercial of Syd-
ney had £9,440,046 of advances, and could
lose £1,411,960 before the creditors’ money
would be touched—that is, 3s. in the £. Now
it has £14,236,405 of advances and £3,852,734
of its own funds, so it could lose ss. sd. in
the £ before the creditors would be affected.
In 1890 its capital and reserves were equal
to only 15 p.c. of the advances ; now they are
equal to 27 p.c. As to any danger from a
run, there is at no time much risk nowa-
days, on account of the understanding
between the associated banks. But, in any
case, while this bank owes £25.774,099, it has
no less than £14,890,785 liquid. That is to
say, nearly 5S p.c. In ordinary circum-
stances, that would be far too much. There
is usually no need for such strength. But
these are very extraordinary times. People
are putting the'r money in banks like the
Commercial of Sydney for safe custody as
much as anything. Hardly anybody is seek-
ing permanent investments. Governments
have succeeded in thoroughly unsettling
nearly everybody. And prudent bankers like
those of the Commercial of Sydney, knowing
that they are to a great extent being made a
convenience of, simply put the lponey by, to
be available when called upon. Thus while
Mr. Dibbs leaves his brawny successor to
face a period maybe of reduced profits, cer-
tainly of stress, he also leaves him dug in as
no bank in Australia was when the last great
trouble came to the country 24 years ago.

Though Queensland has had a bad summer,
and though it is difficult to understand .how
banking profits could have been maintained,
the Q.N. shows actually better results than
in any previous recent period. The most
likely explanation is that past figures have
really not given much of an indication of the
true current profits. It is impossible for
any bank to say positively “We made (say)
£40,000 profit this half-year—just that and
no more or less.” A bank only once in its
life starts a half-year with no old sins to
atone for, and it never winds up within the
term all the business it has taken in hand
during that term. A manager may make a
loan to-day on security which to-morrow
may not look to s him half so good as it did
24 hours before; and he may then decide
that, to be on the safe side, he ought to put
by £lOOO of the half-year’s profit to wipe
out the possible loss on the loan he is sorry
he made. Or he may believe that the values
on which he is making advances are all so
high that he cannot reasonably escape some
heavy losses presently; and he may there-
fore resolve that he ought to put aside in
the good times a great deal of what looks
like profit to presently meet the losses that
will show up on the old transactions. It
seems very likely that this is what the Q.N.
has been doing.
There is usually one incentive to a mana-
ger to show all that he reasonably can show
(and sometimes more than that), viz., the
hunger of the shareholders for the profits.
But in this bank no profits go to the share-
holders, and none can go to them for some
time to come. This is because of an old
arrangement with the private depositors
who couldn’t get their money back when
the bank closed its doors in the early ’9o’s.
There were, it was reckoned, assets to repre-
sent only three-fourths of the deposits; so
it was agreed that the other fourth should be
made good out of future profits, and that
no dividend should be paid until this fourth
was returned. Hence so far as dividends
are concerned it hasn’t mattered whether
the Q.N. said it made £40.000 or £60.000.
The disclosure of a small profit if a large
one has been made would no doubt affect the
market value of shares; but bank manage-
ments have a habit of saying that the
market value of shares does not concern
them. Whether the Q.N. management has
been playing a very safe game and building
up a large inner reserve for contingencies
The Bulletin doesn’t know for certain;
but the fact that general-manager Ralston
can show a bigger profit than ever in spite
of bad times strongly suggests that that
policy has been pursued.
The resumption of dividends depends first,
then, upon the repayment of that ss. in the
£ of old deposits previously mentioned. To-
wards that discharge the following shows
approximately the progress made: —

The “special” payment made in 1913 was
apparently out of the contingency fund men-
tioned just now ; and it was said some time
ago that another “special” could be expected
in 1916. In four years 6s. 9d. has been paid
off, and there is Bs. 9d. still to pay, of which
approximately Is. 3d. is in hand. In view
of the war it might be expected that a longer
period than (say) 44 years would now be
required to pay off the balance; but if the
bank can put aside £39,746 in so bad a half-
year as that just passed, it can hardly be ex-
pected to do worse during the next five
years; and even without any “special” pav-
ment at all out of contingency funds, five
years would see the removal of the last bone
of the venerable dead-llorse.
As usual, the Q.N.’s cash position is very
strong. In coin, cash at bankers, Australian
notes and sliort-call money in London it
holds no less than £4,364,992; and, apart
from its interminable stock, it owes the
public only £7,969,157. It is thus in a posi-
tion to pay 55 p.c. of all withdrawable money
practically on the nail and without realising
on a single security- If there is any weak-
ness at all in the position it lies in the com-
parative smallness of the shareholders’ visi-
ble funds. There is £6,288,587 in advances,
etc., and another £318,821 in premises, etc.,
and there is at present only £670,000 of
share capital and reserves to protect those
advances. The rest of the money belongs to
outsiders. But there is no less than
£3,091,669 of interminable stock ; and so far
as depositors are concerned that is as good
as share capital. So they are right; nobody
could reasonably wish to be more right.
