That comparatively poor profit is the result
of drought rather than of war. A trustee
co.’s revenue flows in through three chan-
nels: (1) Annual commission on earnings
of trust estates; (2) single commission on
the'capital value of estates; (3) interest on
the co.’s own funds. Owing to the drought
the earnings of many country estates have
been very small during the past couple of
years. Last year most farmers and wool-
growers made an actual loss. That cut into
this co.’s commission earnings.
But what of the future? In one respect a
trustee co. is unusually well placed. All the
plant and stock which such a co. needs could
be bought for a couple of hundred pounds—a
brass plate, a few boxes of pens, a rubber
stamp or two, and a seal. Actually this big
co.’s furniture and fittings, which constitute
all its stock and plant, are valued in the
books at only £5OO. Necessary till-money
might run to another £5OO or £lOOO. All the
rest of the capital and reserves can be in-
vested. As a matter of fact, the investments
of this co., apart from the business, amount
to £138,000, while the total of the capital
and reserves is £144,145. For a co. with
money to lend, the high rates of interest
which the world is going to pay for some
years to come is plainly a good thing. And
a trustee co.’s advantages don’t stop there.
Among this co.’s trust assets are very nearly
£3,000,000 of mortgage advances and fixed
deposits, and they will soon bring in from
£30,000 to £45.000 a year more than they did
a while ago. The co.’s commission on that
will be worth a trifle. On the other hand,
its income from commission on the corpus or
capital value will shrink. When interest
rises, capital values fall —unless the property
is of the sort that becomes more productive
with the rise of interest. The shrinkage
applies to public securities, shares and real
estate. Further, the war taxes that are ahead
of us will depreciate values. When a farm
brings in £lOOO a year, it is worth more than
when it brings in only £950 a year—£so
being added to the outgoings to pay tax.
There are tens of thousands of propertied
people in Australia who will not get a farth-
ing of benefit from the high rate of interest:
for them there is nothing ahead but extra
payments. The T., E. &A. and other trustee
cos. will have to make extra payments, too,
and they will witness a reduced flow of re-
venue through certain channels; but they are
fortunate in being able to score a larger flow
than usual through other channels.

ROYDS, LTD., Investors’ Advisers, Challis House, Sydney
Attorney-General Hughes .says it isn r
necessary to have a Commission to get at
the inside of the metal-buying business, let
the things there are to be found out might
surprise even W. M. Hughes, in spite of all
he has learned about the business during the
month or two he has been getting himself
informed and misinformed about it. Early
in the war action was taken against the
Australian agent of Aaron Hirsch & Son, big
German buyers. That agent’s business being
blocked, another firm —H. R. Merton & Co.
—stepped in and collared a good part of it.
“Are you sure Merton’s are all right.’ the
Attorney-General’s Department was asked.
“Oh, yes.” they said; “Merton’s are metal
contractors to the British Admiralty.” Those
in the inside of the business could have told
them then, what Hughes knows now, that
you can also spell Merton Melirten, and
that you can find that name at Frankfort.
The legs of people in high places were thus
being pulled then, and they are being pulled
now. The best-known English metal firm—
anyway, the one best known iii Australia —
is largely German. One of the partners in
it is the former manager of the Australian
Metal Co.—the German concern whose man-
aging director was interned the other day.
The Electrolytic Refining Co., at Port
Kembla (N.5.W.),. is partly German owned.
In the spelter business what isn’t German
is mostly Belgian; and in the nickel and
cobalt business the Austrian Rothschilds
are interested with their French (chiefly
French), German and English partners. In
fact, the world’s metal business isn't so
much German as Germanised. Germany
has a finger everywhere. And it has more
than a finger in the London Metal Exchange,
for the members of that body are mostly
either foreigners or dummies for foreigners.
The most thoroughly British firm in the
copper business in a big way in London is
Smith’s; and, curiously enough, Smith’s was
amongst the first firms turned down by the
ill-informed Commonwealth Government.
Heaven only knows what sinister influences
were at work pulling the strings.
The thing that Germany has aimed at all
along has been to secure the final treatment
of the ore or metal. As long as it had that
it knew it had the control of the metal.
That was the text on which a series of
Bulletin articles was written three or
four years ago, and the Government was
then implored to do something for the smelt-
ing and refining business in Australia. As
usual, it “couldn’t see its way.” And Brit-
ain couldn’t see its way either. To fool
about with metal exchanges, as Hughes is
now dome, is useless, or almost useless.
The Bulletin wouldn’t be a bit surprised
to hear that the scheme could be traced
back to some German with his tongue in his
cheek. The thing Great Britain and Aus-
tralia must do is exactly what Germany has
done— vis., to spend money on smelting
works and refineries, subsidising tliem, if
necessary. Until those works and refineries
are established, or until there is assurance
that they will be established, the Australian
mining cos. will not annul their contracts
with the foreigners, because they know that
if they did they would fall between two
stools. Give them reasonable assurance of
Australian or British works to displace
those in Germany and Belgium, and there
isn’t any doubt in the world that the con-
tracts will be torn up to-morrow.
After consulting the British authorities,
tlie Commonwealth Government has lifted
the embargo placed on the Amalgamated
Zinc (De Bavay) Co.’s proposed shipment of
zinc concentrates from Broken Hill to the
TT.S.A. The first lot of 6000 tons will go
away at once. The contract runs to 50,000
tons, which will bring in about £475,000.
* * * *
Cope’s Creek Central (N.S.W.) made only
£778 for six months to May 31. The two
plants were a.t work only part of the half
year. At Tingha 59,980 yards averaged
1.0131 b., but costs at 10.75 d. per yard prac-
tically ate the proceeds. 'At Skeleton Creek
101,630 yards of wash produced 50 tons
6cwt. black tin, averaging I.llb. per yard.
Altogether 78 tons of oxide worth £8955
were produced. The co. has purchased for
£6OO 58 acres adjoining its own area at
Skeleton Creek, and dredging ground has
been applied for on the Mann River.
* ■* * *
A promising little gold show that merits
development is the Bob and Billv, about
five miles from Oberon (N.S.W.). Many
years ago good stone, crushing about an
ounce, besides rich specimens, was obtained
from this claim, and it has lately been
opened up again by a working party which
includes one of the original prospectors.
A fair-sized quartz reef, which gives every
sign of permanence, is showing, and some
nice specimen stuff has been obtained.
* * * *
A sale of plant has occurred to disturb the
Mabel, at Wiseman’s Creek (N.S.W.), which
has been slumbering for about 12 months.
This is the finale of the ill-judged attempt
of a Sydney crowd to apply the all-solution
system to an ore carrying, besides gold, a
more than appreciable quantity of copper and
acid from partially decomposed copper pyrites.
Besides these drawbacks, the ore contains a
good deal of baryta, and baryta is by reason
of its high specific gravity a curse to the
mill-man and cyanider. Situated in an out-
of-the-way corner, with about 255. a ton car-
riage from the railway, little Mabel was
sadly handicapped, but she was given a game
try, and the pity is that she was a failure.
Since the old Phoenix (copper) further up
the hill closed down, there isn’t a working
mine in the vicinity.
* * * *
Corella Copper (Q.) closed its half-year
on April 30 with a net profit of £4932. The
smelter was in commission about two months,
and in that time 3442 tons ore were reduced
to 470 tons high-grade matte, which went by
motor and rail to the Hampden converter.
The contents were 234 tons copper and
1460 z. gold. Profit averaged £1 Bs. Bd. per
ton of ore, and £2l per ton of copper. The
grade was 6.8 p.c. copper, so the ore was
worth £4 15s. with copper at £75. But a
good deal of stuff was obtained in developing
the Bosebud, and that no doubt helped these
figures. Just at present the water supply is
low, so it is quite possible the smelter will
close down. Reserves are 16,700 tons, con-
taining 1596 tons copper; also 15,000 tons
of low-grade calcite ore useful as flux.
-X * * *
Mount Elliott (Q.) is about to start smelt-
ing again. It now controls.the Dobbin and
Cloncurry mines, and will doubtless draw
on them, and maybe on the Oxide. But the
future of the Oxide depends on an exten-
sion of the Mount Cuthbert line: and there
is some rough country to cross.
* -X- * #
A. 11. Moline has cleaned up the bottom at
Cock’s Pioneer, Eldorado (Vic.), for 1728:>z.
gold and 25 tons stream tin, got from 70,000
cubic yards of drift. This is the plum in
the pudding. Taking the whole run, 30510 z.
gold and 40 tons of black tin came from
380,000 cubic yards. This should yield a
profit if costs weren’t very much affected by
having to remove the props and laths from
old McEvoy workings. These covered
3100 sq. yards of a total of 11.300 sq. yards
of bottom, and they must have cut out a
fair-sized slab of highly profitable ground.
* * * *
Rose of Denmark, Gaffney’s Creek (Vic.),
has fallen to 7dwt., but even on that divi-
dends can be made. The co. does best in
the winter, when water is plentiful and
crushing costs lowest. Stopes are kept going
from No. 3 to No. 6 level, where small floors
in a big dyke are being smashed down. The
best-looking just now lies above No. 3 level
and is being prospected. As a rule in the
Vic. mountains the best stone is nearest the
sunlight, so a sweetening may be got here
to bring divs. up a bit. As Al, near by, is
burning dimly, any blossoming by the Rose
will help to improve the feeling of Gaffney’s.
* * * *
“G.D.M.” : One ought always to be chary
about castigating a young and rising mining
expert, yet all consideration for the young'
man’s future must be laid aside while I tell
“R. 8.” (B. 15/7/T5) that his data regard-
ing the Railton-Latrobe oil-shale deposits
are utterly erroneous. Let me be categorical:
(1) Prom what “R. 8.” saw five years ago he is
incompetent to write about the present Railton-
Latrobe Co.’s shale mine and distilling plant. 1
was there one month ago and saw the new retorts
being completed and the tunnelling, which “R. 8.”
declares* is not possible, being carried on with much
success.
(2) I’ve been to Pumpherston, Scotland. The
average of Scotch shale is 28 gallons to the ton of
ore, not 18. This fact was given me. by Mr. Robert
Moore, chairman of the company, with whom I
travelled through India.
(3) The grade of Scottish shale has not been fol-
lowed down to vanishing point. The Scottish cos.
suffered and are suffering from the dishonest com-
petition of the Standard Oil Trust of Yankeeland,
which is to-day busy trying to stifle the petroleum
industry in Australia.
(4) Crude oil is worth 4d., not 24d., per gal.
(5) The profits are certainly not in the refining,
although Latrobe petrol in quality beats the im-
ported article. In a car from Devonport to Launces-
ton, using Latrobe petrol, we did 30 miles to the
gallon.
(6) The residue after distillation of the crude oil is
not manure, but spraying oil for use in the local
orchards—better in quality than the Yankee rubbish.
At the Railton Co.’s office I saw samples of 22
different oil products.
(7) Of course, Latrobe is not in the same street
as Wolgan and Capertee, but its shale is more
cheaply and easily worked. I’ve been in the Wolgan
mine.

Westralia’s ore average for last year was
37.81 shillings, or 1.38 shillings below that
for 1913. Phillips River at 158.91 shil-
lings had the highest average, and Broad
Arrow with 27.82 shillings the lowest.
East Coolgardie (Ivalgoorlie), with 34.20
shillings, was the second lowest; and Yil-
garn came next with 35.64 shillings. Other
averages were: Pilbarra, 81.66; West Pil-
barra, 92.72 ; Peak Hill, 95.19 ; East Murchi-
son, 39.82 ; Murchison, 48.78 ; Yalgoo, 72.27 ;
Mount Margaret, 39.63; North Coolgardie.
61.04 ; North-East Coolgardie, 53.20; Cool-
gardie, 46.34; Dundas, 41.30. The men em-
ployed in mining were distributed thus: East
Coolgardie, 5003; Yilgarn, 884; Murchison,
1394; Mount Margaret, 1067; North Cool-
gardie, 1159; East Murchison. 968; Collie,
525; and Coolgardie, 498. Broad Arrow,
Dundas, North East Coolgardie, Yalgoo, Pil-
barra, West Pilbarra, Peak Hill, Phillips
River, Kimberley, Ashburton. Gascoyne,
Greenbushes and Northampton had 1676
between them,

Westralia’s June dividends amounted to
£128,177, and made a total for the half-
year of £388,468. Of this Great Boulder
paid £131,250, Ivanhoe £55,000, Ka'lgurli
£48,000. Golden Horseshoe £45,000, Edna
May £38,565, Sand Queen £28,500, Sons of
Gwalia £16,250, Bullfinch Prop. £11,903,
Lake View and Star £lO,OOO. Mararoa
£5OOO, and Ivyarra £2OOO. This current
month £70,000 has so far been loosed. Great
Boulder has now distributed £4,613,050,
Ivanhoe £3,373,750, and Golden Horseshoe
£3,180,000.
* * * *
The average value of gold produced per
man employed in the industry in Westralia
last year was £435, or approximately £l6
more than in 1913. The average tonnage
of ore raised per man increased from 214 to
230. East Coolgardie was again the highest
with 339 tons, and Yilgarn next with 250.
The average profit per ton last year was
ss. lid. In 1903, the year of record gold-
production. it was 18s. 9d.; in 1904 it was
2s. less; in the following year it was a
decimal lower. After that the fall was
rapid.
* * * *
Golden Ridge got leave in the Perth
Supreme Court the other day to reduce its
capital, which it had found to be in excess
of requirements. The Board denies a story
current in the West that the co. has taken
an option on the Yellow .Jacket at Broad
A rrow.
* * * *
Most accounts don’t rate highly a new
goldfield alleged to have been unearthed at
what is known as the Middle Ironeap, 100
miles south of Southern Cross (W.A.). and
which some venturesome papers reckoned a
second Kalgoorlie. The find is now declared
to be low grade, and appearances don’t
inspire confidence.
* * * *
In December last the news was gently
broken to Kalgurli shareholders that a fur-
ther reduction in dividends was imminent.
Since then two of 4s. have been paid, and
a third (notice of which was accompanied
by the announcement that the Board pur-
poses cutting down the quarterly distribu-
tions from 4s. to 2s. 6d.) was paid on July
6. Although the mine is dying slowly,
shareholders can rest assured that the man-
agement is doing everything possible for it
and for them.
* * * *
A Melbourne-financed syndicate has made
a start to work a magnesite deposit at
Bulong (W.A.). Operations are stated to
be under the control of a firm which is said
to be the only one in Australia dealing with
this mineral in a large way, and 500 tons
are to be taken out as a test parcel.
* * * *
Associated Northern’s June profit of £2lOO
from the Ora Banda (W.A.) is the best since
treatment was resumed in December. The
ore treated was 1000 tons more than that
put through in the first month’s run, but the
recovery at 28s. 4d. a ton was down 7s. Bd.
During the past six months 18,615 tons have
been treated for a net profit of £4139, and in
the same period an additional £1192 has been
netted from the treatment of ti’ibutors’ ore
and accumulated residues at the co.’s Iron
Duke (Kalgoorlie) lease.
* * * *
From the first man who examined the lot:
When I said the Edna belt (W.A.) was a large
one, I was talking generally of the region as a pros-
pecting field. I meant Westonia, from Boodallin to
anywhere “the other man” (B. 15/7/T5) likes. It’s
useless this gentleman trying to get back on my esti-
mate of value in the Edna by talking of ore not yet
developed. I put present values at 665. a share, or
22 months’ work, and providing that No. 4 is as good
as No. 3, there is another 335. a share. I may be
wrong, but I believe I’m right; and the other fellow
can’t contradict me with speculations as to ore un-
exposed. My figures deal with the mine as it is, and
the probable developments at No. 4 are taken on an
optimistic basis. The fact that the Edna does all the
pumping doesn’t affect the truth that the Central's
costs—mining and milling—are the lowest on the
field. Pumping is a big item, and will be a greater;
and if the Deeps—which the other fellow, inferenti-
ally, loves —wants to earn its name, it must sink for
its life and then pump the field. There is no call for
the petulance of the irritated barracker for the Deeps.
A mine is a matter of opinion, but all the same I
believe that £5 a share is the value of the Edna.
* * * * -
The Ida 11. Co. has decided to stick to
legitimate gold mining. Having an idea that
it could make more money elsewhere, the •
Board, a while ago, let things slide a bit at
the Laverton property, and gave Nigerian tin
WEES 8 the .“glad eye.” In one lump
£12,000 was invested in the Rokuha mine,
which, according to a gaudy estimate, had
hundreds of thousands of pounds in sight.
As the venture has since been abandoned the
inference is that this wealth only existed in
somebody’s imagination.
* * * *
There is no telling what these transcon-
tinental railways won’t lead to. Four and
a half miles from the East-and-West line.
76 miles out from Port Augusta, a Broken
Hill co. has been put on to what looks like
something special in a manganese show.
It is on Pernatty Lake. For 400 yards ore
of high grade has*been opened up, hut the
lateral extent of the deposit has not been
determined. A lot of 50 tons has been
sampled and the lcwt. sample analysed by
the S. Aus. School of Mines. It showed
50.12 p.c. manganese dioxide and 1.56 man-
ganese oxide, together equal to 51.86 p.c.
manganese; while for phosphorus it went
only 0.01 p.c., sulphur 0.01 p.c., and silica
1.36 p.c. The manganese contents are good
and the content of the three prejudicial
elements exceedingly small. There isn’t a
greedy market for the ore; but the dis-
covery ought to be of great assistance pre-
sently in developing the Australian steel
and steel-alloys industries.

A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, July 2G, 1915.
My Deal Arini, —
The Munro Ferguson visit lo the Hope
Osbornes’ sheep-run at Coolac came to an
end when his Ex. was summoned to sign his
name or something in Melbourne. Excel-
lency Helen came on to Sydney and again
put up with Miss Eaclith Walker at
Yaralla.
The patriotic show at the Agricultural
Ground stood out in a plethora of week-end
raids for the Cause. Hon. organisers
■'Snowy” Baker and 11. M. Somer gave the
public a big helping of sport for their cash.
The rain which flirted round at noon kept
Away. In spite of a soft, spongy take-off
for the high jump, this event was up to the
average. Dolly Fogg and Myra Smith, very
trim in divided coats, sat astride their
hunters in the New Girl manner. But a
bit of old Satan, or perhaps a dash of
caution, animated their mounts. The gee-
gees were quite affable until the hurdle had
reached a certain height. After that,
Balaam’s ass was their model in deportment.
They looked at the timber, and even smelt it.
Over it they would not budge. At the
normal six feet or thereabouts Bandolier and
Sunstar (owned by Mr. Judd) carried their
ladies in sporting style. A colored rider,
who gave a sleek black hunter a trial sprint
at one of the high hurdles, sat down sud-
denly on the wet sod. But he cleared the
obstacle at the second effort. Loud applause.
G.-G. Ferguson, very jovial in khaki, came
along with Lady Helen. Even the great
are not without their minor sorrows. His
Ex.’s Gladstone collar —of a smaller size than
those the famous William used to bandage
his throat in—suffered from an insufficiency
of starch, and took a most unmilitary lurch
under one ear. Her Ex., faithful to cherry-
colored ostrich plumes, was snugly sheltered
in a black velvet coat, worn over a frock in
the same lively shade as her top-hamper,
Help Fane looks a sick man still, and his
slim wife is a mere wraith compared with
our upstanding Australian Amazons. Gov-
ernor Strickland, with a helping from the
vice-regal kindergarten, had Aide Pasco and
the Hunter’s Hill mountaineer, d’Apice, at
his heels. The Strickland lady enjoyed an
unbroken view of the sports from her chaise,
where the Munro Ferguson dame presently
shared her rug and carriage seat.
In the official stand was the Jap Consul-
General, who was inadvertently mislaid for
a time. When the teacups tinkled in the
sacred enclosure where Governors and poli-
ticians nibble plunvcake together, a hurried
deputation dashed up to the retiring Shimizu
to lead him forward into the official splendor
of the point-lace tea-tables.
The trotting duet between Mrs. Emslie
and a man in khaki showed the woman rider
to advantage. Bareheaded, with a short
white coat over her dark skirt, this fine
horsewoman sits forward astride in a Tod
Sloan attitude, and gets a wonderful pace
out of her hack.
The Muuro-Ferguson lady is one of the
fleet footed, and she went to her tremendous
tasks at the double last week. In the
absence of Himself —recalled to Melbourne
for a day—she delivered some wise remarks
at the Scots College. The occasion was the
airing of a new set of class-rooms. The
wisdom lay in reproving the traditional
British snub that lies in waiting for the
inventor and man of science. “Our indif-
ference,” said she, “is perhaps why Ger-
many is where it is to-day, instead of being
pushed back on to its own territory.” The
ascetic-looking Professor Mclntyre was in
charge of the welcome.
Apropos of the Scots’ College, Dr. Jessie
Aspinall, a daughter of the former prin-
cipal, is now Mrs. A. W. Freeman. The
wedding is about a month old. Husband —
a mining engineer—is a brother of the gal-
lant young Captain who was killed in the
first rush on Gallipoli. Hence the silence
which surrounded the ceremony.
Governor Strickland took his young daugh-
ters to the Chamber of Commerce the other
morning, to see the things we used to buy
from Germany—and will again, unless a tariff
stops us. One table was draped with the
cheap stocking that deceived the pre-war
bargain-hunter. It looked so fine and lacy,
but it wore out in record time in holes big
enough to step straight through. On
another section were the artificial flowers
sold for their violent colors and low prices.
German knltur never exnressed itself, in the
artistic French or Italian manner. Even
the table stacked with enemy toys showed
the coarser methods of Kaiser land in the
grotesque pictures on the boxes of games.
In the perfumery department they made a
bold bid for the female purse. Less deli-
cate than the French or best English goods,
here again the Germans supplied articles
that Australia might beat out of sight if
it had as much application and the same
kind of protection at the Customs. In the
matter of household and kitchen goods the
ITuns had a big pull. This was their best
suit and they played their cards to take
every trick. Comparatively few women
have visited the Chamber of Commerce show.
As local dames have been banding them-
selves together to do Dreadful things to
enemy goods, they ought to step into the
Commerce parlor this week.
And when they come out they might ask
themselves if it really matters whether the
goods are made at Stuttgart or Timbuctoo.
The thing that really does matter is that
they should be made in Australia; or, if
they can’t be made in Australia, then in
Britain. Twenty-five years ago Australia
was saying “France is the enemy—we
mustn’t buy things from France.” So we
bought things from Germany instead —and
made Germany great. There’s only one
country we can afford to make great. Our
own Country.
Grenville Miller, the mild, youngish man
who was Private Sec. to Governor Rawson,
is somewhere in the North Sea just now.
He is the official nib on the staff of H.M.S.
(say) Woolloomooloo. Miller, who mar-
ried Clara McKay, a relative of the Knox
family, which deals in sugar and acid, was
on Admiral Poore’s clerical staff for a while.
He had the luck, by the way, to be on a
ship that took part in the Falkland Islands
battle.
The arrival of the soldiers who were
wounded and those who were merely “re-
turned” by the authorities, was full of
minor humors. The "disciplinary cases”
looked as heroic as they could —stuck a hand
inside their noble vests or limped slowly
along the station, giving the Bed Cross
ladies the idea that here was plenty _of
material to purr over. A hard-hearted citi-
zen, who watched the excited dames take on
the importance of field-marshals and dash
at the “disciplinary” hard-cases, says the
situation was Gilbertian. However, the
genuine brand of hero was eventually sal-
vaged from the wastage and sent to the
Randwick hospital; and the Bed Cross ladies
bustled out to offer their sympathy and a
vacuum cleaner.
Adrian Stephen, the Sydney lawyer who
has the miqyobe that drives a man to write
drama, took a sheaf of his playlets to
London. He went there primarily, of course,
to get into the firing line—a fairly easy thing
for those who are built that way—and was
recently at the Front with a commission in
the Itoyal Field Artillery.
Australia Day this week makes a purple
patch in the useful drab of Bed Cross meet-
ings. Martin-place will be, for that great
event, such a market-garden as Sydney does
not grow in ordinary times. Here, one feels,
is the germ of something which the average
housekeeper may yet call blessed —some
central place where, armed with anything
from a string bag to a wheelbarrow navi-
gated by her juvenile Hope, she may buy
fruit or vegetables in the Continental man-
ner. Hedged by convention, Materfamilias
is as yet helpless in the hands of the itiner-
ant hawker. On Friday only mounted
police will be able to tear her away from the
glut of green feed in the neighborhood of the
post office.
Miss Bud Neill, daughter of a former Syd-
ney banker, is back here after much gadding
about. On Australia Day she will have a
fine stand in Castlereagh-street, nearly oppo-
site the Theatre Royal. Among the myriad
other helpers are two nurses who came home
with the Kyarra wounded, and are making
themselves thus useful before bustling back
to active service.
Three cheers to be given for the Allies
and ourselves on July 30 will again congest
the traffic outside the Town Hall. The item
might well have been cut out. On Belgian
Day it made for nothing but infuriated
language and the loss of peggy-bags.
The woolbuyers’ evening at the Wentworth
Hotel earned about £7OOO for the Red Cross.
Unfortunately for those who missed the fun
at this auction of a bale of wool and other
goods (Harry Ilolt, auctioneer), there were
other shows that evening. Some of those not
present, for instance, spent an icy-cold hour
or two in the Stadium, where the same cause
was being ministered to.
The United Charities’ Fund had its report
and balance-sheet well aired in the Town
Hall the other showery afternoon. As
spokesman, Governor Strickland handed out
small envelopes, with cheques to match ; for
the Charities suffered badly in their last
street collection from the clash of war.
Archbishop Wright, who has a cosy dimple
in his chin, made a few remarks. Tom
Hughes, M.L.C., goaded those present to
consider the urgency of the cry of the
smaller charities which have been almost
suffocated by the mountainous war. And
then Archdeacon D’Arcy Irvine told the
company of Tconversation he had just had
with an informative fowl, which had
whispered him that ins Ex. had to
hurry off. So the meeting grabbed
its umbrellas and stampeded; and a
pretty damsel behind a large white
fish-net veil gathered up any balance-sheets
left over, and put them away for Sunday
reading.
Her Majesty’s on Australia Day will over-
flow with a matinee that threatens to con-
tinue into next week. Something will liap-
pen, of course, to prevent the tail of the
programme getting involved with Sunday
morning’s church bells. But, even so, those
who have given five guineas for a stall will
get an awful lot for their cash.
Having torn most of her clothes off her
back for Belgian Day (I allude, of course, to
the outer shell of coney seal) Woman has
now come down to mere pearls and diamond
pendants. Florence Young parted with a
neck ornament which was aft-unioned at the
Town Hall the other day. Any lady who
happens to own a diamond tiara she lias no
further use for can communicate with the
Martin-place market ladies. They will cheer-
fully dispose of it, or a pound of butter and
a sucking pig, on strict!} 7 business lines.

i.icp fnr Hip Dviilv Dolores samr her best,
But the night was wet, and the audience had
cold feet, and the atmosphere was against
the booming success of the auction. Henri
Staell, bringing a caged canary on to the
Town Hall platform, looked as if he feared
the worst. Yet the tiny songster brought
£11; and a very fluffy puppy, with its legs
giving way under it (stage fright, I sup-
pose), fetched about nine guineas.

Dr. Hankins, who used to have a brass-
plate in this hamlet, lias been saying good-
bye to old pals here. A while ago, as a
widower, he went back to England and mar-
ried the sweetheart of his youth. But the
fates sent a motor accident to upset the new
Mrs. Hankins’s desire for travel. So he
returned alone and took his stethoscope to
Warwick (Q.). As the lady wants to go on
living under an English umbrella, hubby
packed up again and left last week.

Louise Mack varies her story of the inva-
sion 0 f Belgium with a good deal of naive
ingenuity. On Saturday night, after a brisk
canter through her passports, etc., she dis-
mounted for her best anecdote. This is the
narrative of her service as a Belgian peasant
washing up in an Antwerp restaurant, where
she watched the German spies fall upon the
necks of their friends the invaders,
Young Wmekler, a brother of artist Ruby,
has been badly wounded. Ihe bullet took a
track through his shoulder; nevertheless he
is likely to be up and at ’em again later on.
Ruby, by the way, recently sent her proud
father a book from Boston, which repro-
duced her charming cobweb illustrations for
a new “Arabian Nights.”
Westralia has done this country and its
women the injustice of capturing an overseas

the seventh day of July, 1915, commenced
against you and that the plaintiff by her
petition and writ .of summons claims a
dissolution of her marriage with you and the
custody of the child of the said marriage
and FURTHER TAKE NOTICE that the

of July, 1915, authorised service of the said

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, July 2G, 1915.
My Dear Yarrie, —
There is amiable rivalry between the
wives of the G.-G. and the State Governor
in the way of good Imperial works. The
bun of greater merit looks like going to
Lady Stanley. The M.F. lady has plumped
steadily all along for the Red Cross, which
originally sent Susie’s sewing to English
soldiers. Then the opposition suddenly put
its money on an “appeal for wounded Aus-
tralian soldiers.” The thing was an unwit-
ting master-stroke. The other benefactress’s
efforts were pushed right out of the lime-
light. The modest little Stonington person-
age awoke to find herself billed and photo-
graphed and advertised in every town and
country paper like a new theatrical star.
The Stanleys’ recent party for the mem-
bers of the Victorian Red Cross executive
committee (which consists of weighty men
of business and war) reminds me of a
story. One afternoon a matron of the in-
quiring-chook species got mixed in her
numerous patriotic meeting dates. She
transpired at Government House and asked
the butler if the meeting was on. The
wretched fellow said “Yes,” and led her to a
curtained doorway. She bustled inside and
found a serious conclave of elderly men, in
tl\e posture of responsible citizens who are
settling the affairs of a nation. Did they
throw things at her or give her in charge
for intrusion? No! The Governor* ad-
vanced with a surprised but polite smile.
He offered his patrician hand, which she
took and waggled in an embarrassed man-
ner. The Empire-savers rose as one man,
and offered her the best chair. • Someone
rang for tea, and when the interloper had
been thoroughly soothed, she was parcelled
up for despatch by an aide and put in a
taxi. It was a pleasing interlude amid the
grim realities of war. I have not heard
what happened to the erring butler. Pro-
bably he was beheaded. But these military
punishments are always censored.
Reverting to the Stanley partv, it was
necessarily a solemn function. As an offset
to a surplusage of Importance the women’s
party dresses were all that is frivolously
beautiful. Lady Stanley looked adorable,
not to say heavenly, in Paradise blue silk
veiled with fine gold lace. Her sleeves hung
wide and long, like those of Henry. VIII.’s
time, and there was patterned black crepe
de chine swathed round in drapings some-
where in the design. Some quaint old
Florentine jewels, with emeralds and pearls
in the settings, added to the glory, the
emerald earrings being most fascinating.
The Madden dame in black and Mrs. Pea-
cock in black and white led the fashion of
dark frocking all round the room, though a
University, matron wore old gold satin" and
old gold lace, and Roma Clarke was in a
gorgeously-colored frock. Miss Ada Army-
tage was in black. There were also some
Grimwade dames and a Baillieu in black.
The party lasted decorously from 8.30 till
10, with a dignified divergence from talk
to the consumption of food. The new aide
with the large, lustrous eves looked. I
thought, a shade peevish. It may be that
he is finding the life less thrilling than that
at the Front. Di Gilio and his minions
played in the shadowy gallery of the hall
like hidden angels; and the wild flowers
and heath—specially ordered, on patriotic
grounds, from Mrs. Murray’s shop—pro-
duced a fine decorative effect.
How the'Gilds are changing, to be sui’c!
I have just met one of ancient lineage and
vast wealth rushing down Collins-street with
a billy in her hand. “Soup-stock to heat
up for the recruits,” she explained breath-
lessly. “I made it yesterday and skimmed
the fat off this morning. Mrs. Horribly-
Fashionable is waiting for me now at the
Depot. The Vacuum Oil people have lent
her a stove and supplied the oil.” And she
fled on, leaving me aghast.
Matron Tait, of the Melbourne Hospital,
is being badgered to death by inexperienced
young things who want to come into the
hospital and be got ready to nurse
returned soldiers. She has grown weary
of explaining that nurses of a few
months’ experience are useless for special
cases; also that an influx of more
than the regulation number of trainees
disturbs the whole delicate system of hospi-
tal management. She tvishes it to be known,
however, that she can use hundreds of
helpers when the rush comes. It would
be of incalculable value to her to have the
names and abodes of helpers whom she could
call upon when necessai’y. But it would
not be their job to nurse patients. They
would just do odd, but none the less useful,
jobs—mending, sorting linen, rolling band-
ages, and so on.
A few favored maidens have been taken
in ; they were called upon as vacancies hap-
pened in the ordinary routine. One of the
Grice girls got her summons in the middle of
a dinner party. She had to report on duty
(with full kit ready) at six o’clock the next
morning. Her name had been on the list
for ages, and she had begun to forget there
was a hospital in existence. Admiral
Bridges’s niece got in early and now is up
to her neck in the joyous business of scrub-
bing wards and washing Chinamen —the in-
variable work of the new chum. Any leis-
ured women following Mrs. Deakin’s appeal
for volunteers for work can enter their
names at the Central Depot. If they are
prepared to be taken on to help in hospital
toil, their names will be sent on from the
Central Depot to the Hospital.
I have been told of a wealthy man who
is condemned to life in a hash-house as a
punishment for wedding an undomesticated
woman ; and mother has reared a daughter
to “take after her.” He went off like a
bomb when the two ladies asked to be
allowed to go to the war and get work —
“any work.”
The Kennel Club show, whose pi-oceeds go
to (he Red Cross, occurred in the Exhibition
Building. Donald Mackinnon, as president,
showed the notabilities round, specialising,
if anything, in Governors. The latter, with
their respective wives, marched resolutely
through the sawdust and the din and made
polite signs of approval. Ordinary speech
was, of course, impossible, and the thought-
less aides had forgotten somehow to bring
the family megaphones. There was an official
luncheon party behind the Cats section,
which is less uproarious and smellful than
the Dog area.
Mrs. Elliot swept the board in her own
line. A field-spaniel, by name Bonny Bobby,
won her the Wavenor trophy of twenty
guineas. Mrs. J. E. Chubb’s black-and-tan
terriers were victorious on every side. Mrs.
Waters (in a lovely blue toque) put in some
masterly work with her imported Poms. So
did Miss Brown, but she was taken very i’ll
at the opening ceremony and had to he
removed in an ambulance. She is still at
St. Vincent’s Hospital near by. Her special
little Pom got a blue ribbon, though. Mrs.
David Syme, who has some wonderful Aus-
tralian terriers, was very much missed,
She was seriously ill—too much so to even
think about her beloved dogs—when I last
heard of her. Till a fortnight ago Mrs.
Syme was as active as any of her numerous
daughters-in-law. Then one low-ebb day
she was seized with a heart attack. So far,
however, her Scotch vitality has kept her
going.
Theodore Fink has been hard hit by the
death in action of his son Gordon. The young
man was athletic, bi-ainy and artistic, and
it was a sideways ending of a good Aus-
tltalian. In temperament he resembled the
recently-deceased barrister Wolfe Fink, one
of the kindliest men this side of the Line.
Geelong is chuckling again. Of course
everybody knows that Geelong is never
really awake. But, says the good-tempered
township, what odds —we can win the Y.G.
in our sleep! Young Jacka, the one Aus-
tralian to _ thus far get the gx*eat prize at
Gallipoli, is a Geelong youngster. Worse
still, he is a policeman’s child; and you
know how dull policemen are. And, worst
of all, he has always worked for the State;
and you know what the Government stroke
does for a man. This young Jacka, being
left in a trench by himself, killed all the
Turks who attacked it—shot five and
bayonetted two. If he had been born of
other parents in a really live town and had
been brought up differently he might have
done something unpleasant to those seven
Turks.
Howard Leon, lately in practice at
Torrensyille (S.A.), is going as an A.M.C.
officer to the Front, or as near as he can get
to it. His father is the ancient and massive
Sam Leon. K.C., Victorian Crown Prose-
cutor, a glance at whose large and piercing
eye and fiercely Roman nose has put the fear
of the law into many a wobbling witness for
the defence during the several decades of his
Victorian bar-practice.
Nigel Prichard (brother of- Katherine,
who raked in Hodder and Stftughton’s prize
for her first novel) is bound for the Front
and Glory. .Before leaving he got his medi-
cal degree.,
William Borwick, who used to teach viti-
culture to the youths at Dookie, is also leav-
ing for the customary address. He is a
brother of Leonard, the “poet-pianist.”
Lieutenant de la Poer Beresford, killed
in action in France last month, was a
grandson of the late Warrington Rogers,
K.C., Acting Judge in Victoria. Laura
Rogers married Lieutenant de la Poer
Beresford, R.N., and the dead soldier was
their son.
Reggie Power, who has been cursing at
his inability to get away for the last six
months, has now swept up the ravages
worked by the di-ought on his sheep-run, and
is leaving for the seat of trouble. He de-
parts with his wife early next month.
Eustace Miller, better known to his inti-
mates as EsrnA son of handsome Mrs.
Edward Miller, has just received a commis-
sion in the Grenadier Guards. He was
known to fame in this city chiefly .
kaiserish moustache, his nimble wit, and h a
dislike of anything bucolic. I d° n t fV, 1 . ®
he has shorn a sheep, or milked, or killed a
pig for years.
Frank Carse, whose mother was a lal-
kiner (and hence rich), is oil: to the war. me
British authorities want some experienced
gunners to serve as instructors, and r rank
is one of the chosen.
A color note from a girl at Alexandria
Just opposite is the house of the Sultans cousin.
The approach fascinates me. A long drive; on each
side lovely acacia trees and date palms; a thick
hedge of scarlet hibiscus; then wide marble steps
leading up to the palace. The small son of the
house is coming down the drive dressed in a white
Jersey suit, white tarboosh, white socks and strap
black shoes. Singing “Tipperary.” Servants in
many-colored robes. Now one of “the' ladies” comes
into the picture. She wears a black, nun-like cloak ;
a white, very thin chiffon yasniak; high-heeled
French shoes ; and carries a royal blue umbrella.
The Sultan arrived on May 23 to spend the sum-
mer here. The town was en fete. Right from
the station to the palace at Ras-el-tin there were
beautiful arches of welcome, and in between Vene-
tian poles, from which hung gold baskets filled with
bougainvillea creeper in gorgeous shades of purple
and pink. From every window along the Rue Cherif
Pasha, flags of the Allies waved ; and with them the
Egyptian flag, the new one, scarlet with three white
crescents on it. The crowds were kept back by police
on white chargers; their uniforms of white, with
gold belts, black riding boots and scarlet fez, looked
very smart.
The Sultan’s bodyguard surrounds him closely—a
magnificent lot of men, riding with the horses’ noses
nearly touching him. Since the attempt on his life
at Cairo they guard him jealously. The procession
went past at a hand-gallop, with the Yeomanry
(British) bringing up the rear and winning the most
cheers from natives and foreigners alike.
I got this last week :
Mr. T. Geo. Ellery, the Town Clerk-elect of Mel-
bourne, accompanied by Mrs. Ellery, arrived in Mel-
bourne by the express on Sunday. They are staying at
Menzies for the present. Mr. Ellery will commence
bis new duties on August 1.
It is as well that Melbourne should know,
even before the grim reality of town-clerking
begins, that Mr. Ellery is no blithe, hello-
old-man type of official.
A new scheme for raising Red Cross cash
has been started in Christchurch by Mrs.
Rhodes. The idea is for the knitting sex
to make a scarf for Queen Mary, no one
to knit more than a single row and to pay
a mere shilling for the privilege. Her Ex.
of Maorilaiul is to knit the first row, and
then the scarf is to start and keep going
at a shilling a row until it is finished.
Everyone who has a hand in it will write
her name and address in a book. When
the affair that keeps out the cold is finished
it will be forwarded to the Queen with the
books of signatures, and her Majesty will
be asked to hand the warmth to her son
at the Front. The best point about the
idea is that the wool to be used was grown
and tcoven in Maoriland. The worst com-
plaint the scheme is afflicted with is the
charge for having a cut. It might have
been a guinea a row and kept the lad just
as comfortable.
Maud Grantham is to give a concert in aid
of Australia’s wounded at the Assembly
Hall, in Collins-street, some time next
month ; and Hall Gye has promised to contri-
bute black-and-white work to the cause. The
tabernacle next Scots’ Church is a pretty
little hall, which is working up a concert-
business connection in spite of its name.
The Fairbairn ball for children —due on
the last night of July—seems to be working
up well. The latest helper is Grace Palotta,
who will lead the children in a grand march,
singing “Oh, Listen to the Band.”
Mr. and Mrs. Phillips Fox are preparing
hundreds of gum leaves with little sketched
designs for sale on Australia Day. Handled
in the best possible business way, the pic-
torial gum leaf should be a first-class seller.
The Oriental Hotel is doing its bit for
Australia Day, and the bit takes the shape
of an after-theatre supper-party.
The Amy Castles concert on August 11
promises to be a tremendous affair. All
the Governors, their women folk and the
Church are to be there.
Conductor Laver and the professional
players who have volunteered their services
expect a good financial result from the
Orchestral concert in aid of the Wounded
Soldiers’ Fund at the Town Hall next Satur-
day afternoon. The hall is given free, and
an enthusiastic concert-goer opened the plan
by booking 10 seats at £5 each. A fine body
of players from the Musicians’ Union are
rehearsing selections from English, French,
Russian and Italian orchestral works—just
to show that music doesn’t begin and end in
“Gott’s own country.”
Florence Ilodway is now painting Mrs.
George Armstrong, Melba’s good-looking
daughter-in-law. Florence’s portrait of Melba,
which is reproduced as a frontispiece in the
‘Gift Book,” is a fine effort, notwith-
standing that Melba proved a difficult model.
Berenice Edwell will give an exhibition of
miniatures and sketches in the Centreway
from the 3rd to the 17th August. Lady
Stanley will see the portrait of her
daughter Pamela, for the first time, at the
private view.
Grace Palotta has gone into the Clapp
sisters’ business. If she makes clothes as
well as she wears them she should be a gain
to the firm.
Commander Cornellie, he of the twirling
moustaches and bold blue eyes, writes from
Calais (9/6/’l5) : “My beautiful ship,
L’Avenir, is bound for Australia again, but
not with her former commander and cadets.
Her errand is to let Australian people see
that although we have fallen we are not
yet vanquished.” Cornellie and his Belgian
cadets are all in the fighting line.
Mrs. Masseran, the lady upon whom
Melbourne Sassiety depended to change its
figure every season, is in a French hospital
cooking for the patients. The latter are in
luck. Mrs. Masseran could distil ambrosia
out of an old boot when she was not making
fashionable figures out of lumps of fat.
From my Adelaide correspondent: —•
Mrs. Talbot Smith leaves by the Malwa next week.
You will remember that one of her sons was about
the first S. Australian to be killed at the Dardanelles,
and another is still there.
There was a monster recruiting meeting at Isling-
ton a day or so ago, when the men from the Govern-
ment Loco, works were addressed by the Governor,
Commissioner of Public Works Jackson, Colonel
Sandford and others. Meanwhile, one of those ex-
amples which are worth more than illimitable talk
is being set by Napier Birlcs, managing-director of
Charles Birks and Co. Although he is a married
man with a family he has elected to begin his mili-
tary career as a private in an Australian camp. His
ambition is to go to the Front as an Australian from
Australia. There is a less inspiring variety of
affluent Australian who prefers England and a com-
mission, and the likelihood of a pleasant job garri-
soning a county town. Another of the S. Australian
Birkses, Inspector of Agriculture in N. S. Wales, has
enlisted in that State.
There was an amateur show at the Theatre Royal
on Friday night in aid of the Red Cross and the
S.P.C.A. Grace Palotta was the only professional
who appeared. The dress-circle and stalls were
packed at 10s. 6d. and 6s. per head (mostly 10s.
6d.), and in the gallery you paid 3s. Everything
young and charming that wasn’t in the show was
selling things in front —sweets, devilled almonds,
flowers and programmes—and A. G. Fenner netted
just on £5OO by auctioning anything from a Teddy
Bear to an antique tea service. Lady Galway made
a speech from her box before the curtain went up.
News arrives of the death of Douglas Byard, of
Hahndorf, who was reported as missing .soon after
the Dardanelles landing. He was a son of Mr. and
Mrs. D. J. Byard, of Hahndorf, and a brother or
Mrs. Carew Reynell, whose husband is also at the
Dardanelles. The Frank Goodes’ son is severely
wounded.
The Mayor is still on the sick list, and his place
is being taken by Alderman Prosser.
A cable mentions that Baron Yon Bissing
is still uninterned in England ; though there
is an agitation on foot to have him run in,
and though lie has already been pushed in
and let out again once. Can this be the
fascinating Hun who flashed through Mel-
bourne a couple of years ago, creating an
aching void in many a maidenly heart when
lie left?
Sylvia, youngest daughter of the late
General Bingham, has become the wife of
an eligible Scotsman —Major Kennedy. The
pretty bride is a sister of Mrs. Tony Hor-
dern.
An echo of a cause celebre comes from the
marriage of Mrs. Ruby Wallace with one
William McGrath, an official in the Forestry
Department, Burma. After leaving these
shores the lady pursued a brief footlight
career at the London Gaiety Theatre.
The popular skipper of the Mongolia was
dined at a well-known cafe the other evening.
At about 9.30, while the revellers were
reminiscing hard on old college days,
the manager applied the move-on clause.
“But,” gasped one of the organisers
of the meal, “this is a caf'6, not
a pub—we don't want more fizz.”
After an awful pause the manager, more in
sorrow than in anger, asked the merrymakers
what consideration could be expected by a
dozen men whose wine bill fell short of £5
for the lot? Moral: Be careful where you
follow the King.
The Dowager-Imperial Armytage granch
mamma (forgive me if I have the title a little
wrong; but you will know whom I mean)
is returning. She was in a German eye-
hospital when the war broke out. There
is a story extant that her devoted grandson
Hugh, who is a British officer, went to
Germany to collect her, and managed to do
it without his identity being discovered.
Mrs. Purchase, wife of the Auckland man
who is Surgeon-Major in the A.M.C., re-
cently died of typhoid in Egypt. Having
been one of the early toilers at an Alex-
andria hospital, she was given a military
funeral.
Yours sincerely.
JOHANNA.

GROSS, FEVERISH, BILIOUS CHILDREN
NEED “CALIFORNIA SYRUP OF FIGS.”
ijook, Mother! See if the tongue is
coated; this denotes sluggish liver
and bowels.
Mother! Your child isn’t naturally cross
and peevish. See if the tongue is coated;
this is a sure sign that its little stomach,
liver and bowels need attention at once.
When listless, pale, feverish, “stuffy,” with
cold, throat sore; when the child has tainted
breath and doesn’t eat, sleep or act natur-
ally, or has stomach-ache or diarrhoea, re-
member, a gentle liver and bowel cleansing
should always be the first treatment given.
Nothing equals “California Syrup of Figs”
for children’s ills; give a teaspoonful, and
in a few hours all the waste matter, sour bile
and fermenting food clogged in the bowels
pass out of the system, an’, you have a
healthy and playful child again. All chil-
dren love this harmless, delicious “fruit laxa-
tive,” and it never fails to effect a good
“inside cleansing.” Directions for babies,
children of all ages and grown-ups are plainly
on the bottle.
Keep it handy in your home. A little
given to-day saves a sick child to-morrow,
but get the genuine. Ask your chemist for

fornia Fig Syrup Company/’ Counterfeits

“Outcast”: Re “Lagani Namo” ai d
Papuan drums (B. 29/4/’l5). They are not all of
the hour-glass pattern by any means. The Bamu
River specimens are almost straight, and open out
at the end opposite the tympanum into two widely
divergent jaws, very reminiscent of the crocodile’s
mouth, which, in fact, they are made to represent.
The carvings on the body of the instruments are made
to represent the croc.’s scales. These drums are
very highly' prized, and are traded all through the
Fly and as far as the Torres Straits islands. The
famous long drums of the inland swamp-dwellers of
the Fly', which take one man to hold and another to
beat, are also straight and of the same ty'pe. Like
nine-tenths of the western inland drums, they are
topped with scraped kangaroo skin, not snake or
lizard skin. The Orokaivas of the North use for
certain dances a very small drum, about a foot long,
and the mountain folk of the same district don’t
use a drum at all, but a length of split bamboo.

“R.T.N.”: If “Zaniia's” horses had been
broken in by niggers they would exhibit the same*
nervousness when first cauidit and handled by a white
man. It’s the “smell,” not the color, that they go
ty. I have frequently handled horses all day in a
dusty yard, and had them quite tractable. In th:
evening, after I had had a wash and a change, they
would be frightened of me till 1 had caught one and
got the horse smell on me again.
“Lagani Namo”: Between the Ply and
Bamu (Papua) I meandered into an obscure village.
An unusually big dubu house attracted me to spend
trade tobacco to gain entrance. Vatigi, they told
me, was the principal chief, and I had ready for him
a gay loin-cloth, mirror, tobacco and matches. The
interior of the dubu was so gloomy that I could
barely find my way between the cubicles, and there
seemed to be something ominous in the atmosphere.
My Kiwai “boy” and myself went cautiously side by
side until we’d penetrated about 60 yards, when day-
light filtered up through an exit in the floor. Then
we saw an elderly native bedaubed with purple and
yellow pigments, bedecked with scarlet hibiscus flow-
ers, a wealth of dog’s-teeth necklaces and pig’s tusks,
leaning back against a rack containing scores of
human skulls. I was face to face with Vatigi. He
stared stonily at me as I stepped forward to place my
presents on his knee. I asked why he had not come
to see us. He made no answer. Again I spoke to
him, and again there was no reply—-nothing except
ominous whisperings in the gloom. Then I knew
what had tingled my nostrils. It was dead man. As
I touched him he fell sideways, and my guide shouted
a warning and leaped through the exit in the floor.
As I dropped after him pandemonium cut loose, and
the way we bolted to the river and aboard the
schooner would have been a revelation to Jack Don-
aldson. In the hurry I forgot to ask what all the
fuss was about.
* * * *
“S.E.”: One Clouston, while prowling
about in the backblocks of the Nelson district
(M.L.) the other day, made a discovery that has set
all the naturalists of the country arguing. As quite
a. lot of the poultry that belongs to Maoriland had
not been seen for many years, the writers on bird lore
conveniently agreed that they were extinct. Lately
£BO was offered in Auckland for a pair of kakapos
—the big ground parrots that used to swarm on the
ranges. Clouston, who is an enthusiast in such
stock, found a great colony of the birds. With them
there were hundreds of the giant kiwi, a wingless
angel that was thought to have passed in its marble
some years ago. He also discovered a number of
smaller fry that are rare, and he brought back to
town a lot of specimens. One kakapo was 3ft. high.
The Government then did a thing that it had not
done in such a hurry for many a day. It at once
gazetted the place a sanctuary, so that the collector
and his shot-gun will be kept out. The discoverer ex-
plains that the place he found was ideal for the
birds, as some of the earth-worms were sft. long.
At time of writing the old controversy is raging as
to whether there may not be a stra.v moa or two in
some fastness.

“Boronia” : When the argument about the
length of vegetable roots was on the table I hadn’t the
limit handy. I now deposit it from the Yallingup
Caves, near Busselton (W.A.). It was about 4in. in
diameter, protruding from the centre of one of the
caverns. The guide informed me that the depth
from the surface of the hillside, under which the
caves are situated, to where the tendril makes its
appearance is 90ft., and from there to the floor 14ft.
This makes a length of 104 ft., and anything asserted
to be longer will have 1o be forwarded to my private
address before I will believe it.
“Devilfish”: Dunno if you can class the
conger-eel as a musical fish, but on the off-chance
I formally enter him for “A Nehoe’s” competition
(B. 24/6/’l5). He (the conger) barks like a
medium-tailed pup suffering from measles. As a
stable mate to the conger accept the silver perch
of the Murray. When caught it announces its dis-
approval by a grunting or snorting note. You might
call it “gnorting” and be about right.

“J.G.” : “Hardface” takes me to task (B.
24/6/’l5) for criticising the Broadford Shire (Vic.)
bumbles for slaughtering native trees on the Sydney-
road. “Hardface” says these trees were an unin-
viting lot. They were—when chopped down by the
Broadford vandals. Before the axes got going the
trees about there were equal to the very picturesque
patches on each side of the township. But even if the
native trees did not come up to specifications at
Broadford, will “Hardface” seriously admit that the
wretched imported planes will equal the trees
chopped out ? And if it was necessary to plant
more street trees, why were not some of our beauti-
ful eucalypt.s chosen instead of the much less desir-
able foreigners? “Hardface” further says that all
the trees that were on the Sydney-road from the
Dividing Range to beyond Seymour 30 years ago are
still there. If they are, will “Hardface” please
explain how the thousands of tree-stumps on the
sides of the road got there? 1 personally know
that hundreds of these stumps are all that remain
of beautiful shade trees that were growing on the
roadway less than five years ago. I don’t want
“Hardface” to pay my fare to Gippsland to see the
avenues there, as I have already sampled a good
many of them, and I will assert again that the
natural avenues that were once on the Sydney-road
were unexcelled by any. I would refer “Hardface”
to the last annual report of the Victorian Road'
Board, in which a full account is given of the roads
of Gippsland, the Western, Central and North-
eastern districts. Among the many illustrations
the only one that shows the beauties of a natural
avenue is a section of the Sydney-road between
.Wallan and Kilmore which so far has escaped the
murderous axe of those who despise Australian
things.

“Mujik” : One of the habits of the masked
owl, also known as the white or screech owl, is not
altogether ladylike:. . Whether perching on a public
tree or a private fence 'ft proceeds to disgorge num-
bers of pellets composed of the indigestible residue
from breakfast. The bird’s usual habit is to do this
at night, and beneath the perching place of such an
owl at Reedbeds (S.A.) a patient investigator recently
accumulated 172 complete pellets and 100 broken
ones. Then he got a gun, and later exhibited a
stuffed pellet-factory, supported by a treatise on the
contents thereof, at a meeting of the Royal Society at
Adelaide. Disintegration of numerous pellets and
arithmetical calculations showed that 1 the collector
had found the residue of a menu composed of assorted
span-ow, starling, lorikeet, mouse, rabbit, rat, bat,
frog, beetle and lizard. As probably many pellets
were negligently disgorged elsewhere, the conclusion
was arrived at that an average owl would during life
digest 640 sparrows, 64 starlings, 40 lorikeets, 20
other small birds, 1600 mice, 60 rabbits, four bats and
192 frogs, besides rats and insects. There seems a
plentiful supply of mouse to a meagre allowance of
bat in this ration, but the only thing to regret about
it ail is that there appears to be a complete absence
of Hun.
“Rag” : They used to make it pretty steep
in the West—and not so long ago, either. The
tobacconist lady, having handed me some silver as
change, suggested that I should take a half-crown
ticket in a raffle for a week-end girl and a bottle of
whisky to be drawn on the Saturday night! I fled and
told a boarding-house acquaintance of the incident. He
was unperturbed, merely mentioning that he had a
ticket in the gamble, too. About midnight on
Saturday he came unsteadily to my room. “Any
luck?’’ I asked. “No, ole man—hie! Duzzen
marrer ; got ’nurrer ticket—hie—for nex‘ Sarraday.”

A bushman who wandered into Victoria
Barracks (Sydney) to enlist a few mornings ago
was the Real Thing. He had done a 200-mile trip
to rail, partly on horseback, partly per boot. His
appearance yelled that he was from the Back.
Trailing behind him was his cattle dog, which had
stuck to him all the way down. It even followed
him into the recruiting rooms, and when the bush-
whacker undressed for medical inspection, sat tight
and kept an eye on the clothes. Then they both left
for Constantinople.

PITCHING CAMP.
[Fob The Bulletin.]
Behind the hills the sun goes down
And throws soft bands of light
Athwart the shadows, purple-brown,
Swift-fading into night.
From lofty gum-trees smooth and strong,
Re-echoed joyously,
The kookaburra’s even-song
Comes pealing clear and free.
And hark ! a chorus high and shrill
The surpliced magpies raise,
Waking their church upon the hill
With ringing trills of praise.
And from the twilight of a dell,
Soft-carpeted and green,
Rings suddenly a. tuneful bell
By elfin hands, unseen.
The black swans whir across the sky'.
With steady, rhythmic sound
The swift, well-ordered squadrons fly
Chanting their “Homeward Bound.”
Among the leaves the cheerful noise,
Of twitterings and cheeps,
And, eager for nocturnal joys,
A bright-eyed ’possum peeps.
A red fox calling for his mate
Makes empty echoes ring ;
A top-knot pigeon, homing late,
Flits by on noiseless wing.
The stars have put the day to flight,
And from a near-b.y hill
A mopoke calls “Good-night! Good-night!”
And all the Bush is still.
VICTORIA.
GORDON J. HOLMES.

“Rasmus": The bed of Lake Mackatle
(Q.), one of the places where the pelican builds lier
nest, on Chun station, was the Waterloo of Francis
Birtles-s overlanding car recently.- This enormous
(tract ashy ground, which becomes
inundated by the simultaneous overflow of King’s
Creek and the Georgina in high flood, has been filled
twice or thrice in the memory of man, the last time
many years ago. Writer has seen innumerable young
and okl pelicans and their eggs on the small islets
that formed in its floodwaters on the occasion of its
filling in 1894, and many strange orgies of the blacks,
who used to draft the half-grown birds, unable to
fly, and drive a mob to the edge of the lake, where
wood could be got for cooking them.

‘ Cane and Taws" : Only the country school-
teacher knows how strenuous things are in these days
o< drought, etc. This is how it was brought home to

“Hay” : We were a party of well-sinkers
out AA'est of Sunset, and, as usual, were glad to see
Bill when he arrived with a dray-load of fresh
rations from the head station and a supply of news
from the world. Seeing that we had been all hal’d
at it, Bill asked casually, “D’you blokes always work
on Sunday?” “No,” was our reply, “we always
knock off for Sunday. AA'e did yesterday.” “But
it’s Sunday to-day,” declared Bill. Then argument
raged fiercely till Bill convinced the crowd that we
had dropped a day somewhere. Fortunately, as the
hour was early, there was still time to repent; and,
as we had done all our usual Sunday jobs of hair-
cutting, hoot-mending, washing, etc., the day before,
we had a luxurious time reading up the armful of
literature with which we were refreshed by Bill
every six weeks. The only one who growled about
having two Sundays in one week was the cook,
because we insisted on having the orthodox duff
over again.

“MeG.” : To see a general spoiling of the
alimentary canal no better spot could he chosen
than a shearing shed. The day starts with tea or
cocoa, mixed with cake or bread, possibly a chop or
two; then to toil. At eight o’clock the rush for
breakfast would make a good “movie.” More tea
at ten; at mid-day a feed; tea again at two; at
four further tea and hunks of food; at six additional
assorted food; at eight supper. The rouseabout
whose work is light keeps going at this carnival of
grub as hard as the shearer, whose work is heavy
and performed in a stooping posture, which places a
hard strain on the digestive apparatus. This sys-
tem of feeding is all wrong, and one can only he
amazed that men so generally intelligent don’t see
through it. After a day or two they obey the
promptings of a clogged system and start hunting for
aperients. I know, worse luck, how shearing creates
desire for the stimulation of food, but I also know
that the whole business could be put on a more
rational basis, and our bingies given a better chance
of making good. This massive eating at the wrong-
time is the root reason for so many men breaking-
down, and the matter can only he settled by one of
the numerous McDaggs saying I’m a liar.
“Bendigonian”: Has “Pick” ever camped
out on a cold night in stringy-bark country? His
par about best burning woods (B. 8/7/15) makes
me doubt it. Stringy bark is about the worst
ever when it comes to burning; while gidgea (or
gidgy—the Abo. Phonetic Society has not quite
decided about tlie spelling) ensures almost perfect
combustion. Yarran and myall are good burners;
and. if flame is desired, what is the matter with
pine? As to gidgea “stinking,” the wood stinketh
not while burning. It is the leaves which hum —-
especially before rain. As a weather tipster, gidgea
is better than any AA'ragge and Bussell of them all.
I take it the test of a good lire is plenty of red,
and if so gidgea fills the bill.

his own way with Eve’s lover. Driving along a

“Pick”: The bush theory that lightning-
shattered timber is hard to burn, and that splinters
from it when used as toothpicks will cure ingrowing
toenail, may be dismissed without a character. It is
false. AVhen a green tree is struck the hunks of wood
pelted abroad will, of course, repel fire for a time,
because any green-split timber will long- retain its
sappiness .if left lying fiat upon the ground. But
when a dry tree receives the bolt it not only isn’t
rendered inflammable, but in half the cases, or there-
abouts, it takes fire and is consumed. AVax matches
have had many libels posted up against them on
account of bush fires that had their origin in this
way, especially ip striiig-y-bnrk country.
“Wayback”: In tlie scientific method of
dealing with finance and other issues of great
moment Parliament is a long way behind- our
Literary Institute. AA’e meet every month. To put
in another billiard table, to close the billiard room
altogether, or to just sack the marker; to raise the
subscription or to lower it; to borrow money from
the building fund (which is about £25 in credit)
—or to let the general account sink or swim; to go
and seize the Premier and illtreat him for stopping
payment of the subsidy, or to just pass a severely -
worded motion of censure on him —these questions
are raised at every meeting and dealt with, to-
gether, one after another; upside down and inside
out. AVhen the extent to. which subscriptions should
be raised is under discussion, up pops a member
with the bright idea that if the wall between the
billiard room and the recreation room were knocked
out and another table put in it would make a vast
difference, as it is a well-known fact that men play
better billiards if there is another table in the
room. That brings up again the question of sack-
ing the marker, followed by detailed accounts of
many instances in which players have scored much
better when marking for themselves. “Mr. Chair-
man,” protests tlie oldest man on the committee,
“I think we are getting a bit off the tract. Now.
if we held little entertainments during the summer
evenings out on the vacant land next to this build-
ing, they could be made to be a great success. It
would not be necessary to have a piano there. AVe
could have music and refreshments. Of course, a
loc of people would stand outside and loolc over the
fence, but we would not want them; the ones we
wanted would be inside among tlie trees and tlie
Chinese lanterns.” He is jostled for tlie floor by
someone wanting to know where the sense is in
paying one bank 6 p.e. interest on a loan while the
Institute has £25 to credit in tlie Savings Bank at
3$ p.e. AA'liv not pay off the loan ? “Ah,” puts in
another member, “but if we pay it off how do we
know the Government will let us borrow again?
That’s the point.” So it is, and it lias to he dealt
with for an hour and a half every month.
“Firestick”: Have any Abo.-scribes ever
seen a horse with a perfectly straight backbone?
A couple have recently been imported by E. P. Tapp,
of Yaneannia station (N.S.AAL), from A’ictoria. The
backbones of these beasts are absolutely straight,
solid hone, having no joints, from the coupling to
tlie wither. They were the property of, and bred by,
Mr. Tapp’s father in A’ie. The ancestral dam was
originally purchased in Queensland, and herself had
a straight backbone, which peculiarity has been in-
herited by all her progeny. The animals are a par-
ticularly good sort, their pet pace is trotting, and they
are excellent harness horses.

“Hay”: As one who has sampled many
kinds of firing I nominate belali, or, as it is called
in some parts, black oak, as easily first. One may
hank up the camp fire and find a good start for a
fresh light-up after a week’s absence. Once set
alight "a belah will burn right out to the ends
of the roots. I’ve seen a fallen tree traced upon the
ground in fine ashes to the ends of its smallest
branches. But let the bushman beware how lie
uses his well-sharpened axe upon the herb- —green
or dry—for its short-grained density will gap (lie
bes't of axes, unless ground short after the manner
of a cold chisel. Like the mallee root it makes the
clean, hot, smokeless fire beloved by the Outback
chef. By the way, the hush cook’s pet, aversion
is sandal-wood, which blazes all the time and blackens
everything within range with a horrible kind of
soot. The abo. prized the sandalwood because of its
habit of keeping alight, and he used to find it easier
to carry a stick of it along with him when Shifting
camp than to make a fire by the tedious process of
rubbing a twig in a cleft log.

“W.C.F.”: Evidently “G.J.H.” (B.
10/6/’l5) knows as little about revolvers as he says
“Bad Australian” knows about auto, pistols. No
revolver will throw 1000 yards? Bless me, a revolver
using as small a -cartridge as the .22 long will do
that. I haven't seen an auto, pistol yet to equal the
.32-20 Colt revolver, which has a higher velocity than
any pistol of the same calibre. In the Territory and
the parts of Queensland where men still carry firearms
there are 50 revolvers to every pistol. These are the
places where reliability is needed, and it has been
proved there that the pistol is not so trustworthy as
the revolver.
“Silas Snell”: It had been hot and dry in
and about Halligun's Flat. Ruin and empty plates
stared the cookies in the face, and the terrific effort of
the time was not to mollify and soften the mortgage,
but to pay “the insurance" in anticipation of fire.
“ ’Twill niver jist bur-rn, rnar-rlt me wor-rd,” said
Minahan. “ ’Twill ixplode.” At this crisis Mann had
his fire. Minahan was there. Minahan was nervous
and reproachful. He cornered Mann with anxious
complainings. “How c’u’d yeh do ut, Antlirew?" he
said. “Tliim’s har-rd times fer the Insurance. Have
yeh no consideration fer yer neighbors at all, thirn
iv yer own blood V religion? Wid the many fires
’n’ the hardness iv the times, ’tis possible the Insur-
ance can’t afford it.” A few nights later Peter
Wheelan had his fire. Minahan nearly took a fit.
Wheel an, like Mann, was insured in the same com-
pany as himself. “We can afford t’ wait no longer,
Maggie,” said Minahan to his missus. “Sure, if we
do there’ll be nothin’ left for us. The Insurance is
near impty, I’m thinkin’.” Then Minahan pushed a
firestick under his front-room floor, and rode across
L for leather to cateli the 9.15 train for Melbourne,
intent (upon putting in his claim before Wheelan
could come along and clean out the last few pounds
the Insurance possessed. Minahan was up bright and
early; he reached the insurance office 40 minutes
before opening time. Wheelan was there, sitting on
the step. Minahan’s disgust had the tragic force of a
Greek drama. “Yeh dir-rty bla-guar-rd !” he said;
“you thievin’, poor, lawless scounthril!” said he.
“Ye’re here t’ rob me iv me money, are yeh? Ye’d
take the last penny they have, ’iv lave me homeless
wid me sorrers. But ye’ll hate me first, Peter
Wheelan; ye’ll bate a better man first, so you will.”
He rushed Peter, and a great fight raged on the foot-
path, lasting for half an hour. Minahan was
carried off to the hospital in an unconscious state.
Ho could not get to the insurance office till next day,
when he arrived with six stitches in his scalp and
his head in a bundle. “Is Peter Wheelan paid?”
cried he. “Have ye anny money left at all? I’m
Uanyill Minahan, of Hallig-an’s Flat, ’n’ me house is
bur-rned on me.” “Daniel Minahan?” said the
clerk. “There's a letter for you.” Mrs. Minahan
had addressed the letter to Dan, not knowing how
else to find him in Melbourne. The letter ran:
“Come home, will yeh, ’n’ not be makin’ a born
fool iv yersilf, Danyill Minahan? Yer fire wint out!”
Minahan took home a sore heart and a sore head,
llis house Was not even singed.
THE GRIMMEST SACRIFICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When we encountered the" battleship Majestic we
saw through our periscope that the crew were pre-
paring’ lunch. We-hesitated, but duty triumphed over
geh|huent, and we torpedoed her.
-—German communique.
AYe've heaped a hitter load of scorn
On German heroes, I’m afraid
Their massacre of babes newborn,
Their actions of all honor shorn
AA'hen making a domestic raid,
Their way with Belgian wife and maid.
But here we find the Kaiser’s tribe
One kindly weakness can confess,
And when in future we’d describe
Their feats with oath and biting gibe,
Let’s not forget for grub they stress
A very human tenderness.
They saw the sailors cutting meat,
Saw sausages, and pork, and fruit,
And many things to drink and eat,
Then thought a moment of retreat,
Of sparing goods they couldn’t loot.
Stern Duty shrieked before they’d shoot!
They fired, and there before their eyes
They saw the fat polonies fall,
Saw floating chops and portly pies.
Aeh Gott, thefr agonising cries!
They’d sacrificed at duty’s call
Their holiest sentiment of all!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

Lustrous Hair.
A simple lotion that can he made at home,
and which will positively force the hair to
grow, is within the reach ot everybody. It
stops the hair from falling out, clears the
scalp of dandruff, and leaves a clean, tingling

Removing Her Skin.
The quickest and surest wmy to make a
good complexion out of a bad one is to actu-
ally remove the thin veil of faded or dis-
colored outer skin from the face. This can be
done, easily, safely and privately by any
woman. The process is one of gentle absorp-
tion. . , .
O o t some pure mercolised wax from your
chemist, and apply it at night like cold cream,
washing it off in the morning. In a few days
the mcrcolide in the wax will have absorbed
the disfiguring cuticle, revealing the fresh
young skin underneath: you will have a beau-
tiful, clear, natural complexion. The process
is pleasant and harmless, making the face look
brilliantly attractive and youthful. It also
effectually removes such blemishes as freckles,
tan, moth patches, liver spots, pimples, etc.
Every woman should keep a little mercolised
wax on hand, as this simple, old-fashioned
remedy is the. best complexion beautifier and
preserver known.

Grey Hair Unnecessary.
One need not resort to the very questionable
expedient of hair dye in order not to have
grey hair. The grey hair can easily be
changed back to a natural color in a few days
time merely by the application of a simple,
old-fashioned and perfectly harmless home-
made lotion. Procure from your chemist an
ounce of tammalite concentrate and mix it
with four ounces of bay rum. Apply this to
the hair a few times with a small sponge and
you will soon have the pleasure of seeing your
grey hair gradually darkening to the desired
shade. The lotion is pleasant, not sticky or
greasy, and does not injure the hair in any
way- ... .. .

almost saucy look, besides accentuating the arch of

of shoe that, in a pleasant variety of styles
