“T. 0.1.”: In that long list of Poverty
Pointers drafted by “Busk” (B. 15/7/’l5)
the oldest in length of service, as in years,
is W. H. Ford, now living at North Fitz-
roy. Born in 1830 he appeared at Astley’s,
London, with his mother in or about 1845.
Coming to Sydney in 1847, he could not
get an engagement, so took the advice of the
old Vic.’s stage doorkeeper and accepted a
job up-country. The job was on Tarampa
station, where to amuse the kiddies he con-
verted the woolslied into a theatre, “The
Royal.” Some beef-buying butchers were
present at one of the shows there, and ad-
vised Ford to go to Brisbane, lie did so,
and built the first theatre in that city, llis
company, mostly amateurs, made a living,
but not much more. Ford pioneered the
drama as far as Townsville and Ravens-
wood. Likewise he was first manager and
director of the Sydney Music Hall, on the
site of which now stands the Tivoli. The
old ’un at 85 is chirpy, but a matinee “ben.”
might not come amiss. In Brooke’s and
Barry Sullivan’s day he was wardrobe
keeper, taking his stage business as a sort
of side issue.
Ivy Shilling has to work off an old, full-
flavored joke in the latest London fugitive
success, “Watch Your Step”—American-
built. She has a small part as a laundress
in a street window with a double shutter.
With one shutter closed, you can read “I
shall undress in this window at” —such an
hour. A tnen-only crowd gathers. Then
Ivy casually closes the other shutter, and
the whole advertisement can be read. “I”
is an initial, “shall” the end of the name
Marshall, “undress” the end of the word
“laundress,” and so on.
The Co-op. Film Exchange of Australia
is stepping out into the 5-reel film business
now that it has reached the div.-paying
stage. Two films just imported, “Hearts
in Exile” and “The Dollar Mark,” measure
2i hours each, and look like good money-
makers. The growing demand in pictures
is for a long meal of melodrama, with
astounding scenic effects to compensate for
the silence of the mummers.
“Cockaigne”: A play by the late Mon-
signor Benson has just been refused the
Chamberlain’s license in London. Of course
it is quite innocent. The only ground of
objection is the introduction of the \ irgin
Mary. Yet the Chamberlain is quite liable
to license a smutty musical comedy. Ben-
son’s play is called “The Upper Room,” evi-
dently suggested by Jerome’s “Third Floor
Back.” A representation had been given in
Manchester* by the Lancashire Catholic
Players’ Society, with the approbation of
Bishop Casartelli. The critics were, unani-
mous in praise; and doubtless the script will
be sent to Australia so that the thing may
be done by Catholic societies here. Ros-
tand, by the way, went a step further by
introducing Christ in “La Samaritaine,”
written for Bernhardt, and beautifully acted
at the Renaissance Theatre, Paris, on a
Good Friday night.
“Haresfoot”: G. V. Brooke was born in
1818, Coppin in ISI9. They first met about
1840, when Coppin was a low comedian in
the English provincial company of Daven-
port, the original of Vincent Crummies. Cop-
pin, by the way, was possibly the Folair of
“Nicholas Nickleby.” He acted Osric to the
Ilamlet of Brooke, who was always a star.
For 14 years afterwards he kept Brooke
in mind as having more genius than any per-
former he had seen. In 1854 he went to
England, and signed articles with Brooke
behind the scenes of the Birmingham Theatre,
then managed by Mr. and Mrs. Rignold,
parents of George and William. The engage-
ment was for 100 nights. Brooke engaged
Fanny Cathcart as his apprentice at £2 a
week. Fanny’s father and her brothers Row-
ley and James F. (our Australian friend)
handed her in confidence to the charge of
Mrs. Brooke. She was engaged to Bob Heir,
but Brooke would not take him —he was too
inexperienced. However, he followed. Cop-
pin had no wife, and wanted to marry
Fanny; but she was true to her old love.
Later there was a separation; but harmony
was restored by Brooke, Coppin, Heir and
Mrs. Heir all performing together in “The
Honeymoon.”
“C. Ockney”: It seems curious that Aus-
tralia has never seen “Ruddigore,” the G.
and S. opera that contains, to my mind,
some of the most delightful numbers Arthur
Sullivan ever composed. True, it was looked
upon as a failure in London; nevertheless,
according to Gilbert, it brought them in a
lot of money. The title, maybe, is against
it. When originally produced it was even
more unattractive —“Ruddy Gore.” On the
first night at the Savoy the gallery shouted
on the fall of the curtain: -'Take it off and
give us the ‘Mikado.’ ” The notices next
morning were not encouraging. Just about
that time there had been a good deal of
pirating of the Gilbert-and-Sullivan works.
Said Labouchere: “ ‘Ruddigore’ could be left
on the Embankment all night and nobody
would touch it.” All the same, it’s a thing
well worth hearing, and Australia ought to
be given the chance.
“‘Scallywag” c Graham Moffat’s “Scrape
o’ the Feu” leaves me wondering what our
own play-builders are a-doing. Or is it the
fact that a play with a universal theme
must be given a foreign setting to succeed
in Australia? The bush teems with obstinate
grandfathers and benevolent grandmothers,
and has many an Alec Ingles whose prodi-
gality has not been without dramatic in-
terest. But —a word of warning.. The
smart Alecs who fool innocent girls out
beyond the Darling seldom live to make
fortunes and come home triumphant like
they do in Bonnie Scotland. They have
a habit of falling down abandoned shafts;
they get knocked about distressingly after
the pub closes; sometimes, even, the pro-
digal makes research at the wrong end of
a Winchester. Most of them, however,
whether Scottish or civilised, issue invita-
tions to a wedding. I cannot recall one
Australian who had the crimson effrontery
to stroll home five years too late for the
wedding. Perhaps they go to Scotland to
repair the wastage due to the influence of
civilisation on conventional immorality, and
give Graham Moffat inspiration.
To be in the fashion. Edward Knoblauch,
author of “Kismet,” has also written a war
play. It is called “Marie Odile,” and has
made a hit in N’York, where David Belasco
produced it with sumptuous scenery. Tree,
who blundered badly in declining “Kismet.”
has secured the new piece for London. The
action takes place in a convent in Alsace.
A feature is made of convent life, the reci-
tation of the rosary, the chanting of psalms
and the reception of a novice being among
the scenes. Needless to say, the convent
is invaded. The irruption leads up to “a
series of events of a decidedly unconven-
tional and somewhat daring description”;
but, on the unimpeachable word of the press
agent, “Mr. Knoblauch has handled a sub-
ject not by any means free from danger
with rare delicacy and skill.”
The clean-shave movement seems to have
taken hold of the seniors now in the U.S.A.
The “Uncle Sam” goatee beard of caricature
became extinct in the cities long ago; and
the typical American politician became a
heavy man with a heavy moustache. Now,
however, a photo of Wilson, Bryan and the
Cabinet discussing the Lusitania question
shows one small moustache, one set of mut-
ton-chop whiskers, and all the rest of the
table-full as hare-faced as Holman.
“Chemist”: John Bull has lately pro-
hibited the export of acetyl-salicylic acid,
carbolic acid, salicylic acid, phenacetitt,
phenazone, salicylate of soda and naph-
thaline. Most of these things are
largely used in medicine, being daily
prescribed by doctors the world over.
They have been mainly manufactured in
Germany. Why not in Australia? They
are nearly all derivatives of coal tar. and
the only reason why we may have to go
without them is that we have neglected our
opportunities, while Germany has seized all
the opportunities it saw. Of cyanide we
are one of the largest users in the world,
and the materials are all here and mostly
wasted. Yet every particle of its supplies
of this poison Australia gets from the for-
eigner. The old gag was that we didn’t
bother about making these little things
because we could find much more profitable
employment in primary industries. Isn’t
it about time we dropped that plausible
yarn, in view of the fact that one State_Gov-
ernment alone is borrowing money at 5 per
cent, frankly in order to give its supporters
something to do?
An Australian belonging to the Supply
Column wrote from the south coast of Eng-
land on June 1 and said he was ohe of half
a million trained soldiers who were being
held in readiness to move for Flanders at a
moment’s notice. The letter was held up by
the censor and was only delivered last week.
From an Adelaide correspondent:—
London Opinion throws a light on the Orpen picture,
“Sowing New Seed,” which Adelaide Art Gallery
bought—or, rather, the artist, Rose McPherson,
bought—and which, after being hung for a time, was
withdrawn. The local objections were: (1) Sheer
ugliness, (2) lack of interest, and (on the part of a
few) nudity, though there are really artistic nudes
in the same gallery that raise no protest. The
London writer dropped in on Orpen, and mentioned
the picture and the row in Adelaide. “I am afraid
some of them don’t quite Understand it,” he smiled.
“I am not quite sure that I do myself.” A badly-
drawn caricature of a purely-loeal squabble, and so
obscure a caricature that the artist isn’t sure what
it means himself!
One of the Australian Red Cross nurses
who were decorated at Buckingham Palace
last week told the Times that “the King
looked very tired.” Three hundred persons
had been invested with one order or another,
and the nurses came at the end of the pro-
cession. It would be a fair thing for his
Gracious to feel very tired.
Lieut. W. S. Kent-lluglies, wounded by
the Turk, is a son of the Collins-street
doctor, now in England with the Australian
Field Hospital, and a nephew of Canon
Hughes, of Eastern Hill, and of lolonel
F. G. Hughes, of the Light Horse. SToung
Hughes’s wound is not his only disl fetion.
He was the first student to enlist ) Vic-
toria, being captain of the Grammal fcphool
when the German began to show f £ real
self to the world.
W. 11. Page, formerly member of t_e Tas.
Assembly, and before that a judge in India,
had a son, Bernard, who is now at the
Front as a military chaplain. When the
ex-Judge left Tasmania he settled in Bel-
gium—at Bruges—and lie and his family
were there when the Germans came in, and
are there still and cannot get out. In a
letter lately sent thi3 way by Father Bernard,
who is with a cavalry division, the writer
says he has not heard from his parents for
seven months, and can find out nothing
about them. A Jesuit of his acquaintance,
however, once saw them and told him they
looked “all right.” But the son of the ex-
Tasmanian says he does not know how they
manage to live, as they had no money in
any Belgian bank, and none to talk about
in their pockets. Another of the Page boys,
Leo, is in the Royal Flying Corps.
A LEADER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The crowd about the depbt stood,
And talked ot war. Near by, a man—
One of the acting brotherhood,
His coat bedecked with astrachan—■
Poised on the kerb. All ill-content,
Upon the day a scowl he bent.
I’d seen him oft in stormy plays
Of blood and thunder, when he wore
A uniform with gold ablaze,
And waded deep in foreign gore.
“Nay, nay,” said he, “I will not start 1
These cads have no respect for art!
“I offered, sir, to face the foe —■
You know the training I have had.
They told me I was free to go,
But only in the ranks. By gad,
A private, I! Fine thing, indeed,
For one who’s always playing lead!”
VICTORIA.

ABRIDGED PROSPECTUS
OF
Commonwealth of Australia
£5,000,000 4J Per Cent. War Loan, 1925
PRICE OF ISSUE, PAR.
The Subscription List will be closed on 31st AUGUST, 1915.
THE LOAN IS TO BE UTILISED BY THE COMMONWEALTH FOR WAR PURPOSES ONLY AND
WILL TAKE THE FORM EITHER OF INSCRIBED STOCK OR TREASURY BONDS, AT THE OPTION
OF THE SUBSCRIBER.
Bonds and Transfers of Inscribed Stock will be free of Commonwealth and State Stamp Duty and
Interest will be free of Commonwealth and State Income Tax. Bonds and Inscribed Stock will be
accepted at par in payment of Probate and Succession Duty due to the Commonwealth. The Issue is a
Trustee Investment, and Trustees may invest therein.
Bonds will be issued in denominations of £lO, £lOO, £5OO, and £lOOO, and will be payable to
Bearer, or Stock will be Inscribed in amounts of £lOO or any multiple thereof.
The Loan will be repaid at par at the Offices of the Commonwealth Bank of Australia IN. SYDNEY,
MELBOURNE, BRISBANE, ADELAIDE, PERTH or HOBART, on 15th DECEMBER, 1925.
Interest on Bonds and Stock will be paid half -yea ly on 15th June and 15th December.
A FULL HALF-YEAR’S INTEREST MULL BE PAID ON 15th DECEMBER, 1915.
Both Interest Warrants and Coupons will be payable free of exchange at any Branch of the Common-
wealth Bank of Australia throughout the Commonwealth.
The above Loan is authorised under ACT OF PARLIAMENT of the Commonwealth of Australia, and
both Capital and Interest, will be a charge upon the Consolidated Revenue of the Commonwealth.
The COMMONWEALTH BANK OF AUSTRALIA is authorised on behalf of the TREASURER OF
THE COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA to receive applications for the above Loan.
Applications, which must be accompanied by a Deposit of £lO per cent., and which should be on
the special printed forms, will be received at any Branch of the Commonwealth Bank of Australia, or may
lie forwarded through the medium of Head Offices and Branches of ANY BANK, or anv recognised
STOCKBROKER or any POST-OFFICE WHERE MONEY ORDER BUSINESS IS CONDUCTED.
Instalments will also be received at any Branch of the COMMONWEALTH BANK OF AUSTRALIA,
or may be forwarded through the medium of Head Offices and Branches of ANY BANK, or any recognised
STOCKBROKER or any POST OFFICE WHERE MONEY ORDER BUSINESS IS CONDUCTED, and
will be payable as follows: £lO per cent, on Application; £l5 per cent, on 15th September, 1915: £2O
per cent, on 15th October, 1915 ; £25 per cent, on 15th November, 1915; £3O per cent, on 30th
November, 1915.
Any or all of the Instalments may be prepaid. In case of default in the payment of any instalment
at its due date, the deposit and instalments previously paid will be liable to forfeiture.
The Stock will be inscribed in accordance with the provisions of the Commonwealth Inscribed Stock
Act 1911-15, and Inscription Books of the Loan will be kept at the Commonwealth Treasury at the Seat
of Government, where all transfers of Stock will be made.
Provisional Receipts will be issued on payment of £lO per cent, on application, and Scrip Certificates
to Bearer with Coupon attached for Interest payable on 15th December, 1915, will be issued in exchange
for such Provisional Receipts. As soon as these Scrip Certificates have been paid in full and payment
indorsed accordingly, they may be exchanged for Bonds to Bearer (as soon as the latter can be
prepared) or may be converted into Inscribed Stock in denominations of £lOO or any multiple thereof.
Holders of Inscribed Stock will have the right to convert into Bonds and holders of Bonds will have
the right to convert into Inscribed Stock at any time on application in writing to the Commonwealth
Treasury, Melbourne, or any Branch of the Commonwealth Bank of Australia. *
Recognised STOCKBROKERS will be allowed a Commission of ss. per £lOO on allotments made
in respect of applications for this issue, received from them, and which bear their Stamp.
COMMONWEALTH BANK OF AUSTRALIA,
24th July, 1915.
Application Forms can be obtained from any Bank throughout the Common-
wealth, or any Post Office where Money Order Business is conducted,
or THIS FORM MAY BE USED.

AUDITORS’ REPORT.
We have examined the above Balance-sheet, made up to 30th .Tune. 1015, and report that we find the several items therein stated to conform to the Balances in the
General Ledger, and the Balance-sheets of the respective Branches of the Bank collectively.
We have counted the cash and examined the Bills held by the Bank at the Head Office in Sydney on the 30th June, 1015, and hereby certify that we have found them
correct. We further certify that all Vouchers, Accounts and Documents necessary for verifying the Balance-sheet have been submitted to us.
The Cash, Bills and other Securities held at the Branch Banks in New South Wales and Queensland on the date of Balancing have been certified to as being correct
by the usual documents, and the figures of the Balance-sheet of the London Branch have been received by cable.
In our opinion the above is a full and fair Balanpe-»s}p?et, containing the particulars required by the Articles of Association of The Australian Bank of Commerce,
Limited, and properly drawn up so as to exhibit a true and correct view of the state of the Company’s affairs as shown by the Books of the Company.
WILLIAM CRAIG, F.C.P.A.. \

SYDNEY CARRIER
EULOGISES DR. MORSE’S INDIAN
ROOT PILLS AS A LIVER MEDICINE.
“I have great pleasure in giving my testi-
mony to the benefits derived from Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills,” writes Mr. John
Hackland of 41 Westmoreland Street,
Forest Lodge, Sydney. “For years I suf-
fered with a very bad liver. Only those so
afflicted know what agony one has to endure.
Constant sick headaches that made life a
burden and sleep impossible; I could not
attend to business or speak a civil word to
anyone, always in a bad temper, unable to
eat or drink anything without due consider-
ation as to how it would affect my liver, for
fear of a bilious attack. I tried a lot of
medicines, for I have been a member of a
Benefit Society for over 25 years, and got
advice from some of the very good doctors
of my lodge. Nothing gave me any relief
until a friend told me to try Indian Root
Pills ; I did so and found relief after the
first night I took two. I kept on for a
couple of weeks taking two each night on
o-oing to bed j now I feel as well as I did 20
years ago. I still take a pill occasionally,
and by so doing never have the dreadful sick
headache with that irritable, cantakerous
feeling that made me quite unfit for work or
pleasure. To those who suffer like I did I
would advise a course of Indian Root Pills,
they have made a different man of me and
they will soon feel as well as I do. This is
my' own unsolicited testimony, and you have
my full permission to use it for advertising
purposes.— John llackland.''
POLITICAL
POINTS
By the middle of July Victoria had so
eaten into its stocks of meat in cold stor-
age, the current supply of fat stock being
quite unequal to the demand, that before
spring can bring meat into the market there
threatens to be almost a famine. In this
connection it is interesting to note that
nothing has happened to Angliss, M.L.C.,
who, by exporting 37,000 carcases in June
in defiance of the Government order, helped
to cause this shortage and produce the in-
creased prices. The smallest thing the
Government can do is.to seize at June prices
the meat now held in stock by Angliss. It
is a mere accident, no doubt, that he holds
the greater part of the present stored supply.
THE GENTLE PROD.
RISING YOUNG ACTOR: “Did you see my
photo, in the paper yesterday?”
THE LADY (toho thinks every man should he
at the Front) : "No; were you killed or
wounded?”
Though George Black’s experience with
the N.S.W. State trawlers has been so shock-
ing, still the things have been valuable in a
way. They have shown, perhaps, that the
State hasn’t a hope of making the business
a success with-the present imported vessels.
But they have shown as well that there is a
great store of fish to bring in, and that men
working for themselves could make a fine fat
thing. There are dozens of lines that lend
themselves to co-operative toil, and George
Black’s stormy experiment proves trawling
to be one of them.
The Vaughan Government is at grips
with S.A.’s infrequent fat ox and occasional
well-nourished sheep, a State retail butchery
having been established in Adelaide, and
proud Liberal dames resort to it for chops
and steak. The sight of a haughty Libera 1-
ette making dignified exit from a Sosh meat
depot with a bulging kit of raw flesh must
stir all right-thinking people to realise h >w
rapidly things are going from bad to worse.
A “representative deputation,” including
a score of pseudo-Protectionist members of
Australia’s Parliament, interviewed Customs
Minister Tudor lately, and got a less chilly
reception than it deserved. In the interests
of the “poor farmer” it urged the immediate
remission of the 10 per cent, duty on jute
goods. These articles—bran bags, sacks,
wool packs, etc. —are made by the cheap
colored labor which Minister Tudor and
most other Australians abhor and will not
admit to the Commonwealth; and the sacks
which might be, and once were, made here
are "protected” against such labor by a 10
per cent, duty! If the Government was
honest enough to carry out its declared
policy—White Australia and effective Pro-
tection—it would levy a duty of 60 or 70
per cent, on manufactured jute goods. Then
the white Australian would get a lot more
work and the farmer would get his bags for
a lot less than 9s. per dozen. A duty of 10
per cent, is merely annoying the farmer and
fooling the electors who sent professing Pro-
tectionists to Parliament.
Some men call tenders for trouble. There
is a vacancy in N. S. IVales for an assistant
Railway Commissioner, and all the “heads”
are in the scramble for the job. Yet, accord-
ing to a return lately made to the Assembly,
awards of Wages Boards, etc., last year
added £543.000 to the bill the Commissioners
have to pay; and when they did what any
business man would do and put up the
charge for the service that was costing more,
there was a yell of protest from Bourke to
Byron Bay, and the railway bosses were told
that the right thing to do was to meet the
higher cost with lower fares, so that people
would give their truck of potatoes two trips
instead of one. If a Wages Board which
found that the cost of living was rising
told a union -that the best thing for the
trouble was to reduce wages so as to encour-
age employers to hand out more work, there
would be a request for a commission to in-
quire into the Board’s state of health.
“I see they’re still talking about collaring
German trade,” said the chemist as he
opened the showcase where the toothbrushes
were. He picked a sample out of the cheap
tray and passed it over for inspection.
“That’s a Jap-made brush. Three months
after war was declared, and just when our
stocks of these things were running out, the
Jap’s representatives were in here with sam-
ples looking for orders. Now I suppose
they’ve got a jiu-jitsu hold on the business.
Of course, when an- Australian bumps up
against this sort of thing he knows there is
no need to hurry with the tariff. All we’ve
got to do is to sit down and wait for the
war to finish. There’s no hurry—no hurry
at all!”
Attorney-General Hughes woke up last
week to the horrible alleged fact that,
whereas Germany is getting all the zinc
(spelter) it wants for under £3O per ton,
the British Government has to pay more
than £lOO. Hitting out wildly, he decided
that there must he a Metal Exchange, con-
trolled for the present by the Government,
and open only to pro-Britisli. Germany, if
it ever hears of this, will enjoy another
laugh. Hughes’s idea seems to he that the
Broken Hill cos. are not selling to English
buyers because, out of pure cussedness
apparently, those cos. will not annul their
old contracts with German firms. The
simple fact is that there are no English
buyers worth mentioning. As with so many
other industries, freetrade Britain let this
spelter business slip right away to protec-
tionist Germany. Fifteen years ago the
very-English Sulphide Corporation, whose
chairman is Kintore. an ex-Gov. of S. Aus.,
tried to establish the. spelter business at
Newcastle, in Australia. When it failed,
the c-o. could find no sale for its concen-
trates except'to Germany. But the very-
English co. didn’t like that, and formed a
subsidiary co. —of which Wallington, of the
King’s household at Buckingham Palace, is
a director —to treat the Sulphide Cor-
poration’s product. For 10 years or
so that subsidiary co. has been strug-
gling against tremendous difficulties to
again plant the business solidly in
England, and it lias never earned a divi-
dend, and it isn’t even yet in a position to
take one-twentieth part of the .one mine’s
output of zinc concentrates. As remarked
just now, this co. is very English; also, its
general manager is very English and very
able; and its managing agents in Australia
are Gibbs, Bright and Co., who are very
English when they are not Australian. If
that co. hasn’t been able to find a market
in England for its zinc output, and to get
its smelting done either here or there, how
much good does Hughes think his Metal
Exchange is going to do?
Anywhere out of Australia for examples
and mentors. The Argus regrets that we
have not a Botha at the head of affairs in
Australia.” Better a Prime Minister
imported from Canton than one from Wide
Bay—which has the fault of being in Aus-
tralia.
THE INCUBUS.
TIME: “How long! How long!”
In one act Fisher placed the Common-
wealth services on a war basis last week.
The Commonwealth civil servant only gets
his advancement after qualifying by exami-
nation for each push ahead, and there will
be no more examinations until the war is
over. It is the logical position, of course,
otherwise men who stay at home would
score over those who go to the Front.
London Times, still easily the most power-
ful press factor in Britain, showed its teeth
on the issue of the recent N. S. Wales loan.
After mentioning that the reception of the
loan was “less cordial” than it might have
been if underwriters were not still nursing
a large number of recent infants, it added:
“Moreover, the sanctioning of the enormous
amount of new capital which has been raised
this year seems scarcely compatible with
the policy of husbanding the resources of
the country.” Prime Minister Fisher, by
the way, told the Reps, the other evening
that the Commonwealth Government had
asked the Imperial Government for a loan
to carry on railway works, and had been
turned down. The prospects of new State
loans therefore get gloomier and gloomier.
It is questionable whether the British Gov-
ernment will even sanction the Norton
Griffiths (N.S.W.) loans, though they have
teen underwritten.
Thus Prime Minister Fisher during the
debate on the Loan Bill: —
The principle of equality of sacrifice is that a
man getting- .Cl a clay and a man getting £lO a
day each shall give a clay’s wages.
This may be the Fisher principle, but the
only other individual who could be found to
give the shabby old doctrine a nod of recog-
nition would be some antiquated pillar of
Fat. A man in receipt of £lO a day is
drawing nearly £4OOO a year. Put it at
£7O weekly. Take even a day’s wages
per week from him and he will • not
be deprived of anything to eat, nor
will his family. lie will not be called
upon to appear in public with a patch in
his trousers. His car will still be a public
scent. If the children are not feeling well
they will go to the seaside or up to the
mountains just the same as before. But
take £1 a week from the Australian man
with a wage of £6, and he must shift into
a shabbier street; his wife and children must
pinch here and pinch there, on meat and
butter and clothing and books. They must
make real sacrifices with every hour of their
lives. Of course Fisher, who is really one
of the duller members of the Cabinet, would
never be permitted to put his amazing theory
into practice. The fact that Ministers are
talking of graduated land and income taxes
proves that
Borrowing is such a national habit now-
that the lines of G. Essex Evans’s “Ode to
the Commonwealth” have to be given a new
rendering:—
Awake! Awake! The wings of pawn
Are beating hack the thoughts of pay!
Those politicians wln>
are conspiring to sink
the tariff should have
a look at mercantile
invoices and then go
back to their job. In
Yankee hardware lines
discounts on 'articles
which could be easily
manufactured in Aus-
tralia, and on which the
duty is high—say, 30
per cent, ad valorem —■
are staggering. This
makes the Australian
invoice price some-
times as much as 50
per cent, lower than
the ordinary sale price
in the U.S.A.; and
even with freight, cost
of carriage and pack-
ing, exchange and
other charges, the
landed cost of goods
is, in many cases,
lower than the manu-
facturers’ listed price.
Those who are in the
trade witnessed a sig-
nificant movement a
week or two ago. As
soon as it was an-
nounced that Fisher
wouldn’t go on with
tariff reform, off came
some of the Yankee
discounts. In other
words, up went prices
to the Australian
wholesaler and re-
tailer. The foreign-
trader says prices go
up when you put on n
duty. What has just
happened shows that
]irices go up when the
foreigner finds out that
the local Government
has been drugged and
that duties will not go on.
Chief Secretary Black (N.S.W.) got dis-
tinctly the best of an encounter the other
day with a Sydney paper which raised
horrified hands at liis action in loosening
the regulations for the sale of peanuts on
Sundays. Black pointed out that until the
censorious journal was consistent enough to
only collect and handle the news of its Mon-
day edition after midnight on Sunday it was
itself. violating a law which applies as much
to papers as to peanuts.
THE FORGE OF EXAMPLE.
SLEEPLESS PARTY: “Didyer read Holman's
appeal in Jars’ night’s papert ’E wants
men to go to the Front. Wot are you
pointer do about it ?”
VOICE OF SLEEP (drowsily): “Same as
’Olman, I s’pose!”
The Russians are establishing at Moscow
large works for the manufacture of dyes
and chemical products from native raw
material. This shows our ally up in his true
light, basely trying to capture German trade
with something more tangible than a noise
made through his whiskers. That at the
moment when something might be done in
Australia the tariff should be thrown over-
board by the Fisher Government is merely
an instance of Australian thoroughness.
“Lots o’ time,” says Fisher! Certainly.
Three cheers.
A SONG OF ECONOMY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Avaunt, red beef! Call not the mutton chop
From its dear rest upon the butcher's hook 1
Avoid the snare of butter! Let it stop
Where the cow grazes by the purling brook.
Speak not of sugar! While the war shall last
Such luxuries to me are dead and past.
We must economise. The cabbage green
Is far too high in price to use as food.
The eauliblossom’s smile is seldom seen;
Potatoes are as coy as Ermyntrude.
Beans have grown scarce, and yellow peas may fade
Down the long’ vistas of my feeding glade.
Porridge remains, and sometimes tea and bread,
And fish may gather to the public trawl,
And highly-flavored margarine instead
Of butter still may answer to my call;
Or, at the worst, in famine’s lofty scorn,
I may subsist, like poultry, on cracked corn.
We must economise. My Mary’s hat
Is that she wore in happy summers gone;
She yet may skin the mild domestic cat
Ere winter die for something to put on;
While I, but for the stern police restraint,
1« lieu of trousers might be clothed in paint.
We must economise! We really must!
Will someone tell us where we should begin?
But importations won’t be stopped, 1 trust;
And barring foreign loans would be a sin.
I want to do my best for England’s star;
But let us see that we don’t go too far!
N.S.W.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
It was a wise suggestion that the inter-
state cricket matches should be abandoned this year.
Otherwise an unfair condition of affairs, either on
the playing or, what would be worse, on the fighting
field, would arise and leave an unpleasant odor.
For instance, in regard to the Sheffield Shield, a
weaker cricket State, observing Victoria (the
holder) despatching the flower of her players, might
be tempted to restrain good men who would other-
wise enlist in order to snatch possession of the
hardware. Two or three or even all the States
might more or less succumb to such a temptation.
It is far from likely, of course, but the abolition
of the possibility is a matter that earns congratula-
tion. But while that is so there is no call to shut
down on the game as a game. Nor on football;
nor on tennis; nor on any of the athletic pastimes.
Indeed, it would be a calamity if the athlete, upon
whom the nation so largely depends for its exist-
ence at the Front, should not be encouraged to his
utmost fitness. He is enlisting well; there need be
no fear that he will not continue to enlist well; and
as he drafts out from the sward to the camp the
physical pick of the race is marching.
* * * *
There are so many different ways of
getting into the war. George Gunn, the cricketer,
has enlisted for the mechanical transport section of
the Army Service Corps.
* * * *
V.R.C. committee, in camera, has given
a hard knock to the stipendiary stewards’ authority.
Recently the stipes held a long and careful inquiry
into the startling reversal of form of Linrose, with
the result that Killorn, who rode the mare both times,
was disqualified for three years, whereas the owner
and trainer were exonerated from blame. The ver-
dict argued that the stipes, working behind closed
doors, had convinced themselves, by means of bet-
ting books, that some person or persons had “got at”
Killorn, and reaped a harvest. But when Ivillorn’s
appeal to the com. was heard, after a week’s post-
ponement, the sentence was quashed. “Fresh evi-
dence” convinced the secret tribunal that everything
had been honest as honest could be in the Linrose
matter, and even the stipes, “it is believed,” ad-
mitted that the fresh evidence justified the decision
which made them look such dangerouslv unsatisfac-
tory inquirers. As to the nature of the stale old
evidence and the fresh new evidence, the V.R.C. com-
mittee remains discreetly mum. A punishment of
three years’ disqualification for a leading jockey has
been annulled, but he gets no apology for the slur
cast on his character, nor do the stipes get repri-
manded for casting it. The reversal of judgment
is more staggering than the reversal of form. Another
jockey was suspended for three months by the
same stipendiary stewards for careless riding in a
hurdle race; but the same committee, which hadn’t
seen the race, decided that the boy was not careless.
Another lot of appeals from stipe law in connection
with the steeplechaser Battleship will be discussed
on Friday, and the Melbourne racing world wonders
whether any invidious distinctions will be made in
this case, and, if so, why ? The Battleship case recks
with mvsterv.

The balance-sheets of the V.R.C. and
Q.T.C. —-the controlling bodies in their respective
States—throw a beam of light on the size of the
business of horse-racing. This is the condensed
result of the year’s operations:—
Credit—Pro-
Stake- fit and Loss
Profits. money. Accounts.
V.R.C. .. £17,309 £76,489 £22,880
Q.T.C. .. 1,949 39,460 42,702
The bulky credit balances make it clear that the
decision to hand over profits during the coming year
to the Australian war funds is not likely to leave
the clubs utterly stranded on the rocks when the tide
goes out.

“T.F.”: In Bananaland those to whom
the short road to wealth seemeth through specula-
tion on the totalisator are making a noise over the
bulkiness of the tax. In the past season over 15
per cent, was cut out of tote investments thuswise: —
Government, 5 per cent. . . . . £11,299
Queensland Turf Club, 6f per cent. 15,254
Totalisator contractors, 2 per cent 4,519
Fractions retained, 1.17 per cent. 2,661
'Total deduction . . . . £33,733
In N. S. Wales last year the A.J.C. (Sydney)
gathered £15,450 in license fees from bookmakers
before the “books” commenced to shear the lambs.
The Bananalanders should think of that and then
present an illuminated address to the machine for
letting them off so lightly.
George Clifford, one of Maoriland’s best-
known sportsmen, entered upon his 21st year of
office as president of the M.L. Racing Conference
last week. He was born in 1847, turned his back
on the Bar in the ’7o’s to take up farming amongst
the station kings of Canterbury, and for a quarter of
a century or more has probably done more towards
purifying the sport of kings, spielers, etc., than any
man in the Ricketty Isles.
Some of the Maoriland Jockey Clubs are
taking the axe to the Tod Sloan or crouch style of
riding as far as hurdle races and steeplechases are
concerned. The Taranaki Club has been the first to
come forward with a motion to pass out this stvle of
navigating a mount. The chief arguments against it
are that a horse pilot who is perched on his vehicle
like a monkey on a stick has no command over the
moke, arid is a danger to himself and to every other
rider in the race. Which sounds suspiciously like
an excellent imitation of the truth.
* * * *
At the Bundaberg (Q.) Racing Club’s
recent meeting there was something suspiciously
like a record on the totalisator (ss. a ticket). In
the Time Trot Handicap there was one ticket on the
first horse, one on the second and none on the third.
These were the only starters. The dividend was Bs.
In the Club Handicap, three starters, the dividend
was 7s. In another race, with 89 tickets in the
machine, the first nag paid 12s. and the second
animal 3s. Sheer recklessness!
More racing coincidences: Royal (Royal
Artillery—Fay) recently woh three races at Carrie-
ton (S. Aus.). In two of them, Ladle and Enver
Bey were second and third respectively, and the
dividend in each case was £1 6s. for £l. Enver
Bey was second in Royal’s third win.
**‘ * *
The well-known mare Slipper, which was
owned by the late Murkand Lai of Melbourne, was
sold last week to E. Bones, an Essendon publican.
The price paid was £2OO.
“P.A.Q,”: Some years ago in London I
witnessed a struggle between the “Terrible Turk,”
then at the zenith of his fame as a wrestler, and one
D'Arcy, a popular Cockney Tommy and champion
wrestler. The place was crowded, more than half
the audience being brother Tommies. The first
bout, a ding-dong go, lasted fully 20 minutes, during
which time D’Arcy clung to his maji with the
tenacity of a leech. By this time the Turk must
have brought his sweating powers into action, and
when a Turk sweats —whew ! Things got so slippery
that D’Arcy let go, and as a result was on the mat
in a second. There was no applause. The silence
was at last broken by a Tommy from the top gal-
lery calling out in a very hurt voice: “Why didn’t
yer ’old ’im, D’Arcy?” “’Old ’im,” answered the
disgusted D’Arcy ; “ ’Old ’im ! ’Ow could I ’old the
greasy ?” Then two ladies fainted and
the lights went out.
* * * *
Fighting Billy Murray, a sallow youth
from the U.S., whose right name is Vasco de Gama
or Torquemada, or something similar, and who dis-
closes the passionate mouth- and bullet-head of old
Africa, encountered Mick King, of Australia, at Syd-
ney Stadium on Saturday night. Murray has an
impressive record, which includes fights with
McGoorty, Chip and Clabby, in all of which he
escaped extermination. He is strong and combative,
as rugged as the side of a cliff, and almost as slow.
He cannot box. Or at least lie could not box
Mick King. His best —in fact) -about his only effec-
tive —hit was a pile-driving hinge to the body which
painted his adversary’s ribs scarlet before the con-
test was half through. This punch was delivered
with either hand, but preferably the left. King
fought on the retreat a good deal, more especially in
the closing rounds. It made the affair less thrilling
than it might have been, but it was sound general-
ship. Not many men, unless in armor, could afford
to engage in a slogging match with one of Murray's
strength and gameness. Despite his manifestly
superior skill, King did not win by any great mar-
gin. While he cut the foreigner’s face to pieces
with accurately-directed straight lefts, the latter
bombarded him down below, asi above described, and
was always the aggressor.' Murray should put up a
blithering display with a venturesome, forceful
fighter like Darcy.
* * * *
That fine old veteran, Hughie Mehegan.
was knocked out by Herb McCoy at Melbourne
Stadium on Saturday night. The lethal blow was a
right uppercut to the jaw applied in the 15th round.
Hughie had already been dropped for nine in the
preceding round. Rc.fh men were within a trifle of
9st. 121 b.

Freddy Kerr, one of the best feather-
weights in Maoriland, has taken the final count at
Gallipoli. Norman Bennetts, who won the Aus-
tralasian featherweight title at Dunedin three years
ago, promptly volunteered to fill the gap, and is
. training for the Front. Two other well-known M.L.
boxers, Paul McQuarrie and Geo. Matthewson, have
recently been wounded at the Dardanelles, but are
doing well, and will soon be back upper-cutting the
harassed Turk again.
From Harry Stone, png: Since leaving
God s country, Australia, I have been anxious to
return, and you can expect me soon and lively. I
have been boxing in the United States since my arri-
val here, and won every battle I had. One boxer
whom Tom Andrews thinks is a world-beater, Kid
Graves, I out-pointed in every round, and seven
other battles I have won easy. Regards to all who
know me.

* * * *
minister for External Affairs Hugh
Mahon has a son (Austin) preparing to leave for the
Front. First of all he will complete his final year of
medicine at Melbourne . Uni. In sport he has done
well on the running track.
* * * *
League football in Melbourne, from now
on until the finals, will practically be exhibition
games. Nothing but a full-blown miracle can wrench
Collingwood, Carlton. Fitzroy and Melbourne from
the play-off places. On Saturday the first-mentioned
proceeded to Geelong' and dished the seasiders by
91 points to 51, champion goal-kicker Lee adding
another seven to his tally. Carlton—without Challis,
.whose over-lapping toe has not obstructed his path
to war-glory on a second application—collared 74
points to x'lelbourne’s 45 ; and Fitzroy pushed South
further into oblivion by 8 goals 6 behinds to South’s
7 goals 6 behinds. The Saints and ’Dons also col-
lided, the former winning by 10 goals 10 behinds to
3 goals 13 behinds.
For some mysterious reason the only
Ass. game, semi-final between Brunswick and Ports,
failed to draw enough spectators -to pay the gate-
keepers. Yet the play was pleasing and vigorous.
Ports, with some swift and early goals, got a good
lead, but towards the end organisation and training
wore them down, and the Brickies won into the final
with 74 points to 56.
The League has decided to play on to the
scheduled end of the season, and will by then be in
a position of splendid isolation, the Ass., the Melb.
District’s Ass., the Midland Ass., Katamatite District
Ass. and sundry other country football bodies hav-
ing decided to wind matters up more or less sud-
denly.
* * * *
Following- the lead of the Vic. Football
Association, the Vic. Junior Association has deter-
mined to bring the season to an early close. No
fewer than 124 players have enlisted. Ascot Vale
Club has contributed 22 and Footscray and Port
Melbourne 20 each.
* * * *
The remarkable run of success of Prince
Alfred College in Adelaide school athletics has now
lasted over a year without a break; and it still
goes on. Last week the Saint Peter’s boys were
taken down at football. They were a young and
light team, and a horribly wet day extinguished any
chance they might have had. They scored nine
points in the first 10 minutes, and didn’t look in
again. “Princes” won by 12 goals 18 behinds to
one goal three behinds, the most hollow victory in
the 30-odd years' record of the match.
* * * *
There was, a unique football match at
Dunedin the other day in aid: of the M.L. Wounded
.Soldiers’ Fiirid,- when two elevens, composed entirely
of the sleeker sex played, two spells of 20 minutes
each under the Soccer code at the Caledonian Ground.
One lot of girls represented the Mutual Stores and
the other Mollison’s—two big fluff palaces in the
Scawtch and soda metropolis. The players had been
well coached for some weeks previously and, in short
skirts and jerseys, they gave a surprisingly good
display—of the game, of course. Mutual Stores
won after an equal tussle by two goals to one.
* * * *
Over 1000 footballers in Melbourne have
enlisted, but that’s not all that have tried to crowd
into khaki. Last week a batch of men turned their
backs on the playing fields and offered themselves for
service, but were told they couldn’t go into camp
yet. In Maoriland many athletes unable to get into
the ranks promptly are stringing across to N. S.
Wales to enlist. Yet you hear some people still
burble: “Why don’t the big, hulking footballers stop
the game and go to the Front?”
* * * •
Cecil McVilly, who won the Diamond
Sculls at Henley in 1913, is now in camp at Clare-
mont (Tas.) getting ready for a row with the Turk.'
He represented Australia in the 1912 Olympic games
at Stockholm, and as he did not shine there he
stayed on in London and pulled off what is practi-
cally the world's amateur sculling championship the
following year. The professional side of sculling has
as its star khaki representative Ernest Barry, the
world’s champion.
*** . *
Six of last year’s eight-oar crew
from Tasmania have given up the scull for the
rifle; and in the other States so many topnotchers
have enlisted that the abandonment of inter-State
regattas is generally regarded as a fair thing.
* * * *
N.S.W. heavyweight sculling champion-
ship is stirring up further argument. Last week
champion Ben Tlioroughgood accepted S. Penfold’s
challenge for the title and £5O stake-money. Also,
he notified Syd. Pearce that if he wins he will issue
an ultimatum in his direction. Thoroughgood, who
alleges that his race with Pearce at Middle Harbor
left him 30s. out of pocket when all exes, were paid,
sticks out for both events to be held on the -Hunter
River, and reckons that it will pay to allow reason-
able expenses to challengers to go north. The
Penfold match is set down for August 21.
*** ‘ *
Lady Goold-Adams, wife of Bananaland’s
Governor, is a good shot. She has a fine eve with
the miniature rifle, and the other day at the opening
of a range she lay out on the firing platform and
put up a score of 53 out of a possible 70. She
and his Ex. donkey-licked their two opponents, though
one of the latter (the smiling lady captain of the
club) made top score of 63, and the other was a
major of the Headquarters Staff (whose score was
20).
* * * *
Good hunting at Tauranga (M.L.) ! Said
an English ranger to The Bulletin: “Taking cli-
mate into consideration it would be hard to beat any-
where on earth.” Curlew (godwit) flock in thou-
sands on the mud flats; large, quantities of : grey
duck, teal and swan (shooting of these starts in
May) along the estuary; pigeons in the bush ; fallow
and red deer to stalk ; peasant and quail; rabbit and
hare. When you become tired of filling the - bags
you can change the subject with the trout.
* * * *
Is this the record for persistency? It
took S. Thomas, of Sydney, and G. Horsburgh, of
East Melbourne, both baseballers, five attempts at
the recruiting bases before they got to the home
plate. They say they would have been at it yet
if the umpire hadn’t given them “in.” His heart
was broke. Gillies Campbell, who shares with
Itippax the pitching honors in Sydney ’ball, has
been turned down owing to chest deficiency. But
that’s only the first flutter.
* * * *
Leichhardt’s temporary disqualification
still causes un-Christian language amongst Sydney
baseballers. Last week the club, backed up by “legal
opinion,” appealed -for the immediate removal of the
disqualification, but the State Association merely
acknowledged receipt of the letter and put it
aside amongst the ornaments. Leichhardt is now
pondering whether it will follow up the “legal
opinion” or iust sit down in a chastened spirit till
the disqualification has run its. length.
**• * *
“Moana”: “0.K.” (B. l/7/’l5) com-
plains that Wilding and J. J. Addison were used in
France merely as “cannon fodder,” when their train-
ing as tennis players had equipped them splendidly
for aviation. I don’t know anything about Addison,
but I do know that poor Anthony was “Captain
Wilding, of the Air Wing.” He had done a lot of
air work with Grahame White as pilot, and was
regarded as one of the best observers in the service.
He came to be killed in a trench in this way. He
was doing some important ground-scouting in con-
nection with the Air Service, and was riding a
motor-bike along a road in North France, when sud-
denly German shells began to hit the road just
ahead. A regiment of the Suffolks was entrenched
along the side of the road. Knowing one of the
officers, Wilding took shelter with him and some
others in a dug-out. They had hardly got into it
when it was wrecked by a shell, the whole of those in
it being buried. All the officers and Wilding were
killed. Anthony didn’t lose his life because he was
put to do work that “any two stodgy, resolute
patriots” could have done as well, but simply because
his luck chanced to be very much out that day.
T * * *
The billiard match for the professional
championship of Maoriland at Auckland was won by
C. McConachy, of Timaru, from W. E. Stephenson, of
Auckland, the holder. The play was 9000 up, and
McConachy won with 725 points to spare. Scarcely
more than a decent-sized break separated the pair
at any time. Some good judges hold that the
winner has a stylish and crisp losing hazard game
that even George Gray could not excel.
* * * *
Some evidence of the increasing efficiency
of the motor-car engine can be gathered from the
times of the winners of the great Indianapolis “500.”
When Harroun won in 1911 he averaged 74.8 m.p.h.
In 1912 Dawson registered 78.7 m.p.h. Goux had an
easy win in 1913, his figure being 75.93 m.p.h., but
he could easily have done better if pushed. In 1914
Thomas jumped to 82.47, whilst this year Ralph de
Palma broke all existing American records by cover-
ing the 500 miles at a speed of 89.84 m.p.h. In
connection with de Palma’s great drive it is interest-
ing to note that his time of shr. 33min. 55sec. is
only 17min. 15sec. slower than the world’s record of
shr. 16min. 40sec., which was established by Messrs.
Lee, Guinness, de Resta (who ran second to de
Palma this year, and Chassagigne, who all took turn
about at the wheel of the Sunbeam, when the record
was put up at Brooklands (Eng.) in October, 1913.
Of the two performances de Palma’s is undoubtedlv
the better, for he drove right through the contest bv
himself, and also had to contend with a big field of
competitors in the early part of the race.
* . * * *
Birtles, overlanding in his car, telegraphed
from Hughenden this week that he had arrived there
from Cape York, and was heading for Brisbane with
a poisoned hand prior to making the long, lonely run
to Perth. “I have now,” he wired, “completed
35,000 miles in two years.”
* * * *
When it is warm enough for surfing
again somebody might go to a quiet beach and give a
trial to a device described in Outing. A surf-board
is fitted with cylinders ahead and astern to keep it
well up in the water, a small propeller, and a
bicycle-pedal arrangement—stated to be easily
worked in water —to make the propeller-blades turn.
Then you lie flat on it and behave accordingly. It
sounds a weird idea, but is said to give delightful
results after a little practice. American, of course.
The fact was elicited in the State Parlia-
ment that the N.S.W. Government owns 111
motor-cars. Thirty-three of them are used
for carriage of goods. The balance, no less
than 78, are for conveying other things—
sometimes hot air through the* country; at
other times chiffon through the city.
Further results of the Big Australian
policy of the N. S. Wales State Govern-
ment :—•
Here in the Albury district hay-chaff has been
practically unprocurable since the decree issued that
the N. S. Wales cocky shall not ask more than
£7 10s. for his fodder. In Victoria far higher prices
are obtainable, so the Ma Cocky takes his supplies
where he can get £l4 or £l5 a ton. The State
railways, 'acting under instructions, won’t load N. S.
Wales chaff for Victoria, but they will carry it as
far as, say, Culcairn or Gerogery; and then
a man can cart it 20 or 30 miles to Wodonga, over
the border, whence it can be trucked anywhere in
Victoria. the grass has been growing very
slowly, and N. S. Wales stock have been perishing by
the tens of thousand—killed by the stupidity of
the N. S. Wales Government which has once more at-
tempted to butcher the country producers to serve
the ends of the city consumers.

What fine Big Australians some of these
Democrats are! Following the example of
Holman and Hall, of N. S. Wales, who
objected to Vic. and Bananaland getting
wheat grown in their part of Australia, the
Labor Government at Brisbane shut down on
the crime whereby Australians living over
the border were getting Queensland butter.
Now the same patriots have decided to “pro-
hibit Southerners getting meat at the ex-
pense of the people in this State” by stop-
ping them from buying any of the briskets
severed from Queensland beef carcases before
export. That the wretched foreigners in
other parts,of the Commonwealth are going
to make good a big loss on sugar for the
benefit of Bananaland is, of course, a mere
incident. Federation a rumor V Not at all.
It is a great joke.

The Flavor Lasts
Ex-Governor-General Denman, who has
Ivon warning England that it had better
shake itself up and go in for universal train-
ing and compulsory military service, recently
put these points amongst others: —
Tlie experience of Australia and Maoriland goes
to prove that no more democratic measure could be
placed on the Statute book.
We are told it will disorganise trade. It is
precisely because our trade must be organised for
war that compulsion is vitally necessary.
The fact that the War Office is taking married
men up to 40 and reducing the standard of height to
sft. 2in. is proof in itself that the voluntary system
has broken down.
Denman took the opportunity to put in their
class the Tory crowd in Australia, who, hav-
ing fruitlessly fought the compulsory defence
scheme, afterwards claimed it as a child of
their own. Said he :
The system was first introduced in the Common-
wealth by a Labor Government.... The of
the scheme is largely due to their administration.
The Labor Government and the Labor party of that
day are fairly entitled to take credit for this
success.
Three men, Fisher, Hughes and Pearce, are
particularly selected for the ex-G.-G.'s con-
gratulations.
Alf Eddcn. the veteran Laborite of N.S.W.
Leg. Assembly, is seriously ill, but still can
laugh over one of his yarns. Up at little
Adamstown, where he lives, he overheard a
couple of constituents discussing him. One
was an old-age pensioner, and the other had
an invalidity pension. Says Old Age to In-
validity : “See old Alf there —lie gets bigger
every day.” “Yus,” agreed Invalidity, “and
it's the likes o’ you and me that’s taxed to
fatten the likes o’ ’im.”
From “Rosna” in London: —
Among the wounded here is E. Y. Butler, formerly
inspecting clerk in Wtstralia’s Crown Law Depart-
ment, now a lieut. in ttie 12th Australian Infantry.
After getting a bullet through the peak of his cap
lie was shot in the light thumb and packed off to
England, much annoyed at being put out of the great
game by such a trifling mishap. In his battalion
was Captain Lalor, grandson of Peter of Eureka.
He left a staff appointment in Victoria to join the
12th, where he was familiarly known as “our little
captain,” and was the most popular man in the
battalion. We have heard that he has gone down,
too.
The Commonwealth, by the way, has established a
Casualty office here. When the first Australian
list of 4000 struck the Commissioner’s Office in one
heap the Records secretary (Smart) had a staff of
only three to help him grapple with it. The staff
has since grown to 30, but even so there are not
many pauses for refreshments. Wounded Tommy
Kangaroo's mail runs into thousands; and those
letters take some sorting out. There’s a good
arrangement, too, by which friends and relatives of
men at the Front can register their names and
addresses for instant information of any casualty.
“Garnishor” is in doubt as to whether he
may hammer his wife :
Jacob Bonaker. the N.S.W. Northern Rivers cocky
who kept his wife tied to a tree, may possibly have
been a profounder lawyer than the Beak who admon-
ished him. The law is hazy. In Ilardyman’s case a
sort of negative imprisonment was imposed on a
frivolous wife. Lord Chief Justice Raymond said
that the husband had ruled of her “that she should
neither sit at his table, nor have any government
of his children, but should he kept to a garret;
and,” his Lordship added, “she deserved no better
usage.” On the same set of facts Ilardyman's case
would presumably be sound law to-day. More or
less on the other hand, it was decided in Jackson’s
ease that “where a wife refuses to live with her hus-
band he is not entitled to keep her in confinement
in order to enforce restitution of conjugal rights,”
and the Court ordered the husband to bring up her
body.
Collins, afterwards a. Law Lord and one of the
greatest of twentieth-century jurists, cited Lord
Chancellor Bacon: “The husband hath by law power
and dominion over his wife, and may keep her by
force within the bounds of duty, and may beat her,
but not in a violent or cruel manner.” Collins did
not contend that a husband was entitled to beat his
wife. It was unnecessary for his purpose. He put
it no higher than Coleridge put it in Cochrane's
case: “There can be no doubt of the general
dominion which the law attributes to the husband
over the wife.” But Lords Ilalsbury and Esher held
against Collins in this particular case, and made
some general remarks about the legal history of the
matter which were more emotional than accurate.
Blackstone may be the best guide. He says that
a wife “is called in our lavv-French a feme-covert,
being under her husband’s cover, protection and in-
fluence, so that as to her person the custody of this
belongs of right to her husband, and by some ancient
authorities it was considered so far under his power
that he might give her moderate correction. For
graver misdemeanors sharper whippings (flag elite et
fustibus acriter), and for others on! y -modicum casti-
(jationem. Though with us;-in the politer reign of
Charles the Second, this power of correction began
to be doubted, yet the lower rank of' people, who
were always fond of the old Common Law, still
claim and exert their ancient privilege.” Then as
now for certain matters she could swear the peace
against him. Blaekstone’s last sentence really con-
tains the gist of the whole matter, . which is that
you cannot enforce any law if it is against the in-
stinct of the people. That is the law supreme which
lies at the root of Bonaker’s case. He may have
been in order in tying his wife to a tree. On the
other hand, he may have been as poor a lawyer as
—well, as some Chancellors have been.
“G..T.11.”: “The border railway” . from
Swan Hill (Vic.), mentioned in The
Bulletin as opened the other day, doesn’t,
of course, cross the border. It • runs from
Swan Hill to Piangil, about 28 miles, and
finishes up still a couple of miles short of
X. S. Wales territory. The nearest railway
town in N. S. Wales is Hay, about 160 miles
away ; and although Piangil is within three
miles of Tooleybuc (N.S.W.) the only
means of communication is a dilapidated old
punt over the Murray, at the latter place.
The Northern Riverina settler now has added
to his other woes the exasperation of know-
ing there is a railway almost at his elbow
which, through the jealousy of State Spiters,
is as much good to him as if it were in the
moon. Of course if Holman bridged the
Murray at Tooleybuc he would be commit-
ting the heinous offence' of diverting the
trade of that district from Sydney (nearly
TOO miles away) to Melbourne (about 140
miles away), and no statesman with an eye
to city votes would be guilty of such an
offence as that.

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort, and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.

cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect

ONE HUNDRED AND THIRTY-FOURTH HALF-YEARLY REPORT of
The Commercial Banking Company of Sydney
Limited.
Presented to the Shareholders at an Ordinary General Meeting held at the Head
Banking House, George Street, Sydney on Tuesday, 20th July, 1915.
The Directors submit to the Shareholders a Balance-sheet showing the Liabilities
and Assets of the Bank on the 30th June, 1015, and present the following report:—
The balance at the credit of Profit and Loss Account, after deducting rebate on
current bills and accrued interest on fixed deposits, reducing premises account, paying
Note and other taxes, and providing for bad and doubtful debts, as well as granting to
the Officers a bonus on their salaries, amounts to ilJ<,7do lo J
out of which the Directors recommend the following appropriations

Since the beginning of the vear the Bank has lost the valuable services of its two
youngest Directors: Mr. Norman Shelley, who died suddenly; and Lieutenant-Colonel
A. J. Onslow Thompson, of the Fourth Battalion, of the I irst Infantry Brigade,
Australian Imperial Force, who was killed in action at the Daidanelles. lhe
Directors mourn their loss. , r „, _ , T ,
The Honorable James Burns, M.L.C., and Thomas Forster Knox, Esquire, were
appointed, in terms of Article 94, to fill the vacancies thereby created. These gentlemen
are eligible for election and have given notice of candidature.
This meeting is also called upon to elect a Director, consequent on the retirement,
in terms of the Articles of Association, of the Honorable Henry Moses, M.L.C., who is
eligible for re-election, and has given the requisite notice that he is a candidate tor the
office. It also rests with the meeting to appoint Auditors for the ensuing year.
During the half-year the Branch at Quambone, in this State, has been closed.
The Dividend is now payable.
GEORGE J. COHEN, Chairman.

Sydney, July 14, 1915.
We hereby certify that we have examined the bills, compared the balances and counted the coin in
the Head Office of The Commercial Banking Corppany of Sydney, Limited, and have compared the returns
of the blanches, and have found the same as specified in the foregoing Balance-Sheet.
F. W. HIXSON \ .
W. L. DOCKER / Audltors -
The following resolutions were carried unanimously:—•
“That the Report and Statement of Account be received and adopted, and ordered
to be circulated among the Proprietors.”
“That the Honorable Henry Moses, M.L.C., be re-elected a Director of the Bank
and that the Honorable James Burns, M.L.C., and Thomas Forster Knox,
Esquire, be elected Directors.” .
“That W. L. Docker, Esquire, and J. J. Brenan, Esquire, be appointed Auditors for
the ensuing year.” 7
“That the thanks of the Shareholders be presented to the Directors, General
Manager and Officers of the Bank for their very satisfactory management of
the affairs of the institution.” V T. A. DIBBS, General Manager.
SPECIAL RESOLUTION.
That this meeting places on record its very high appreciation of the distinguished
services rendered to the Bank by Mr. T. A. Dibbs during the 48 years he has held the
position of General Manager, this whole term of office having been marked by a
devotion to the welfare of this institution; which could not be surpassed.
In moving the adoption of the Report and Balance-sheet the Chairman, Mr. George
J. Cohen, said
Before moving the adoption of the Directors’ Report and the Balance-sheet for the
half-year just ended I will occupy your time for a few minutes in comparing the
principal items with those of twelve months ago. Though nearly the whole period of
the twelve months has been covered by the War, which .unfortunately still continues,
it will be seen that the Bank has made good progress.
Of; the items on the debit side, Reserve Fund shows an increase of £75,000, and,
with the further appropriation now recommended, that Fund will stand at £1,780,000.
Our Deposits have increased by £2,793,000, but this expansion we look upon as of a
somewhat transitory character. There appears to be a disposition on the part of many
of our customers to let funds remain idle and aw r ait more favorable opportunities for
investment. The Government accounts too, at the moment, are largely in credit.
The item, Notes in Circulation, is down by £4BOO by payment of Notes that have
come in, and now stands at £16,952.
Bills in Circulation are less by £448,000, largely due to shrinkage in trade.
Profit and Loss Acount, as rriight be expected, shows slightly less.
With these changes our total figures are £29,700,000, showing an increase for the
twelve months of £2,400,000.
On the other side of the Balance-sheet our Coin and Cash at Bankers, Australian
Legal Tender Notes, and Notes and Bills of other Banks, are together more iffian
£3,300,000 over the figures of last year.
Our coin is less, partly by payments under the agreement by the Banks witll the
Commonwealth Government, and by the export of gold to keep ourselves stroij in
London.
Our holding under the four headings I have just mentioned is slightly over ine
and three-quarter millions.
Money at Short Call in London has been reduced by £1,550,000. Rates *. (ere
latterly have been low, and our funds have been more profitably employed in % fili-
tating the operations of our customers:
British Consols and Government Securities an increase of £920.000 princi-
pally caused by the holding of Government Securities shortly maturing, which will
presently disappear from our books, and our deposits will then be correspondingly
reduced.
Bills Receivable in London and Remittances in transit shows a shrinkage of
£570.000, the natural outcome of our reduced exports.
I may add that the total of our Liquid Assets, largely increased and aggregating-
close on fifteen millions, bears a ratio of £57 16s. 4d. per cent, to our liabilities to the
Publ Advances are higher on the year by £273,000, but lower than they were six
months ago. Bank Premises Account is £ll,OOO higher.
When we last met I mentioned that a number of our men had joined the
Expeditionary Forces. • Up to the present eiglity-seven have been released from Bank
duties in Australia, and about seventy of them have been engaged at the Front. I
very much regret to say that intelligence has been received that eight of these have
been killed in action, seven have been wounded, and one is missing.
Thirty-six more of our men have volunteered for service, and arrangements
being made for their release as early as possible.
Seven men from our London Office are also on active seivice.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CORRESPONDENTS.
Frank S. E.: Don’t want two ironbark figures with
stumps instead of hands.... Homo: What’s wrong
with your lion’s mouth ?... .Jack McM.: Beginner’s
stuff. You need a lot of tuition... .A.M.: The joke
is not apparent, but the crudity of the drawing' is
obvious.... AY. J.: If we didn’t object on artistic
grounds the Censor would on grounds of public
policy.... W.E.C.: A ready-made, cracked and ruined
masterpiece.... Cap M.: Three wooden figures and a
dim joke. . . .Claude B.: You have constructed a news-
boy of an entirely new shape. AA’e may like 1 him
better when we get used to him. . . . A.AV.: There are
a few elementary points you haven’t yet picked up.
Start picking now.
The abnormal rush for steam tonnage for
Government work has dislocated the. world’s
freight markets. One result has been the
return of the wind-jammer to duty. Twelve
months ago the disappearance of the sailing
ship, especially the British sailing ship, was
nominated to occur within a decade. The
shipbuilder who laid down the keel of a
sailer would have been regarded much as a
city coachbuilder who commenced to build a
liorse-’bus. But now the wind-jammer com-
mands high freights, and when tenders were
invited for the chartering of the German
sailing ships interned in Australia there
was very keen competition among shipping
men who long ago thought they had done
with sailing ships for ever. Eo.ually keen
is the demand for officers who will ship on .
them. Owners of old hulks, particularly in
America, have suddenly found they are the
proprietors of little gold mines. The Yan-
kee lumber-schooners which discharge in
Australian ports, instead of going leisurely
to Newcastle to load coal, now skip back to
the Pacific slope in ballast, in order to fill
up again at the high freights ruling. Sub-
marine warfare is helping these arks to earn
dividends in their old age, as the loss of
tonnage this way isn’t made up fast enough
by building new steamers. The wind-
jammer would, of course, be an easy prey
for the “sub.”; but such vessels are being
used on routes outside the Gerliogs’ sphere
of uselessness.
Despite their powerful forces
Their science and their guns,
AVe never will be conquered
By the monarch of the hull’s.
Upon brave and neutral Belgian
They threw their brutal weight
But never upon the empire
AVill they deal a crushing fate.
Their threats to invade old england
Don’t fill our hearts with fear,
Their power will be broken
Before the dawn of the new year.

“M.G.” meets his first co-editor : -
J. H. Greene, whose striking “Kultur” verse ap-
+. t>
peared at the top of the Red Page (8/7/’ls), was a
. b \ i / />
schoolfellow of mine at Queen’s College, St. Kilda
(Vic.). He went to Melbourne Uni. and had a
brilliant career, winning scholarship after scholar-
ship; but, before he could finish his course (Science)
in.j , ... ...
the land-boom smash came and forced him, like many
’ 3
other students, to look for an immediate living. He
went west, saw Cooigardie within a month or two
of Bayley s discovery, and returned, a year later, to
make his first incursion into adult journalism with
a graphic series of goldfields sketches in the Argus.
About this time Greene was appointed analyst to the
Melbourne. Gas Cos., and that kept him going,
Between times he wrote stories and verses for The
Bulletin, and, taking his work as a whole, I don’t
think any other Australian writer has ever reached
such a high standard. The comparison is, perhaps,
not quite fair, because Greene wrote so infrequently—
a face which led to his name being overlooked by
many of those who were careful readers of The
Bulletin’s literary work. Two of his contributions,
however, may be remembered —a set of verses called
“The Jester of the Damned,” and a story telling how
tropical heat germinated insect life on a vessel full
of logwood and caused the whole crew to take to the
freshly-tarred rigging, while the insect pirates held
the ship. Later, Greene became stage-struck. He
joined Dan Barry, and I went one night to see him
act. My conclusion was that acting was the only
thing that Greene could not do. However, he stuck
to it, and embodied his experiences in a series of
backblock barnstorming, which ran in The Bulletin,
signed “The Stroller.” About 10 years ago Greene,
who had married an American girl in the same com-
pany, decided to cross the Pacific on a visit io her
people. He has not since returned, and the verse
taken from the New York Times is the first hint I’ve
had of his existence on the other side. In our
schooldays, Greene, Jim Cramsie—now a well-known
squatter of the New England district (N.S.W.) —and
I fan a school-paper called the Queen’s College
Bulletin. It was my first experience in journalism,
but I am still in it, and so, seemingly, is Greene,
whom we all expected would be professor of science
at some university before he was 25. Cramsie was
the only one with sufficient sense to escape. He got
into something much better —the art of letting the
sheep amuse themselves for their owner’s profit.
Died recently in Oaklands, U.S.A., John
David Thorpe, a Rochester (Yic.) native
who invented an auto-sledge that made the
Klondyke regions somewhat accessible. Pri-
vations in the cold Far North hastened the
call.
“D.” on an ancient mystery:—
What a thing personality is! The Kaiser has
achieved nothing in this war. In fact, he has proved
such a failure as a generalissimo that he is under-
stood to have dropped interfering in the carnage.
Yet all over the world people think of him first.
Kitchener has done no fighting; French has been in
the field from the beginning. Yet Wellington was
not more trusted than Kitchener, nor a tenth part
so popular. But the queerest instance comes from
tha navies. The German high secs fleet has become
almost an object of derision by reason of its in-
activity. The British fleet, on the other hand, has
done much. The world’s public knows no British
Admiral by sight, barring perhaps Jellicoe; and the
average citizen would be doubtful about him. But
who does not know the appearance of the apparently
futile von Tirpitz? It cannot merely be his whis-
kers. It must be something in the man himself.
When von Ingenohl was deposed from the command
of the German fleet early in the war the papers
stated it in two-line paragraphs. If anything hap-
pened to Yon Tirpitz the headlines would stretch
across the page.
Parson Frank Lynch, of Holy Trinity,
Williamstown (Vic.), who punched a man
on the nose the other day at a recruiting
meeting for Empire’s sake, and then found
out that he had mistakenly stoushed a fellow-
patriot, gets frequent limelight. Holy
Trinity regards him with mixed views. So
did his previous congregation at East Caul-
field. But as the contention only involves
the question of high-church ritual where
some members wish to be low-church, Lynch
toils on serenely in the vineyard. On one
occasion he put some anti-Labor politics in
his church paper. The venerable Chanter,
M.11.R., was one of the congregation, and in
the ensuing argument Lynch politely hauled
down his flag, publicly admitting that most
of Labor’s aims were above reproach. This
was the act of either a shirker or a white
pan—and nobody who knows him could ever
imagine the impetuous Lynch as shirking
either a controversy or a brick.
Eden George, who once made a surprise
entry into N.S.W. Leg. Assembly by defeat-
ing Dr. James Graham, is a member of the
French Red Cross service. He was on the
job in France when last heard from, and
mentioned that if he was asked how long
it would be necessary for him to remain
there he would say two years.

BANANA LAND
“Barcoo”: Three cheers, Australia, for
Evesham, a station on the Darr River, Cen-
tral Queensland. It has thus far sent to
the Front 24 soldiers, ranging from Watt,
the manager, down to the newest jackeroo.
John Turnbull, the principal owner, has two
sons out of four in the thick of it. Well-
shot, in the same district, runs a good sec-
ond with 16 volunteers.
“P.F.”: Home-Secretary Bowman pro-
poses now to nationalise hospitals. Good;
but is this the time for the change? National-
isation in Queensland means, roughly,
£lOO,OOO a year more to pay out. The
Ryan Government has already handed back
the public servants some £60,000 of deferred
automatic increases, and Adamson confesses
to having railway implications on hand that
mean over millions of loan money, while
every variety of official, high and low, is
clamoring for more, more. Will Australia
ever have a Labor Administration that
understands the meaning of restraint in
matters of expenditure?
“Viki”: Charles Seymour, editor of the
Worker, handed in his resignation last
week. With Matt Reid, Gilbert Casey, Lane
and a couple more, Seymour practically
commenced the latter-day Labor movement
in Queensland. “Charlie” and Lane were
the founders of the Worker, the first issue
of which was distributed from a handcart
because the newsagents, under instructions,
would not touch it. For 12 years Seymour
occupied the room marked “Sub-Eflitor.”
He has helped the party grow from a small,
victimised coterie to attain a majority in
the Assembly and the Hopse of Reps., and
he has seen the paper expand from a monthly
leaflet to a solidly established weekly.
The Brisbane police a few days ago
arrested one Karl Waldraff, a reservist who
had been trained on a German submarine.
Although simply laboring on a farm, Wal-
draff was found to be loaded with a Win-
chester rifle, a Webley revolver, heaps of
ammunition, and an arsenal of anti-Britisli
feelings. As to the anti-British feelings, if
the police would only carefully and intel-
ligently inquire they could find barrow-loads
of bitter German newspapers (come by way
of America) circulating in certain districts
of South Queensland.
The Northern climate makes even the Law
unusually tired. On July 12, at Townsville
Police Court, a man was charged with
stealing from the person. He was com-
mitted for trial at the District Court, then
sitting, and was released on bail at 12 noon.
At 12.30 he obtained a blank cheque form,
forged a demand for £l5, and, shortly after
one o’clock, presented it at a jeweller’s,
where he owed a small account. The trades-
