Rockhampton’s anti-Socialist Harbor
Board pleads desperately with the Govern-
ment to “nationalise Port Alma.” It is
difficult to say which struggles harder for
nationalisation —the Socialists or the anti-
Socialists. The only difference is that one
lot wants to nationalise the commercial
successes; the other's the commercial fail-
ures.
“Old Settler”: What’s all this blither
about drought ’nd high prices ’nd hard-
ships? Butter? Why, for years we never
saw it ? ’nd the first farmers on the Oxley
lived mainly on pumpkin ’nd bandicoot. I
rfemember the first Germans in the Rose-
wood goin’ nine miles to the Bremer to get
water ’nd having to cart it home in kero-
sene-tins. Hardships be jiggered!
The protest made against the killing off
of the gentle koala in Queensland needs to
be repeated in view of the fact that all does
shot just now carry a young bear m the
pouch. The open season begins two months
too soon —if there must be an open season
at all.
Queensland’s Museum librarian, Douglas
Rannie, handed in liis checks a few days
ago at the too-early age of 54. Rannie
hailed from Banff (Scotland), and served
Bananaland for over 30 years in various
capacities. He went all through the bad
old days of the “blackbird” traffic as a labor
agent, and knew the murky ins and outs ot
that shady business better than any man
in Australia. Always he was guilty ot
literary leanings, and his book, ‘‘Adventures
among South Sea Cannibals,' contains
some good reading, despite a tendency to
paint the lily and dip Tanna Tommy in
pitch. In bis reminiscent vein Rannie
talked better than he wrote.
Benson, Director of Agriculture in Tas-
mania, goes back to Bananaland as Director
of Fruit Culture. He went to the Speck
as a reply to the Denham Government’s
failure to redeem a promise about salary.
One of Ryan’s first acts was to send him a
telegram on the subject. Benson once offered
a Minister for Agriculture to stake his repu-
tation that if allowed to plant an area with
olive trees around a Queensland reformatory
the place would be self-supporting when the
trees were in full bearing. But the Tory
boss turned down the project as absurd.
Thus the nucleus of an important industry
was lost, and Queensland still buys most of
its olive oil from the Dago.
Not only are Billjim and Jimbill at the
Front, but Percy and Marmaduke are there,
too. Among Bananaland’s big squatters the
Fairbairns, Ramsays (Oondoroo), Jowetts
and Clarkes have ail sons or brothers to the
fore in France, Belgium or Gallipoli.
Something for Our Consideration.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Harold Cox, editor of the Edinburgh Review,
says it will be necessary for Britain to borrow
£2,000,000,000 during the war. — Cable.
The attention of the Premier was directed to the
official announcement that no loan money would be
available in Great Britain during the financial year.
Mr. Ryan said it was a matter that would require
serious consideration. — News item.
When the days of boom and boost
Pass like smoke from some old pipe;
When the fowls come home to roost
And the loan is over-ripe;
When there’s drought
About,
And the cocky is knocked out,
And a war is daily weakening the nation;
Even maniac-borrowers pause in the middle
of a clause of their little, tinpot laws,
and remark, amid applause, that in
Freedom’s sacred cause,
The matter will require consideration!
When a gentleman like Cox- —•
A Freetrader born and bred —
Says we’ll run upon the rocks
If we do not look ahead ;
When we dash
Much' cash
Into Everlasting Smash
On the strength of some fill-’em-up-again
oration —
It is tiifie to “take a pull,” and to hearken
to John Bull; for our cup of Jag is
full,
And the matter will require consideration!
When the warnings of the past
Were contemptuously shelved
(For they reckoned it would last,
Did the crowd who dug and delved
At per day,
So gay,
In the old and futile way,
As they charged for work which was mere
imitation)
Did it never strike the blokes, who cared
more about their smokes than their
liftings and their strokes, that the very
best of jokes in the end would come to
folks
As a matter that required consideration?
No! The wasters and the drones
Who have made the honey fly—
Who have scattered round the loans,
Who have milked the State cow dry—
Do not flinch
One inch !
For the men who’ll feel the pinch,
And have to face the awkward situation,
Are you—and me! We must find the
£ s. d. which they sling around with
glee ; but it, somehow, seems to be just
a weird that we must dree, for our good
old friend J. 8., far away across the
sea, now requests us to agree _
To give the matter our consideration —-
Immediate, earnest, deep consideration!
QUEENSLAND.
E. N. M.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, July 24, 1915.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
Their Excellencies rolled up their sleeves
to it this week. On Monday they were at
the annual meeting of the Lady Bowen Hos-
pital, where He made a fine speech and She
listened carefully. This afternoon her Ex.
cracked open a fete at Ashgrove in aid of
the patriotic funds. Next week the duty-
book’s entries include two hospital meetings
—the Children’s and the Lady Lamington
—and a journey to Rosewood for the Show.
Thousands of people congregated at the
Central Station on Monday night to wel-
come back 39 wounded soldiers. Since the
names of the men had not been published,
scores of women endeavored, more or less
successfully, to fight their way up to the
barrier to see whether they owned any of
the stray heroes. It was an unnecessarily
painful ordeal for anxious mothers, sisters
and wives. If you know the address of the
brilliant individual responsible for the whole
or any part of the thoughtful arrangements,
Thargomindah, send it on and I’ll pass it
round to 1050 women who want a lock of
his hair.
The invalids, who are all chirpy, have
been accommodated in our first military hos-
pital (it used to be the Immigration Depot
at Kangaroo Point), and when all is ship-
shape there will be room for 200 or more
patients and a nursing staff of 10. The
orderlies, drawn from the citizen forces,
camp in adjacent tents.
Private “Jerry” Tamblyn, one of the re-
turned party, who collided with shrapnel, is
a veteran of the S’African war, and has a
“derry” on shells. So when one fell into
a trench in which there were 14 useful Aus-
tralians, he rushed up and tried to kick it
into Constantinople. Unfortunately it
kicked back, and he was winged. Tamblyn,
an English boy named Anderson, and Bob
Ralston, a member of a well-known Downs
family, were all wounded early in the opera-
tions.
Dr. Mordaunt Sutton, a son of Lieut.-
Colonel Sutton, is one of the latest to offer
for medical service. He was lately a “resi-
dent” at the General Hospital.
Lieut. Bond, who was with the naval
forces at Rabaul, brought back a memento
any man should be proud of. It is a gold
watch, inscribed: “To Lt. T. Bond, in recog-
nition of his exceptional services, from their
Excellencies Colonel Paton, V.D., D. 5.0.,
and Colonel Pethebridge, D. 5.0.” “Their
Excellencies” —pheugh !
The executives of the Red Cross Society
and of the Patriotic Fund Committee held
a joint meeting on Friday afternoon, to
decide who was to do the appealing to the
public for funds for the sick and wounded,
and who was to control the funds collected.
Lady Goold-Adams presided, and explained
that there was no need for overlapping, as
the Patriotic Fund commenced where the
Red Cross i left off. . She suggested that the
Patriotic Committee should call their fund
the “Special Fund for Disabled Soldiers.”
However, it was decided to get the wishes
of the Red Cross in writing, so that no dif-
ferences may arise. This amiability was
born none too soon, as there was a feeling of
bewilderment in some quarters as to who
was administering the funds and how.
Two extraordinary statements are con-
tained in letters received this week from
lads in hospital in the vicinity of the Dar-
danelles. One was to the effect that
stretcher-bearers and others at a dressing-
station objected to attending to the writer
because they considered it impossible for
him to live, and bandaging was waste of
time. He reckons he would have bled to
death but that a R.C. chaplain insisted on
his being bandaged and given a chance. The
other boy, in his letter, said that he thought
the cable system an elaborate joke at
the men’s expense. They were told to
write out their cables, and three weeks
later they were informed that the messages
hadn’t been sent, and would have to be paid
for before they could go. I’ve heard some-
thing about the delays in attending to the
soldiers’ cablegrams before, but the alleged
delay in attending to their wounds in a
dressing-station is hard to swallow.
Io Qualtrough, one of Brisbane’s prettiest
daughters, is forsaking the way of the bache-
lor girl on August 4. Mr. Harry Benson,
of Sydney, is going to cheer her up.
Another wedding next week is that of the
Pelham Clayton chick to a youth from a
station near Barcaldine.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“D.F.”: The paddler Kangaroo, that ex-
cellent imitation of Noah’s Ark which
carries vehicles and live-stock between
Hobart and Bellerive (Tas.), is the oldest
steamship built south of the line now in
active service, and a close competitor for the
title of being the oldest in the world. She
celebrates a 60th birthday this year, and has
been running the whole time. Captain
Chamberlain built Jjer at Hobart out _ of
Tasmanian hardwood, and the original tim-
bers in her hulls (she has two) are as sound
to-day as when they took the. axe. Since the
old hooker was put together it has of course
been learned that native woods 6re not much
use for building things with, because they
don’t fall to pieces quickly enough after-
wards.
“Le Pecheur” : “Nobby” . (B. 8/7/T5)
makes an error in computation which has
put more sawmillers through the Bankruptcy
Courts of Australia than any other respon-
sible foolishness. If there is 40 per cent, of
waste in conversion from log to sawn timber
you should add, he says, 40 per cent, to the
log cost. Thus : “Total log cost 6s. per 100,
plus 40 per cent, loss in conversion brings
the cost up to Bs. 5d.” If the loss is 40 per
cent, you must add not 40 per cent, to your
log cost, but 66§ per cent. If the log cost
was 6s. per 100 and the waste percentage
40 per cent., the cost before milling is 10s.
per .100, not Bs. 5d., and the Sydney landed
cost is 195., not 17s. sd. I agree with
“Nobby” that all this talk about sawmillers
making huge profits is rot. Timber mer-
chants make most of their profits out of saw-
millers who don’t know how to cost, and
from imported timbers. Anyhow, what with
an inefficient tariff, exorbitant wages,, ab-
surd working conditions, and a general
opinion throughout Australia that the man
who manufactures and employs Australians
is fair game for every huckstering, pilfering
politician to shoot at, the importer is the
only sensible man.
* * * *
“Viking”: “Ananias” (B. 24/6/T5) is
Sapphira’s husband still. Australian pine
did not get its bad name from the Darling
Harbor episode. The first little lot of the
timber used to make butter-boxes was on the
young side, and had a slight odor. This was
accentuated by the smell of Stinking Fish
which pervaded the air at the t-ime. Suit-
able pine (hoop) is absolutely without taint-
ing properties, as witness the well-estab-
lished and growing butter-box industry on
the Richmond River.
“Mainshaft” : The two pockets of gas met
by the S.A. Oilwell Co.’s drill at Robe and
the two spurts of oil in the Ocean Oil Co.’s
well at Kingston are probably the fore-
runners of much gas or oil, or both. A gas
well may be as payable as an oil well, but it
is more difficult to control. A gas well that
made the record for running wild was at
Caddo, Louisiana. Work started on it in
March, 1908. On May 11, 1908, it blew out
from the 1800 ft. gas stratum, and the next
day the hole was blowing off 40 million
cubic feet in 24 hours. Mud was forced
down, and the gas then blew out under the
derrick floor. In three weeks the escaping
gas and water formed a crater 100 ft. in
diameter, and as the escaping gas became a
continuous danger, the well was set on fire
and allowed to run wild from June, 1908,
to July, 1913. The crater became 225 ft. in
diameter. To decisively kill the devil, a
relief well was drilled close by. When
it had been drilled to 832 ft., water was
pumped into the gas strata, and in seven
days the devil was tamed and the eruption
ceased. The gas lost was 27 billion cubic
feet. And the cost of the relief well was
only £l2OO.

“D.F.”: Everyone knows that spuds run
to an exalted size on the north-west coast
of Tassy. Urging the claims of the Wilmot
district to a railway the other day, Blytli,
M.H.A., stated that the murphies up there
average 20 to the cwt. Blyth thereupon
brandished a specimen which he explained
was a mere cull, .left over after the real
representatives had been sent away to
market. It was a paltry 51b. 3oz.

“Hamer” : Amongst the callings that the
war has created is the peddling of photo-
graphs of war celebrities. A man called the
other day with large-sized pictures of Kit-
chener, French, Jellicoe and some others.
These, he explained, would look very nice
properly framed. For an additional bob or
two he could supply autographed copies.
With me he- was not successful —at the pre-
sent price of butter how could he be? —but
through a side window I watched him being-
successful next door, sold an auto-
graphed copy. The signature was genuine
all right —he produced a letter signed by
Kitchener" certifying to i-fi.
THE CURRENCY OF LIFE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Joy is the Currency of Life,
I always say ;
Would that my creditors would come
To think my way!
I would with gladness pay their bills
If I but could ;
But all my creditors declare
That that’s no good.
They say that gladness pays no debts,
And seek for cash.
Mounting their hopes, they soar. To me
It seems most rash.
I tell them, truly, that I hate
The thought of debt;
That is the reason why my bills
I would forget.
Yet will they worry o’er my debts —
So do they choose;
Joy is the Currency of Life,
Which they refuse.
I have a ling’ring hope there is
A fair hereafter ;
Then will I pay my bills in Heav’n
With minted laughter.
N.S.W.
HAMER.

Have You Had Influenza?
There are certain disorders such as influ-
enza that especially debilitate and make the
body an easy prey for more dangerous dis-
eases. Ask those who have had the influenza
regarding the present condition of their
health, and most of them will answer : “Since
I had influenza I have never been well.”
They still have profuse perspiration, the per-
sistent weakness of the limbs, the disorgan-
ised digestion, shortness of breath and palpi-
tation of the heart caused by the thin-
blooded condition in which influenza almost
always leaves its victim after the fever and
influenza have subsided. They are, further-
more, at the mercy of relapses and complica-
tions, often very serious. In an attack of
influenza there is a rapid thinning of the
blood, and not until the blood is built up
again is complete health restored.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills quickly make the
blood rich and red, drive out the lingering
germs from the system, and transform des-
pondent influenza victims into cheerful,
healthy men and women.
Try the pills for any form of debility
caused by thin blood.
All chemists sell Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
Get a box to-day and begin at once to regain
your health.
Write to-day to the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine Go., Sydney, for the free book, “Build-
ing Up the Blood.” If you have stomach
trouble, ask for a diet book also.*

The Inky Way.
Forty-three years ago W. O. Mirfin sat
down in Reef ton (M.L.) as editor of the
Inangahua Herald, and stayed on the 30b
till the other day, when a man came round
and took him away. Half a century ago
Mirfin was getting his nib in on the Otago
Daily Times with Vogel, Fox, For jeon and
Judge Robinson. Among other things he
did the courts and oatmeal quotations, and
of all the old-time crew stayed longest on
deck.
* * * *
“Neroly”: A handy practice of the re-
porter has now extended to the war cables,
wherein the remarks of the guns • have be-
come commonplace through repetition. Still
it must have been galling to Wilhelm the
first time he read in his Melbourne morning
paper that, after machine-gun fire, “20,000
German guns also spoke.” Nothing so
pricks the vanity of the victim as to be an
“also spoke.” #
“R”: “J.II.R.’s” par. (B. 8/7/T5) is
difficult of reply, seeing that the writer is
dead. But in the interests of facts some-
thing must be said. The writing of a column
of “strike” stuff doesn’t necessarily make
Mr. Bean anything but a reporter, and that’s
all I said he was. By “visualising” a thing
“faking” is surely not mentioned or thought
of —the more the thing is “visualised” the
more true and intimate must have been the
knowledge of it by the writer. As to Bean’s
stuff being censored into dullness, that is
impossible. * » *
“Bullant” Mclntyre, of Southern Cross
Times (W.A.), writes in his paper
ambiguously:—
There is a man roaming about this field _ named
, who was born in Mexico and reared in Ger-
many. Some time ago this fellow was interned until
he produced proof of German birth. At bullfinch
recently said “I vos Prittish until dey vos yale
me; now I vait my time, und den I shoot der Prit-
tish like dogs.” Our informant is absolutely
reliable, but went to one of the outback parts of
Yilgarn without informing the police of the greaser’s
disloyal utterances. We have known for years.
He is a professional claim-jumper, and a loafer who
imposes upon those who back him. We informed the
police of this German greaser, and learned that they
could not take action ; and so we hereby warn the
German swine that if we meet him he will be in-
terned in the nearest eosteen, and we shall risk the
consequences. So beware. Mr. Pro-German, for this
country is not large enough to hold a brute like you.
We are not going to allow greasy hogs like you to
breathe a syllable against our nation while our
bravest and best are fighting liberty’s great battle.
More than one man has decided to puncture you, and
are now wearing guns for that purpose; so sneak in
and get interned as soon as you read this if you
want to save your own hide; and do it quickly, for
you are not worthy of even this warning.
The trouble with “Bullant” is that he will
write in double entendres.
* * * *
Tim Gooch, one of the few caricaturists
of any quality that Queensland has
developed, has gone under in the Darda-
nelles fury. Before joining the Seventh
Light Horse he had served with honor in
the S’African trouble.
* * * *
Two more Melbourne inky-wayfarers are
exchanging their pens for the more fashion-
able weapon. W. Hervey Phipps, the
Herald’s military reporter, has secured an
appointment on the headquarters staff of the
Tropical Force, and leaves at once for
Rabaul; and J. A. Raws, who flays Labor
politicians for the Argus when he isn’t
playing tennis (he has captained Victoria’s
inter-State team), has gone into camp at
Seymour.
* * * *
The unbounded faith of some people in ad-
vertising ! A Maoriland society in announc-
ing a lecture on “Profitable Poultry” extends
a hearty invitation to every egg-producer.
* * * *
In fiction the literary worker always has
a “den” or a “study” in which he works in
holy calm. In real life he toils as best he
can while a genial caller hangs over him
and talks as if all the clocks in the world
had stopped and groceries were distributed
free. The other day a Sydney pressman
kept a scribbling-pad record of his visitors.
It worked out this way;—
Time. Name. Stay. Topics.
10.10. Smith-Jones. 15miu.Rifle-shooting, servants,
wives.
10.25. Picsnap. 15 ~ Social failings of a mutual
friend.
10.45. Dodgewick. 10 ~ MS. of a person in gaol.
11.55. Ditehwater. 10 ~ Liter-ifore and awful
weather.
12.5, Ditehwater
(2nd session) 10 ~ More literature and more
weather.
12.15. Smith-Jones. 5 „ Suburban roads and
weather.
1.20. Boodle’s Ad-:
vance Agent. 10 „ Boodle’s forthcoming
season at the Tripe Theatre.
2.45. Picsnap. 10 ~ War pictures and artillery.
3. Foodie. 5 „ Mislaid key.
3.45. Poodle. 15 ~ Bouquets, Melba Gift Book,
weather and himself.
After Poodle had gone the pressman
smashed the spare chair, set and laid two
man : traps. left a few poisoned beers in the
passage and took his work home.
“NOTHING DOING.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Across the dim and quaking sky
The heralds of destruction fly,
And as the distant thunder wakes
A hellish storm of shrapnel breaks.
The blood of riven comrades drenches
The soldiers kneeling in the trenches.
Now crumpled on the crimson sod,
Stark eyeballs glaring up to God,
Like slaughtered vermin in a field
The heroes lie who would not yield,
And as a boundless ocean sighing
Sobs out the murmur of the dying.
Comrades in arms, the dead embrace.
The victors, backing from the chase,
Step gingerly, convulsed to feel
What was a Christian ’neath the heel.
With these red bodies broke and battered,
Behold a thousand homes lie shattered 1
The papers say: “The foe's attack
At La Chapelle was driven back.”
The portly reader thrusts aside
His journal, deeply mortified.
“What use,” growls he, “the theme pursuing?
No news at all —there’s nothing doing!”
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

There is a beautiful old Dutch legend that
is almost a part of .the religion of Holland.
Ii says: “Set before your guests something
prepared with your own hands.”
Rut when unexpected visitors arrive at meal hour, friends “drop-
ping in” to lunch—you cannot always do this. With
“REX” Pork and Beans
you can meet the occasion “on the instant” without the least
embarrassment or fuss. They are rich and extremely satisfying.
Every Bean in “REX” PORK AND BEANS is nutlike, mealy
and whole with the flavor of the rich Tomato Sauce right through
them.
The zest is delicious—“REX” PORK AND BEANS are a super-
lative dish, wholesome, and always ready for serving.

The Chant Royal.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I' AM the marvel which came. Mine
was the wonder-birth.
Sired by sweat and by flame from the
innermost womb of the earth,
Fettered by cunning of hand, fashioned by
wisdom of brain,
I have furrowed tlie length of your land and
winnowed your harvest of grain ;
And the sign of my-coming, my march, and
the voice of my singing, my. cry,
Was the raising of girder and arch, clang-
ing ’twixt earth and the sky.
Of my bounty I strengthened your keels.
Oh, the rivets which burred to the
stroke,
And the murmur of glimmering wheels as 1
levelled m,v neck to the yoke !
For I gave of my wealth to your need, that
the glory of Man might increase,
For the Glory of Man was my creed ; with
Wisdom and Beauty and Peace.
From the uttermost lands and the sea I
have carried your riches of freight,
And knelt as a slave at your knee —till ye
taught me to hate!
For the anger which hammered the blade,
and the edge growing keen to the stone;
For the tube which in anger was laid the
soul of Mankind must atone!
And the homes of Mankind shall be dearth,
and waste as the desolate years ;
For my wrath is outspewed o’er the earth,
and her portion is travail and tears.
The sign of my coming, my might, the voice
of my singing, my lilt,
Are rumbling wheels through the night, and
the clashing of sabre and hilt.
Stark terror is haunting your coasts —Oh,
the women who wail in their dread !
Comes the tramping of ravening hosts, and
the prayers will go down with the dead !
Yea! vex not the gods with your prayers,
for the mumbling gods are blind !
I have broken your altar stairs and scat-
tered your shrines to the wind !
Oh, the teeth which are gnashed in the dust!
Oh. the fingers which clutch and which
tear!
All the red hate which guided the thrust is
the hate that I bear!
I have done marvellous things. Mine was
the wonder-birth —
I who have humbled your kings to hide in
the lap of the earth !
Little people in burrow and trench, with
bellies close-set to the mud ;
’Mid the shattering roar and the stench, I
have taken my toll of your blood !
The sign of my coming, my cry, is the whine
of my flight through the dark.
And ye quail at my hurrying by, or blench
as I crash to the mark.
And with voice of the sabre I speak :—the
breath of the gun is my breath ;
Where the thundering tubes ’mid the reek
out-point like the fingers of Death.
Dio - deep from the light at my sound ! Pro-
claim neither shadow nor trace ;
Thus I who was sired from the ground,
have trampled you down in my place.
And as ye have taught me to hate, so hope
for no mercy from Zion.
I am the master of Fate ! Bow down ! I
am Iron! !
MAORILAND.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
Our Dear Converts.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I MADE one of a hungry horde of Chris-
tians at a “tea” given by our Chinese
converts. We were being entertained by
our yellow brothers “in token of affection
and regard.” so the entrance ticket said.
Whatever the feeling that prompted the
givers, there was no doubt as to the satis-
faction of the guests at the eating at some-
one else's expense.
Steadily we undermined pyramids of
sweets and demolished bowls of strawberries-,
and the gratitude of our host was because we
taught him. every Sunday, the ABC of our
language, thus enabling him to compete with
and beat the vegetable-grower and wood-
worker of our own race.
But we of the wowser type don’t let this
outcome worry the peace of mind secured to
us in having “done right.” As for our own
out-o’-works —will not the ravens come, bear-
ing- manna ? And so we eat, and bask in the
smiles of our slant eyed hosts.
At the table-ends ladies, interested in “the
work,” pour tea, and, sweetly imperious,
patronise and hector Willie Li or Te Hung,
who —petted pupil in the Mission class—is
now faithful-dog Tray, and docile in the role
of butler. Li and Te Hung, smiling through
the steam from big kettles, are suggestive of
heathen gods seen through a cloud of incense.
They carry away dirty plates and bring
clean, distribute salad and open lemonade-
agile, deft, born kitchen-men.
When nothing but a vast emptiness re-
mains with the tables and a vast fullness
with ourselves, we filter away to another
hall while the aliens wash up.
Here we find ourselves amid decorations
of Japanese wind-bells a-cliink and a-wave ;
rustling Chinese lanterns arranged round
and about a Chinese dragon, who had pre-
served the entente cordiale by draping the
Union Jack round his hindquarters. Evi-
dently he was a “converted” dragon, or else
the head-decorator, ’spite of our teaching,
was not a converted Chinaman. I mused as
to which was the correct solution as we
broke slowly into song at the instigation of
an evangelist, who aimed at being the “lov-
able bully” type.
“Now,” ordered this person, turning upon
us, “be Sunny Jims ! Sing with vim ! Vim !
But soul into it! Put body into it! Put
elbow-grease into it!”
Useless exhortation. As well expect songs
of praise from a school of sluggish croco-
diles. We have eaten unwisely and too well.
He scorches us with a blazing eye. “Stop!
Stop!” Evidently we are not vinnny
enough.
“You may call that singing,” ho says. “/
call it howling—and very poor howling, too !”
We shuffle our feet, and, _ grinning pain-
fully, try to look as though we enjoy his
comments.
“Sing!” he thunders. Some of us make a
great effort. Personally, I find it impossible,
for I am already in the throes of indigestion,
unable even to grunt “praises.” But he is
not satisfied, and starts nagging again,
pointing an accusing finger at specially
sluggish parts of his audience. Those seated
within the radius of the suspected districts
try to look casual and unconcerned, none
more so than myself. In vain ; I am at the
end of a row, as well as being conspicuous
by reason of great girth. He picks me out
and covers me with the hovering finger.
Taking the audience into his confidence he
says:—•
“There’s a man there, ninth row, end
chair, who has not yet opened bis mouth!"
An awful moment of shocked silence,
while every head in front is turned to stare
at me—the flouter of Sankey !. The eyes of
those behind try to bore holes in me so that
they’ll know me again next time we meet
for food. I mean, for “praise.”
Having thus knocked me down- —in a
spiritual sense —lie starts jumping on me.
“Your favorite hymn?” he demands. “Give
me your favorite hymn !”
He waits breathlessly for it, his mouth,
< >-shaped, ready to bellow the opening line,
his fingers fluttering the pages of “The Song-
ster.”
Hymn? Hymn? Favorite hymn? I am
afraid of his displeasure, and would do much
to avert it; but, alas, I am unable to recall
my favorite—or anyone else’s favorite —
or any hymn. I am not to blame. “Tipper-
ary” has wiped- all lesser compositions from
my mind. ■ ,
Having thus trapped me like a rat and
shaken me he leaves me for dead and calls
for others’ “favorites.” He has no further
reason of complaint. Hymns flow in bounti-
fully. “Onlv time for one verse of each,
friends,” lie hustles. “Sing up and win the
game! Sing up!”
The singing breaks up in disorder at the
entrance of a squad of pupil converts,
headed by the chairman.
Inscrutable, the heathen, having ceased to
be smiling butlers, take the seats set apart
for them as serious performers. I tried to
read the thoughts of the slant-eyed band;
some smooth, banana-skinned; some like
unto the shrivelled lemon, but giving no
clue to the soul of the man from China.
The audience, in possession of propri-
etorial rights, clucked in anticipation of joys
to come, in hearing Hee Mee, Li Yick and
Li Moon sing trios. For, though we teach
our dear brothers the English language, we
reserve the right to make merry at their
efforts to sing it, on such occasions as now,
when they are paying their tuition bill in
cake and tea. And what cares Li Moon for
our mirth? He is a grateful Christian, and
a wise one at that. While we laugh, lie
laughs also —at us.
The chairman—a florid, fat, long-haired
man, used to good living—mounts tile plat-
form. Frock-coated, with a red tie, he strikes
a cheerful note. Neat boots peep from
lavender trouserings, and he sports a red
rose over his heart. Altogether a re-assur-
ing figure against the background of yellow
peril.
Hush! The chairman waits to address
us. One plump hand is raised; he combs
his beard with the other. Ilis complacent
voice pierces my inert brain.
“As you’re aware, friends, the object of
our Mission is to take these poor, friendless
people”—l look for corroboration at the
Chinese faces: they remain null and void—-
“by the hand and ‘teach them our lan-
guage.” (Loud applause.)
“They come to us, these children—chil-
dren, friends, in simplicity, in gratitood, in,
in—er” — (more applause)—“they come to us
earnest and grateful, glad to learn, and are
enabled to earn a living for their dear ones
on the same footing as the misguided Aus-
tralian who. in his —er —in his un-Christian
blindness, seeks to keep him out of this—our
Commonwealth.” (Stampings and clappings
and fond looks showered on the heathen.)
Yes, we teach them. We help them, and
they m their turn are full of grateful love
for us”
The evangelist breaks in here with one
verse of “Love, kindly love”—evidently sung
with vim, for he beams.
The chairman looks annoyed. The inter-
ruption has thrown him out of his stride.
“Love, love filleth our soul,” we sing. Again
I glance at our hosts, hoping to find an
answering gleam of this warm sentiment in
their narrow eyes. I don’t get it. Probably
not sufficiently learned in English to under-
stand the chairman’s drift altogether, they
have missed the cue, and remain unmoved
and inscrutable*
7 grateful love for us,” continues the
chairman. “They are simple, kindly souls—
their hearts filled with brotherly feelings.
—er—cleared of all —er —superstition !’’
(Stupendous applause.)
One expects the Chinese squad to be lifted
shoulder-high by their admirers, or to go off
like a cracker. But, no. With an air of
biding their time they sit immovable.
’Now,” says the speaker, “I will intro-
duce you to Looy Hau. 11 will give an
example of what may be done by patient
Christian teaching. Come along, Looy.”
Looy comes, approaching the piano like a
Stoic going to the stake.
“Looy is a dear brother. We all love him
very much,” adds the chairman encourag-
ingly. ■ And lie loves us.”
Does he?_ I interrogated him with my
eye. He neither affirms nor denies—blushes
not, nor pales. His black eyes glint—but
his expression is nil.
“Looy,” again adds the talkative chair-
man, pressing a hard but affectionate knuckle
into his protege’s ribs, “is modest. He does
not care to lift his voice in praise outside
the confines of his own laundry. A good
laundry man, as well as a good pupil, is
Looy—do up your collar, ally li”-
A tornado of mirth shakes us at this ex-
ample of the chairman’s knowledge of pure
Chinese —we had not known that he was a
linguist. Shoulders, pressed together for
lack of room, shake and heave as one with
joy in the lon mot. A few isolated pupils,
all young, seated in the audience, hemmed in
and lionised by young girl teachers, are
prodded and jabbed in the ribs in excess of
tutorial and sisterly love, as to say, “Where
we love we laugh.” One such, before me
turns upon his fair girl guide a queer look
Can it be gratitude? If so, let gratitude be
knocked on the head.

ħ
Looy’s item, though long delayed, is sin-
gularly appropriate. Regardless of the
accompaniment he strikes up in thread-like
voice
Wass me-e-e whi-ite ass s-snow,
Wass lne-e-e ass pure ass s-snow.
Now he is too fast, with the pianist in hot
pursuit. Now a laggard, being pulled up the
steeps of s-snow by the piano's insistence.
The audience, sympathetic souls, titter and
sway at Looy’s quaintness, yet he sings on
with holy calm.
At the finish our chairman is witty again.
“Looy,” he says, "is not an operatic singer.”
(Shouts of mirth.) “Not a singer; no —but
we teach him —so —and he sings for us his
little hymn of praise.”
Looy, released, regains his clan and does
not give an encore.
“Now we have Ming Ming—a great favorite
with the lady teachers. He is known among
us as one greatly imbued with the Spirit,
lie has been an assiduous scholar at our
feet, and can now converse fluently in the
transaction of his business, that of a market
gardener.”
Ming is a Caruso among Chinamen, and
knows it. He lifts up his voice and tosses
it higher than the highest C, chucks it to the
bass, recaptures it from a flight among the
mirth-ridden audience, disembowels it from
the piano’s interior, and Anally leaves the
ragged remains quivering in the atmosphere.
There is trouble in getting Ming off the
stage. Learning has taught him the value
of an encore. He knows his own popular-
ity ; and I can see that conceit is not an
unknown characteristic of a Chinese con-
vert. But he at last gives place to other
performers. Duets, quartettes and choruses
stumble through their parts in mutilated
English.
Heavy man that I am, and dull, yet am I
a mystic. Insensibly the scene before me
merged itself into another —a picture I once
saw on the tea-caddy. It was a Chinese
town. A-down its street young maidens
trotted, native jugglers chased and caught
butterflies for the sheer fun of the thing
along its garden walks. An ancient Chinese
—a noble, I felt him to be—rode by in a
palanquin. In a lovely pagoda were learned
men studying the sciences; and along the
water’s edge celestial boatmen, wise yet
humble, plied the oar, while Chinese chil-
dren clumped their wooden shoes up and
down the steps. Suddenly across the godlike
scene there came a blur. A mist afar off
near the market place, where peaceful trad-
ing went on. Nearer and nearer it came,
rolling, resolving itself at length into a cloud
of dust and legs, out of which burst a podgy,
tub-like figure, puffins:, sobbing, sweat and
tears streaming down its mottled face, its
stumpy fingers outspread, whiskers flowing.
Our chairman! Whence came he, and why?
His intrusion in such plight was an insult to
fair Pekin. Onward he sped, growing in
size as he neared me ; grunting in agony as
his fat encumbered him.
Cause enough for terror. Behind rushed a
murderer in wooden boots and slacks of blue,
llis right arm Avas raised. He clutched a
curved scimitar —the one I saAV in the
Museum yesterday. His left hand held his
pigtail in leash, ready for throwing, lasso-
like, round the fat throat of the flying Chris-
tian. Spellbound, I looked at the assassin.
Horror! It is our Looy Hau! Looy the
gentle. Looy the lo\ r ed. Looy a renegade;
now an assassin, a butcher, a heathen in his
true colors. No more would he meekly sing
Sankey out of tune. No more Avould he be
satisfied Avith metaphorical blood. The blood
he wanted hoav Avas real, neAv, fresh —the
blood of our chairman.
On he came. He Avas gaining on his vic-
tim. His meek face Avas hideously changed.
The clack of his shoes split the other’s pant-
ing silence like a revolver shot.
On, on comes the chairman. On also
comes Looy. Nearer, nearer. Oh, oh, but-
cher ! He swings his scimitar —ugh !—Avith
an almighty sweep—and cleaves the chairman
in twain.
With a start I awake from comtemplat-
ing the two halves of the plump victim as
they litter the pavement in old Pekin....
I find the evening is over. The audience,
tramping out. is Avalking over my corns.
The chairman, in his very best manner,
shakes hands Avith us at the door. And, in
the obscurity of the porch, a pretty girl is
giving the iemon-faced Ming Ming an im-
promptu lesson in the language.
WEEROONA.
A Tragedy of Suburbia.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A BUTTER LESS breakfast so obviously
called for preventive measures that
Chumper spoke about the matter to
Mrs. Chumper.
“My love,” be explained, “this is just one
of those occasions when a man should assert
himself. I know you do everything calcu-
lated to annoy—l mean, to add joy to this
life, but we are in the throes of a crisis.
Famine is staring us in the face. The chil-
dren may die. I will relieve you of respon-
sibility about butter. You, Evangeline, can
concentrate on cheap fish.”
That morning Chumper dropped into a
couple of city shops. No butter. Three
shops in the lunch hour. A trio of blanks.
Rang up the Premier’s office. None left
there; all used at. lied Cross committee meet-
ings. More shops on the way to the ferry.
Each had sold out a. week ago.
Then Chumper had an inspiration. He
remembered reading magazine yarns setting
forth that the manufacture of margarine had
reached a stage of absolute perfection ; that
only an analytical chemist could tell the
difference between margarine and butter:
that it was going to revolutionise the kit-
chen. Chumper captured a pound, and fled
for the steamer.
“My love,” he remarked as he kissed the
gentle Evangeline, “these infernal parasites
who live by the sweat of their brow milking
animals have got us by the throat. Australia
is without butter. But the scoundrels have
not reckoned on the triumphant progress of
science. Chemistry is coming to rescue little
children from the clutch of these tyrannical
eowspankers. We shall have synthetic butter.
Margarine is a nutritious article of diet, and
here is a pound to go on with.”
Evangeline departed from her habit, and
said nothing. Chumper felt she was im-
pressed, not to say knocked endways, and
rejoiced.
The family sat down to the evening meal,
and there was. something in the butter-dish.
"Is that the margarine, dear';”
“No—-it is not the margarine.” Evange-
line seemed to Chumper unnecessarily em-
phatic, even frigid. "It is a quarter ot a
pound of butter which I obtained after great
difficulty from the wife of the bootmaker at
the corner!”
“The wife of the bootmaker, my dear
Evangeline!”
“Yes, the wife of the bootmaker, whose
husband lias sufficient confidence in her not
to interfere with household administration.”
This sounded distinctly ominous to Chum-
per.
“But, my dear girl, I only”
“Don’t; please don’t go further,” she an-
swered, with haughty precision of tone. “If
it is not enough that I should be censured
for failing to squander my meagre house-
allowance upon boxes of butter —buying
butter in wholesale quantities for a man
whose appetite no one could satisfy”
“Meagre allowance, Evangeline! Butter
in wholesale quantities ! Immoderate appe-
tite ! This is really”
“The truth, every word ! Are you not con-
tent with this exhibition of niggardliness, of
man’s selfishness? I am not going to allow
my children to perish by eating this horrible
mixture that you want to feed them on.”
This was clean over the edge to Chum-
per. “My God,” he mutely reflected, “I
only had thruppence for myself last week,
have used butter with the greatest care,
and narrowly escaped being run down by a
motor, rushing from one beastly grocer’s to
another.” The position seemed to call for
definition.
“What are you referring to as horrible
mixture?” he asked, with dignified restraint.
“I am referring to margarine.”
“But I assure you it is most nutritious.
An admirable food in every way. Surely
you would not have us capitulate to these
sordid cockies —this ring of plutocrats who
think because their wretched cows have gone
dry they can terrorise us into going without
butter! I tell you, Evangeline, I refuse to
be trampled on by these men. I’ll fight to
the bitter end.”
That settled it. One of the youngsters
started to snivel: the other followed the blub-
bering example. Evangeline rushed to their
side.
“I thought so,” she moaned. “Threatening
me and the children with violence-—coming
home to talk like a truculent prize-fighter!
I wonder whether you are quite sober!”
Evangeline gathered her brood and hurried
them off to bed.
Morning broke bleak and dour over the
Chumper household. Instead of a joyful
Evangeline and kids with challenges to jiu-
jitsu, the place was full of an abysmal
silence. Chumper found breakfast set in
solemn state for one on the end of the table,
surrounded by a new smell —not the elusive
smell of bacon, but something that drove the
morning appetite back into its entrench-
ments ; a menacing smell that threatened to
quickly assume the dignity of an able-bodied
stink. Chumper ran it to its lair —a pale-
looking object right in the fairway of plates ;
an inert cube which might have been ex-
tracted from the recess of an Egyptian sar-
cophagus. Clinging to the side of the dish
a scrap of paper. Margarine !
“Thank Heaven!” he murmured, “I still
have presence of mind.” The window was
open. A noise of shattered crockery rent the
stricken atmosphere.
“Gripes!” muttered Chumper.
Evangeline and the boys were in the yard.
Chumper assumed a magisterial air.
“Why are you out here?”
“Why? Because we are driven from the
house—the home you brought me to a bride.”
“But who has driven you? Are we always
going to live apart, isolated in this way?”
“William, I know my duty. I will do it.
These children must not be brought up in a
demoralising atmosphere. When you have
gone I will endeavor to remove any remnants
of that stuff you may have left on the table,
and after burning sulphur”
“But there is none left, Evangeline ! I”
“Great Scot, William, you don’t mean to
say you’ve eaten it all!”
Chumper climbed right down. While the
boys stood out by the gate the triumph of
science, which had been set up in rivalry to
Evangeline’s quarter-pound of butter from
the bootmaker’s, was interred in the little
garden.
There was a packet of chocolates sticking
out of the old man’s pocket when he got home
that night, and there was butter at the old
address.
I know all about it. I’m Chumper.
MUJIK.
An Irish Heart.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OCH, the queerest thing is an Irish
heart!
Will you take it away, Roseen, in
your shawl?
For ’tis sore to stay and ’tis ill to part,
And there’s nothing to ease its beating at all.
Is it drink with the boys till the day is light?
(Will you take it away, Roseen, in your
shawl?)
Ocli, sure, ’tis the heart of the world for
fight
Or friendship or talk while it beats at all!
Is it sighs for pain, is jt tears for pride?
(Will you take it away, Roseen, in your
shaiol?)
’Tis a red-blood heart that is ill deiilSlh
And it plays all tunes while it beats at all.
Is it girls that laugh, is it girls to kiss?
(Will you take it away, Roseen, in your
shawl?)
Bright eyes can teach it a beat to miss,
For it loves all girls while it loves at all.
Is it one soft girl with a clear brown eye?
Will you take it away, Roseen, in your
shawl ?
Och, nurse it warm and ’twill surely try
To be loving no other at all at all!
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
